                                           2
Abstract

The increasing adoption of electrified systems has intensified the demand for compact, ef-
ficient, and scalable power-management solutions capable of both voltage synthesis and
battery balancing in large-scale battery-powered applications. These functions, which
form critical drivetrain components of any battery-driven system, have a substantial im-
pact on overall system performance: they influence size through cooling and filtering
requirements, driving range through conversion efficiency, cost through the types and
ratings of semiconductor devices, and the safety and capacity utilisation of battery cells
through fast and efficient energy balancing. Although multilevel power converters and
battery-balancing circuits are well-established, their realisation in integrated forms re-
mains limited due to challenges in topology applicability, circuit design, including man-
agement of large power switches with effective gate driving, generation of floating driver
supplies, signal level-shifting, and switch stacking, and, critically, converter packaging to
minimise parasitic balancing paths and ensure efficient current delivery from battery cells
to the load. This thesis addresses these challenges by developing a series of highly inte-
grated multilevel converters and battery-balancing circuits, implemented using junction-
isolated lateral semiconductor devices and validated through multiple silicon fabrications
and comprehensive experimental characterisation. The proposed converter and balancer
architectures operate directly with series-connected battery cells. High-voltage conver-
sion and high-speed balancing are achieved by architecting the circuits to function at
the battery-cell-voltage level, allowing high-performance switches with fast switching
capability and low conduction losses to be implemented laterally on-chip. Meanwhile,
off-chip high-voltage switches operate at low switching frequencies and are optimised
for minimal conduction losses, enabling efficient scaling to higher voltage levels. Mea-
surements across multiple fabricated chips demonstrate wide operating-voltage support
(2.4 –4.3 V per cell) and multi-MHz switching. The battery-balancing ICs significantly
reduce the required balancing capacitance and achieve balancing currents of up to 2.5 A.
Multilevel waveforms synthesised from cell-voltage-level steps reach typical amplitudes
of 50 V, delivering 6 A of constant load current, with overall system efficiency of at
least 98.5% in stacked binary-tree configurations. Collectively, this work presents the
first miniaturised multilevel conversion system operating directly at the cell-voltage level
and demonstrates fully integrated, sensor-less battery-balancing architectures scalable to
large battery packs. The results confirm the feasibility and advantages of on-chip realisa-
tion of multilevel power-conversion and balancing circuits for emerging battery-powered
systems, providing a pathway toward higher power density, reduced system size, and
improved efficiency in future battery-driven applications.




                                             4
Impact Statement

The research presented in this thesis has the potential to deliver significant impact both
within and beyond academia. Within the academic community, the work advances
the field of integrated power electronics by demonstrating the feasibility of fully inte-
grated binary-tree multilevel converters and autonomous battery-balancing circuits. The
methodologies developed for level shifting, gate driving, high-current switching, and
scalable stacking provide novel circuit-level solutions that can guide future research
in high-density, high-efficiency power conversion, energy storage systems, and on-chip
power management. The techniques and design frameworks introduced can also be in-
corporated into curricula and training for electrical engineering and microelectronics stu-
dents, enhancing the teaching of integrated power systems and applied circuit design.

   Beyond academia, the research has clear implications for commercial and societal
benefit. Electrified transport, renewable-energy systems, and portable energy-storage
solutions increasingly require high-efficiency, compact, and reliable power conversion
and battery management technologies. Importantly, the designs presented in this work are
realised using mature, low-cost silicon-based processes rather than expensive and scarce
wide-bandgap technologies, demonstrating that complex and high-performance systems
can be achieved using widely available manufacturing platforms. This enables higher
power density, reduced system size, improved energy efficiency, and lower production
costs for battery-powered systems. Improved battery-balancing strategies also contribute
to longer battery lifespan and reduced environmental impact by lowering material waste.
The modular, scalable design approaches facilitate adoption in diverse applications, from
large-scale energy grids to portable devices, potentially impacting public energy access,
environmental sustainability, and climate-change mitigation.

   The dissemination of these results, through peer-reviewed publications, conference
presentations, and industry collaborations, can accelerate the translation of this research
into practical applications. By providing both novel design insights and validated, manu-
facturable solutions using cost-effective technologies, the thesis establishes a foundation
for innovations that improve power efficiency, compactness, reliability, and sustainability
in next-generation battery-powered systems.

Chapter 1


Introduction


1.1     Motivation

Power converters play a central role in the transportation industry’s transition toward net-
zero emissions. They serve as the key technology for converting and controlling electrical
power, managing the flow from batteries to traction systems, from chargers to batteries,
and within vehicles via DC–DC converters. Recent advancements in this field, along-
side progress in battery technologies, have proven critical in expanding the accessibility
of electric vehicles across different market segments and improving their affordability,
especially in the case of Battery Electric Vehicles (BEVs) [1]. To support the transi-
tion toward a net-zero transport ecosystem, the UK’s national semiconductor strategy
prioritises the growth of the domestic sector. Consequently, research and development
efforts must address several key considerations: enabling the adoption of alternative,
cost-effective materials, mitigating risks associated with critical mineral supply chains,
and maximising the performance from existing materials through improved efficiency [2].
These considerations are critical as the global push toward zero-emission transportation
is expected to drive significant growth in demand for power electronic technologies in
the coming years. Meeting this rising demand will require rapid scaling of supply chains
worldwide, placing particular pressure on critical materials such as Silicon Carbide (SiC)
and Gallium Nitride (GaN) [1].

   In this regard, integration in power electronics reflects a key trend aimed at reduc-
ing implementation costs and complexity by combining functions at multiple levels,
such as sensor-on-chip solutions, integrated gate drivers, and integrated switches and
diodes. This approach brings several advantages, including smaller and lighter convert-
ers, reduced component count, lower-cost subcomponents (e.g., modules), and potential
economies of scale from manufacturing fewer system variants [1, 2, 3]. The significance
of integration is substantial. Globally, the market is moving toward greater levels of
integration, with a strong focus on smaller devices, modules, and power converters. Inte-
gration is consistently emphasised as a key priority for enhancing efficiency, increasing
volumetric and gravimetric power densities, and reducing assembly and implementation
costs [1, 2, 3]. In BEV applications, where space limitations and weight reduction are
critical design considerations, a compact and lightweight drivetrain not only improves
energy utilisation but also directly contributes to extending the driving range. In addi-
tion, highly efficient drive architectures ensure that the majority of the electrical energy
drawn from the battery is converted into mechanical propulsion rather than dissipated
as heat. As a result, such configurations reduce overall energy demand, extend battery
lifespan, and enhance the vehicle’s effective driving autonomy [4].

   Another sector undergoing significant efforts toward electrification is the aerospace
industry. Currently, aviation accounts for ∼2% of global CO2 emissions and ∼3% of
total greenhouse-gas emissions, with long-term growth projected at 4–5% annually [5].
Absent effective mitigation, the International Civil Aviation Organization estimates that
sector emissions could roughly triple by 2050, potentially comprising up to 25% of global
carbon emissions [5]. The UK government’s newly established Jet Zero taskforce is a key
initiative in this effort. It is focused on accelerating the development and deployment of
sustainable aviation fuels, enabling zero-emission flights, and enhancing the efficiency of
aviation systems. The taskforce has set an ambitious goal: to achieve net-zero emissions
in UK aviation by 2050, with 22-percent of all jet fuel coming from sustainable sources
by 2040 [6]. This aligns with the UK’s broader climate targets, including the recently
established aim, agreed at COP29, to cut national emissions by 81-percent by 2035 [7].
Electric propulsion, particularly through hybrid and More Electric Aircraft (MEA), and
ultimately All-Electric Aircraft (AEA), presents a promising pathway to achieving these
climate goals. Electrification, powering both propulsion and on-board systems electri-
cally, offers significant improvements in aircraft efficiency and a substantial reduction in
environmental impact [8]. However, the transition to fully electric aviation remains tech-
nically challenging. The MEA concept replaces traditional pneumatic, hydraulic, and
mechanical systems in secondary aircraft functions with electrical alternatives, leading
to lower emissions, reduced fuel consumption, and easier maintenance [8]. Commercial

                                            37
aircraft such as the Boeing 787 and Airbus A380 exemplify the successful application of
MEA technologies [9], benefiting from modern power electronics that enhance reliability
and operational efficiency by reducing component complexity [10].

   In line with such ongoing efforts toward electrification, this thesis aims to advance
the field by proposing highly integrated power electronic converters and battery balanc-
ing circuits. The methodology is centred on a low-voltage design approach, which en-
hances integration to increase power density, minimise leakage and switching losses, re-
duce driving losses and parasitic impedances, and enable high-frequency operation. This
strategy aims to meet the key performance targets of modern electrification by optimising
silicon through innovative designs and topologies, and leveraging silicon as a low-cost,
mature, and widely available technology without sole reliance on expensive and scarce
SiC and GaN materials.



1.2     Trends and Integration Challenges in Drivetrain
        Power Electronics
Power electronic converters are responsible for indispensable functionalities that enable
the control of electric power flow to the traction motor in electric drives and the charging
of the batteries from AC and/or DC sources. Thus, the market opportunity is rapidly
growing for power electronics converters, and they will represent an increasing propor-
tion of the drive system value in each electric vehicle. The stringent demands of the
automotive industry are driving the need for power-dense and efficient solutions, faster-
switching semiconductors, enhanced reliability, and materials capable of withstanding
higher temperatures. These advancements are crucial for various drivetrain components
demonstrated in Fig. 1.1, including inverters that convert battery-supplied DC to AC for
traction motor operation, DC-DC converters that adjust battery power for different elec-
tronic systems, and Onboard Chargers (OBCs) that transform three-phase and/or single-
phase grid AC into DC for battery charging. Semiconductors represent the most costly
component in these systems, whilst silicon remains the dominant material for low-voltage
applications owing to its affordability and ease of manufacturing [11]. However, for
higher voltage applications, SiC is expected to see continued adoption, driven by im-
provements in producing large, high-quality wafers at reduced costs and innovative de-
vice designs [3]. Meanwhile, GaN excels in high-frequency switching applications such

                                            38
Figure 1.1: Architecture of an electric traction drive system with multiple discrete components,
            each performing a single dedicated function




as DC-DC converters. Both SiC and GaN will play a critical role in achieving the power
density targets for 2030 and beyond, though further advancements in cost reduction and
device reliability will be necessary for large-scale adoption. However, to fully harness
the potential of these materials, surrounding components must be optimised for high-
temperature, high-frequency, and high-current conditions. Advancements are necessary
in passive components, Printed Circuit Boards (PCBs), sensor designs, and circuit ar-
chitectures. Additionally, sophisticated control software and fault-tolerant designs are
essential to enhance converter performance and ensure long-term reliability. Standard-
isation and refined system architectures will facilitate scalability and help reduce costs.
Greater integration of these elements is leading to more compact, lightweight, and cost-
effective designs. The automotive industry is increasingly developing converters that in-
tegrate multiple functions. For example, DC-DC converters and OBCs are being merged
into a single unit to save space and reduce costs. Additionally, bi-directional charging and
Vehicle-to-Grid (V2G) capabilities are expected to become standard, allowing vehicles
to function as energy sources [3, 11, 12].

   Table 1.1 summarises the main technology indicators for the primary power convert-
ers used in electric drivetrains of Light-Duty Vehicles (LDVs) and Heavy-Duty Vehicles
(HDVs) for this year and 2030 time-frames based on the last-year technology roadmap of
power electronics, which is produced by the Advanced Propulsion Centre UK on behalf
of the Automotive Council UK.
A clear trend can be observed toward higher power density, improved efficiency, and

Table 1.1: Technology indicators for the major converters used in the electric drivetrain of LDVs

increased voltage levels across all converter types. For instance, inverters in LDVs are
expected to double their power density from approximately 60–80 kW/l in 2025 to about
120 kW/l by 2030, whilst HDV inverters show moderate improvement due to their in-
herently larger power handling requirements. Similarly, advancements in semiconductor
devices and thermal management techniques contribute to higher specific power and ef-
ficiency across both DC–AC and DC–DC converters.

    Despite these improvements, the table highlights persistent challenges related to in-
tegration and scalability, particularly at higher voltage and power levels. HDV con-
verters still exhibit relatively low power densities and efficiencies compared to LDV
units, primarily due to increased current handling and thermal constraints. These lim-
itations emphasise the need for innovative converter architectures and implementation
approaches capable of delivering high power density and efficiency whilst maintaining
cost-effectiveness and manufacturability, which are key objectives addressed in this re-
search. Future designs should incorporate advanced circuit topologies and distributed
architectures, comprising multiple small converters operating in series and/or in parallel,
as well as interleaved systems to enhance performance and meet the ambitious technol-
ogy indicators.

                                               40
   The increasing integration of power electronics presents significant opportunities for
cost reduction and space savings. Future developments are expected to emphasise the
embedding of power electronics within motors and transmissions as Integrated Motor
Drives (IMDs), alongside the design of multifunctional and modular converter architec-
tures that enhance both flexibility and efficiency [1, 3, 13]. To optimise packaging and re-
duce system weight, integrated drive systems are becoming increasingly prevalent. Early
implementations primarily focus on tightly coupling inverters, motors, and transmissions
to achieve maximum efficiency [1, 3, 14].

   Among power converter topologies, the two-level Voltage-Source Inverter (VSI) has
emerged as the dominant choice over Current-Source Inverter (CSI), largely due to its
compatibility with MOS-gated Si switches [15]. These devices allow forward voltage
blocking whilst enabling bidirectional current flow. To further improve power density
and facilitate tighter integration with electrical machines, Wide-Bandgap (WBG) semi-
conductor switches have been extensively investigated [16, 17]. However, the exception-
ally fast switching transients of WBG devices, sometimes exceeding 100 V/ns, pose new
challenges [15, 16]. When such steep Pulse-Width Modulation (PWM) voltage wave-
forms are applied to motor terminals, transmission line effects dominate the electrical re-
sponse. Even with relatively short cable lengths (e.g., 3 m), overvoltages of 2:1 or higher
may arise at machine terminals or across the initial turns of stator windings, potentially
damaging insulation and leading to short-circuit failures [18, 19, 15]. Another critical
concern is the substantial increase in both conducted and radiated Electromagnetic Inter-
ference (EMI) caused by the sharp voltage transitions of WBG devices. These high EMI
levels often exceed regulatory limits, jeopardising the operation of nearby electronic sys-
tems, including the drive’s own controller [15, 20]. One mitigation strategy is to deploy
LC filters at the inverter output, which attenuate high-frequency harmonics in the PWM
waveform. However, this approach adds considerable size, mass, and cost, ultimately
undermining the power density and efficiency advantages of WBG-based inverters. Al-
ternatively, active gate-drive control can be employed to moderate the switching tran-
sitions, thereby suppressing harmful voltage wavefront effects [21]. Nevertheless, this
method compromises one of the fundamental benefits of WBG devices, their ultra-fast
switching capability, resulting in increased switching losses and degraded thermal per-
formance. These limitations continue to hinder widespread adoption of WBG technology
in practical systems [15].

                                            41
   A promising direction for overcoming these challenges, whilst retaining the benefits
of WBG switches, is to reconsider CSIs [22, 23]. With WBG implementation, CSIs can
exploit high PWM switching frequencies (exceeding 100 kHz), enabling reductions in the
size and weight of their DC-link inductors and output capacitors. The resulting inherent
filtering produces near-sinusoidal output voltages with reduced PWM harmonics, lower
common-mode EMI, and minimised bearing currents. Moreover, inductors offer higher
robustness and improved thermal tolerance compared to capacitors. These features have
renewed interest in CSIs. However, their practical deployment remains constrained by
the limited availability of WBG switches with reverse-voltage-blocking capability. Addi-
tionally, CSIs require supplementary conversion stages to transform conventional voltage
sources (e.g., batteries or supercapacitors) into current sources, which introduces further
complexity and design trade-offs [14, 20, 24].

   In addition to these challenges, embedding drivers within motors brings further design
complexities and performance constraints, such as [25, 15]:

    • Subsystem Integration: Adding elements such as charging modules, precision
      sensors, and communication systems increases design complexity and the risk of
      subsystem interference. Efficient use of limited space and harmonised layouts that
      avoid electromagnetic disturbance will be critical for practical designs.
    • Compact and High-Temperature Tolerant Drives: To achieve miniaturisation,
      IMDs must adopt optimised topologies and reduce passive device size. Wide-
      bandgap semiconductors offer promising pathways but introduce challenges re-
      lated to EMI, thermal stress, and reliability, as discussed earlier.
    • Thermal Management: Excessive heating due to tight integration remains one
      of the most pressing issues. Current cooling methods and design theories are in-
      sufficient, with little standardised evaluation of effectiveness. Enhanced inverter
      thermal protection and thermally ruggedised electronics capable of withstanding
      high temperatures and frequent thermal cycling are also needed.
    • Fault Management: Embedding the drive within the motor increases system com-
      plexity, making fault detection and mitigation harder.
    • Structural Optimisation of Motors: The motor body must evolve to better ac-
      commodate embedded drives whilst ensuring effective cooling. Innovations such
      as axial structures, redesigned winding layouts, and improved placement of heat



                                             42
      sinks are needed to balance electromagnetic performance with thermal and EMI
      considerations.
    • Reliability and Ruggedisation: Long-term reliability remains a concern, as the
      drive electronics should ideally outlast mechanical components such as bearings.
      Future IMDs must employ both thermally and mechanically robust designs to en-
      dure high operating temperatures, frequent temperature swings, vibration, and me-
      chanical stresses, whilst simultaneously driving down overall system cost.

   Building on these challenges and opportunities, developing converter topologies be-
yond conventional two-level architectures is essential to address their limitations, as
briefly summarised in Fig. 1.2.
A key limitation concerns device requirements and availability. Due to increased trans-
mission losses, the heavy cabling required to carry high currents, and the growing demand
for faster battery charging, high-voltage DC links are emerging as a preferred alternative
[4]. These systems reduce cable mass and losses by lowering system currents and ampac-
ity requirements [26]. It is estimated that the conducting core weight decreases exponen-
tially with increasing system voltage [27], thus significantly improving the power density
of the system. However, high-voltage operation requires semiconductor switches capable
of blocking the full DC-link voltage. Such devices are less commercially available, more
expensive, and often face longer lead times. In high-frequency motor drives, traditional
devices such as Insulated Gate Bipolar Transistors (IGBTs) are suboptimal, whilst newer
high-voltage options such as SiC MOSFETs remain costly and limited.
In addition to device constraints, switching losses pose another major challenge. Increas-
ing the PWM switching frequency helps mitigate high-frequency harmonic components
arising from variations in the machine core’s flux density [4]. As the switching fre-
quency increases, the current waveform approaches an ideal sinusoidal profile, thereby
reducing iron losses and improving overall drive efficiency. Nevertheless, higher switch-
ing frequencies in inverters also lead to increased switching losses, which scale with the
square of both voltage and frequency. This makes high-voltage operation under high-
frequency PWM inefficient, reducing overall efficiency and increasing thermal stress on
the switches. Furthermore, substantial fluctuations in motor back electromotive force
that exceed voltage constraints, or pronounced voltage instabilities induced by harmonic
distortion, can adversely influence the inverter’s control performance [4].
Beyond switching behaviour, high-voltage DC links such as 800 V induce high dv/dt

                                            43
and can lead to EMI issues and insulation stress [4, 28]. Long cables between the propul-
sion inverter and the AC motor may cause voltage reflection phenomena due to surge
impedance mismatches. These high-frequency voltage spikes, which can reach more
than 2 p.u. of the DC bus voltage, can damage the motor’s stator winding insulation and,
in severe cases, lead to the collapse of the entire propulsion drive system [19, 28]. Bulky
filtering may therefore be required to mitigate over-voltage reflections at the motor ter-
minals [29].
Reliability concerns also extend to passive components. The DC-link electrolytic ca-
pacitors can degrade under high temperature and ripple current, compromising converter
reliability. Additionally, electric machines connected to high-voltage inverters experi-
ence increased harmonic losses, bearing currents, and insulation degradation.
From a topological perspective, conventional two-level inverters offer limited fault toler-
ance and scalability [30]. Phase failures can be catastrophic, and incorporating system-
level redundancy typically adds weight and complexity [19]. Concentrating power in
fewer devices further leads to thermal hotspots, demanding larger and heavier cooling
systems. Moreover, balancer circuits may be required to stabilize the DC neutral point,
reducing power density [27].
Given these cumulative limitations, two-level inverter topologies are expected to become
less viable for future high-performance drive applications.

   In this regard, Multilevel Inverters (MLIs) offer a promising alternative to conven-
tional two-level converters. As illustrated in Fig. 1.2, MLIs enable a more distributed
approach to power processing. This approach involves dividing the DC input voltage
into multiple smaller DC voltage levels, which can be achieved either by stacking strings
of series-connected battery cells or by employing external circuits that partition the total
DC voltage, such as ”DC auto-transformer” that generates a stack of DC voltage levels
based on magnetic coupling [31, 32], capacitive voltage division as in Neutral-Point
Clamped (NPC) converters [33] and Flying Capacitor Converters (FCCs) [34, 35], or
Modular Multilevel Converter (MMC) topologies [36].
Compared to two-level inverters, MLIs offer several significant advantages [27, 26]:


    • Lower Voltage Stress per Switch: By dividing the DC bus voltage across multiple
      switching devices, MLIs enable the use of lower voltage, more readily available



                                            44
Figure 1.2: Implementation challenges of conventional state-of-the-art power converters and bat-
            tery balancers




      semiconductor devices. This expands component choices and improves design
      flexibility.
    • Higher Output Quality: MLIs produce output waveforms with lower Total Har-
      monic Distortion (THD), lower common-mode voltages, and reduced dv/dt, im-
      proving efficiency and extending motor lifespan.
    • Reduced Switching and Motor Losses: The decreased switching voltages reduce
      converter losses and minimise motor-side harmonic losses, resulting in overall en-
      ergy savings.
    • Improved Motor Reliability: Lower common-mode voltages reduce parasitic
      bearing currents, mitigating grease degradation and insulation wear. Three-level

                                              45
      inverters, for instance, have been shown to eliminate partial discharge risks in elec-
      tric aircraft motors.
    • Better EMI Performance: Lower dv/dt improves EMI behaviour, enabling
      smaller and lighter EMI filters, an important factor in drive systems.
    • Built-in Fault Tolerance: MLIs can maintain functionality under fault conditions
      without additional hardware. Adding one redundant cell per phase can increase the
      mean time before failure by over 150%.

   For these advantages provided by MLIs, recent concepts aim to push integration even
further by combining the battery, the Battery Management System (BMS), and the DC–
AC inverter into a unified architecture. This is achieved by assigning dedicated power-
conversion capabilities to each individual battery cell, or to small cell groups, hence
forming Battery-Integrated Multilevel Inverters (BIMIs). In such architectures, the over-
all converter is realised as a cascade of numerous modules, each comprising a battery
unit together with an associated switching stage; collectively, these modules synthesise a
multilevel output voltage. A wide range of BIMI topologies has been proposed in recent
years, including those reported in [37, 38, 39, 40]. Most prominently, the Full-Bridge/H-
bridge (FB), Half-Bridge (HB), and the ”Battery Modular Multilevel Management Sys-
tem (BM3)” submodules have been employed as the building blocks of BIMIs.

   To enable a fair comparison between systems employing different semiconductor
technologies, specifically, a Two-Level (2L) SiC inverter and various BIMIs employ-
ing Si devices, [41] used the metric of embodied energy per semiconductor die area. This
measure is claimed to correlate closely with the embodied carbon footprint and is also
indicative of cost. When imposing equal total embodied carbon for the semiconductor
devices, a BIMI utilises approximately 3.25 times more Si die area than a 2L SiC inverter
with a DC-side battery at 225 kW and 800 V DC. Under this condition, the SiC-based 2L
inverter achieves nearly a fivefold reduction in total losses, including switching losses,
relative to an FB-BIMI operating at nominal current. Moreover, due to their intrinsic
phase-modular arrangement, BIMIs generate pronounced low-frequency pulsations in
the battery power flow, resulting in roughly double the battery losses compared with a 2L
system employing an identical number of battery cells [41].

   Overall, MLIs in general and BIMIs in particular face challenges related to system
complexity, cost, and components count. As the number of synthesisable voltage lev-


                                            46
els increases, so does the need for additional switches, gate drivers, capacitors, control
logic, and isolated communication with each MLI submodule. Furthermore, the driving
complexity of multilevel converters arises due to the existence of numerous switches that
cannot be referenced to ground, hence, gate driver circuits are needed to be floating with
respect to the ground and other supplies [42, 43].

   Due to the inherently non-uniform usage patterns of multiple DC sources (e.g., bat-
tery cells or capacitors) involved in MLI operation, these sources can become unbal-
anced. This imbalance not only raises safety concerns for the battery cells but also limits
the overall energy capacity of the system. For instance, during driving mode, the cell(s)
with the lowest capacity or State of Charge (SOC) constrain the usable capacity of the
entire battery pack. Conversely, during charging, the cell(s) with the highest capacity or
SOC determine the end of the charging process, often leading to a premature termination
of charging [44].

   To mitigate these issues, battery balancing circuits are employed to restore equilib-
rium among the battery cells. This can be achieved using either active or passive bal-
ancing techniques [45, 46], as illustrated in Fig. 1.2. Owing to their higher efficiency
and faster balancing time, active balancing methods are generally the preferred choice.
However, their complex architectures, comprising multiple switches, gate drivers, iso-
lated driver supplies, and other auxiliary components, limit their widespread adoption in
practical systems. As a result, simpler but less efficient passive balancing solutions such
as LTC6803 and MAX11068, which dissipate excess energy as heat, are still commonly
used.

   Autonomous active battery balancers operate without relying on complex control al-
gorithms [45]. They inherently equalise charge among cells based on their terminal
voltages, which in Li-ion batteries correlate with their SOC and remaining capacity.
This approach, however, depends on the existence of low-resistance conduction paths
between cells to enable sufficiently high balancing currents for energy transfer. Con-
sequently, conventional board-level implementations using discrete switches and bulky
components introduce significant parasitic effects, resulting in slower balancing and re-
duced efficiency due to additional losses and leakage currents. Furthermore, parasitic
inductance must be minimised to enable high-frequency switching, which is necessary to
reduce the size of passive components such as capacitors, inductors, or transformers in


                                            47
the balancing network. Long interconnection paths at the board level increase this par-
asitic inductance, thereby limiting the operating speed and performance of the balancer
circuit.

    Overall, to enable next-generation power converters with a high level of integration,
low cost, high power density, and multifunctional features adaptable across different plat-
forms, the challenges associated with increasing voltage levels, subsystem integration,
and power conversion quality must be addressed through innovative designs that lever-
age low-cost technologies and support rapid, unconstrained design cycles.



1.3        Problem Statement
Despite the significant progress achieved in power converter development, particularly
through the adoption of wide-bandgap devices, existing topologies and conventional im-
plementations continue to exhibit several limitations. At the converter level, multilevel
architectures present substantial challenges in terms of assembly complexity, practical
deployment, and cost, especially when scaled to a high number of levels to gain benefits
such as reduced EMI, lower THD, and reduced device voltage ratings. These drawbacks
arise from the large number of semiconductor switches with varying ratings, the cor-
responding gate drivers and their isolated supplies, as well as the additional protection
and auxiliary circuits required. Such challenges are a direct consequence of fully board-
level implementations and the lack of customized switching devices designed for specific
current and voltage ratings. These limitations ultimately restrict the scalability, manufac-
turability, and practical adoption of advanced converter topologies in emerging electrified
transportation and renewable energy systems.

    The availability of high-quality discrete semiconductor switches is particularly lim-
ited in low-voltage applications, as encountered in Multilevel Converters (MLCs) operat-
ing at battery-voltage levels (e.g., Li-ion cells with a voltage range of 2.5 V–4.3 V). These
voltages are insufficient to drive discrete semiconductor switches directly and thus require
dedicated isolated DC–DC converters. Furthermore, MLCs necessitate isolated PWM
signals due to their floating rails and multiple voltage domains. Realising this isolation
typically demands bulky and costly circuitry, such as opto-couplers or transformer- and
capacitor-based digital isolation techniques, which contribute to higher driving losses,
leakage power, parasitic effects, and increased assembly complexity. Consequently, these

                                             48
factors limit power density, efficiency, and scalability in high-performance converter de-
signs.

   At the balancing-circuit level, as discussed in the previous section, autonomous or
sensorless active battery balancers require short balancing paths to maintain high balanc-
ing currents and speed. They share many of the same challenges observed in multilevel
converters, including the need for dedicated power supplies and isolated gate-driver sig-
nals, as well as the absence of suitable discrete switches within the Li-ion cell voltage
range. These factors hinder their adoption in modern BMSs, leading to the continued
reliance on inefficient, low-speed dissipative balancing circuits.

   Given these core challenges in both converter drivers and battery balancers, this re-
search addresses them through a novel approach based on battery-cell-level voltage pro-
cessing. Unlike conventional architectures that rely on centralised voltage domains, the
proposed method decentralises power conversion to the cell level, enabling simpler, scal-
able, and technology-customised operation. This concept allows the use of mature, low-
cost semiconductor technologies for developing customised power converters and balanc-
ing circuits. By processing power directly at the cell level, the converter gains individ-
ual access to each battery cell, allowing multifunctional operation such as bidirectional
power flow for both driving and charging (or regenerative braking) modes. Moreover,
this approach inherently facilitates cell-level energy balancing, enabling direct energy
transfer between cells, ensuring safe operation, and maximising overall battery capac-
ity utilisation. In addition, the proposed approach opens new research directions toward
the integration of intelligent sensing, diagnostic, and protection features within both the
converter and the battery system.



1.4        Research Aim and Objectives
Building on this approach, this research aims to develop highly-integrated battery-cell-
level Binary-Tree Multilevel Converter (BTMLC) and battery balancing circuits. The
proposed circuit architectures are implemented using IC design, fabrication, and test-
ing methodologies as discussed in the next section. To achieve this goal, the following
objectives are set:

    • Synthesise and model converter topologies based on criteria such as feasibility of
         on-chip implementation, control simplicity, and scalability.

                                              49
    • Select and characterise suitable semiconductor technologies considering fabrica-
      tion lead-time, cost, device availability, and switch performance.
    • Develop circuit-level techniques for gate driving, voltage level-shifting, and dead-
      time generation for series-connected battery domains.
    • Design, simulate, layout, and verify:
         – binary-tree multilevel converters, including variants with Zener-based volt-
           age limiter circuits for internal driver rails, and high-current implementations
           using compact driver circuits;
         – battery balancing circuits, including stacked half-bridge and full-bridge
           topologies.
    • Package fabricated ICs using gold and aluminium wire-bonding techniques to en-
      able reliable testing.
    • Assemble and design test boards for packaged ICs.
    • Test and characterise the developed ICs under varying input voltages, switching
      frequencies, and load conditions, focusing on:
         – binary-tree converters (single-chip and stacked configurations with off-chip
           half-bridge stages);
         – battery balancing ICs (half-bridge and full-bridge based) under different im-
           balance conditions.



1.5     Methodology Overview
The strategy to achieve the research objectives relies on a novel voltage synthesis ap-
proach that leverages low-cost switches available in commercial Bipolar-CMOS-DMOS
(BCD) technologies. As conceptualised in Fig. 1.3, the methodology divides the high-
voltage DC link into low-voltage segments (e.g., 5 V) aligned with battery cell voltages.
A custom switching array based on a binary-tree architecture, built from low-voltage-
compatible devices and with a reduced component count, selectively connects these seg-
ments to the output.

   This approach extends the applicability of low-voltage technologies to high-voltage
designs whilst enabling higher-frequency operation without increasing switching or driv-
ing losses. This is achieved through the strategic allocation of switching frequencies and
blocking voltages among the switches. The low-voltage design allows high-performance

                                              50
switches with inherently low conduction losses to operate at high frequencies, whilst
higher-voltage off-chip devices extend the voltage range for applications such as 400 V–
800 V systems. These off-chip switches operate at lower frequencies, allowing optimi-
sation for low conduction losses, and multiple devices can be connected in parallel to
further reduce on-state resistance without incurring additional switching losses.

   Furthermore, the on-chip implementation allows customisation of semiconductor de-
vices in terms of current and voltage handling capabilities, optimising silicon utilisation
and aligning device characteristics with the specific requirements of converter and bal-
ancer designs. This voltage synthesis enables the creation of power conversion building
blocks whose maximum voltage is limited only by the breakdown limits of the under-
lying semiconductor technology. By employing high-voltage breakdown technologies
(e.g., Silicon-on-Insulator (SOI)) or stacking multiple modules in series and/or parallel
at the board level, higher system voltages and/or currents can be achieved whilst main-
taining silicon-level compatibility with mainstream IC platforms.

   In this thesis, two batter-Integrated Power Converters (IPCs) are presented, along
with two battery balancing circuits: a stacked half-bridge capacitor-based balancer and
a full-bridge capacitor-based balancer. The IPC module has been further extended to
include two interconnected modules (denoted as IPC1 and IPC2 in Fig. 1.3) to validate
the stacking capability of the designed IPC and to demonstrate its operation at twice the
voltage breakdown of a single module. A single off-chip half-bridge stage, implemented
using vertical discrete MOSFETs, is employed to combine the outputs of the two stacked
modules and synthesise the full output waveform. This approach is scalable to a higher
number of stages, depending on the target application and input DC voltage level.

   The proposed design methodology for developing an IPC is illustrated in Fig. 1.4.
The process adopts a hybrid bottom-up and top-down design approach.

   The methodology begins with defining the requirements for the driver and balancer
circuits, including the maximum operating frequency, peak driver and balancing cur-
rents, number of battery cells, and maximum converter voltage. Based on these specifi-
cations, the balancing and driver circuits are designed in a suitable semiconductor tech-
nology, ensuring that the selected devices can meet the circuit requirements in terms of
voltage rating, on-state resistance, and current handling capability. Device voltage and
current stresses, along with architectural considerations such as redundancy and mod-


                                            51
Figure 1.3: Overview of abstraction levels, methodology, and principal technical features of the
            proposed converter




                                              52
ularity/scalability, must be carefully accounted for. IC technologies with different lat-
eral/vertical isolation approaches (e.g., SOI, deep-trench isolation, or junction isolation)
may be considered, depending on availability, system needs, fabrication cost, and lead
time.

   Once the technology selection and conceptual circuit architecture are defined, the
standard Application-Specific Integrated Circuit (ASIC) design flow is followed to tran-
sition from design to fabrication and packaging. This includes schematic-driven design
entry using the foundry device models, device and circuit sizing based on power loss and
thermal constraints, and extensive simulation. Following this, layout design is performed,
and parasitic elements (e.g., resistances and capacitances) are extracted to verify their in-
fluence on the initial design, including potential increases in resistance and effects on
gate waveform rise and fall times. This process is iterated until satisfactory performance
is achieved.

   Reliability is then assessed by ensuring that all devices operate within their Safe-
Operating Area (SOA), and by verifying electromigration and self-heating limits in metal
interconnects. Design-for-Manufacturability (DFM) checks are also conducted to im-
prove fabrication yield. Fabrication may proceed either through a dedicated production
run or using a Multi-Project Wafer (MPW) service.

   After fabrication, the device undergoes packaging using a method compatible with
the chosen pad structure, such as wedge or ball wire bonding, stud bumping, flip-chip
bumping, or Chip-Scale Package (CSP). The latter two options are integrated within the
fabrication process. Finally, the fabricated ICs are tested and characterised to evaluate the
Model-to-Hardware Correlation (MHC), resulting in a power IC capable of balancing a
series stack of battery cells and driving a load from the battery pack.


1.6      Thesis Organisation and Contributions
The thesis has been organised in eight main chapters as follows:

    • Chapter 2 presents a comprehensive investigation of voltage synthesis techniques,
        highlighting the trade-offs that motivate the adoption of multilevel converters. It
        then reviews a class of multilevel converters designed to reduce device count as
        well as the classical multilevel converters. The chapter also examines the prin-
        ciples of cell equalisation and explores capacitive battery balancing circuits as a

                                             53
   Figure 1.4: Proposed design flow illustrating the Power IC development process



  high power-density solution as well as magnetic-based and converter-based bat-
  tery balancers for comparison. The chapter concludes by identifying key research
  questions and challenges in the implementation of the existing converter and bat-
  tery balancing circuits.
• Chapter 3 provides background on discrete, integrated, and wide-bandgap semi-
  conductor devices, IC technologies and their front-end and back-end features and
  characterisation, circuit building blocks for the integration of power converters, and
  IC packaging and assembly techniques.
• Chapter 4 presents three integrated multi-output voltage level shifter circuits tai-
  lored for systems with series-connected voltage sources: (1) a continuous active-
  coupled level shifter, (2) a capacitive-coupled level shifter, and (3) a cross-coupled
  active level shifter (included for comparison). The proposed designs are optimised
  for key performance metrics, including high speed, low power consumption, com-
  pact layout area, and resilience to Process, Voltage, and Temperature (PVT) varia-
  tions. Architectural features such as modularity and scalability are also addressed.
  All circuits operate differentially and incorporate dead-time control, enabling com-
  plementary voltage outputs suitable for directly driving gate drivers in multi-cell

                                        54
  powertrain applications, such as battery equalization systems. Designed and fab-
  ricated in a 130-nm BCD technology, the circuits are validated through compre-
  hensive post-layout transient and Monte Carlo simulations. Results demonstrate a
  17× reduction in propagation delay and a 22×–66× decrease in average dead-time
  spread under process and mismatch variations, whilst supporting a wide operating
  voltage range from 5 V down to 1.8 V. Measurement results confirm correct oper-
  ation and validate the proposed circuits’ performance in terms of voltage compati-
  bility and speed.
• Chapter 5 presents an on-chip battery balancing module utilising stacked half-
  bridge converters, driven by a single control signal and operating autonomously
  without the need for voltage or current sensor-based algorithms. The module sup-
  ports balancing across four battery cells and can be easily extended for larger bat-
  tery strings. All control circuitry, including dead-time generation, voltage level
  shifting, and gate driving for the balancing switches, is fully integrated within
  a 3.4 mm2 chip, fabricated in a 130-nm BCD high-voltage process utilising 5 V
  N-channel Lateral Diffused MOSFET (NLDMOS) switches. To validate balanc-
  ing capability, the chip has been connected to four external balancing capacitors
  arranged in a star configuration. Experimental results demonstrate robust perfor-
  mance across a wide operating range, with switching frequencies from 1 kHz to
  5 MHz and input voltages from 2.4 V to 4.2 V. The circuit achieves battery bal-
  ancing with 75% efficiency under the worst-case condition of maximum voltage
  disparity, delivering an average balancing current of 1.4 A, primarily limited by
  off-chip routing resistances and test board assembly constraints.
• Chapter 6 proposes a fully integrated BTMLC that operates directly at the battery
  cell voltage level. By employing a binary-tree architecture with series-connected
  sources down to the cell level, the proposed design enables the use of low-voltage,
  high-performance switches with reduced switching losses, controlled via a nearest-
  level control scheme. The complete system, including power stages, control logic,
  gate drivers, voltage level shifters, and protection circuits, is implemented in a
  130-nm BCD process using NLDMOS switches. The single-phase converter ar-
  chitecture allows dynamic selection of individual cell voltages to generate high-
  resolution pseudo-sinusoidal or arbitrary waveforms, paving the way for building
  high-voltage, high-power conversion systems with superior power density and ef-

                                       55
  ficiency. Experimental results from a silicon prototype with a total chip area of
  15 mm2 demonstrate the capability of the proposed converter to deliver multilevel
  output waveforms over a wide frequency range and input cell voltages, while sup-
  plying an output current of 2 A.
• Chapter 7 presents a stackable integrated full-bridge circuit designed for battery
  balancing applications. The proposed approach emphasises the integration of ad-
  ditional components to minimise parasitic impedances that limit the speed and ef-
  ficiency of battery balancers, reduce losses and leakages, and decrease the overall
  size. The circuit facilitates energy transfer between cells using two balancing ca-
  pacitors per battery cell and features a modular design that can be stacked or ex-
  tended on-chip or on-board to meet varying application requirements. Fabricated
  using 0.13-µm BCD technology with 5-V NLDMOS devices, the chip generates
  drive voltages for the high-side and low-side switches internally at no additional
  cost. Occupying an area of 4.6 mm², experimental results demonstrate the cir-
  cuit’s effective balancing of two series-connected batteries across a wide range of
  switching frequencies and input cell voltages.
• Chapter 8 presents a new design and implementation of an integrated eight-battery-
  tap BTMLC. Novel circuit techniques are developed to generate gate-driver supply
  rails for both high-side and low-side switches using cost-effective low-voltage de-
  vices, with rail voltages derived from the existing seven-cell battery stack. The
  complete system, including power stages, control logic, gate drivers, voltage level
  shifters, and protection circuits, is fabricated in a 130-nm BCD process with NLD-
  MOS power devices. Experimental results from a 20.52-mm2 silicon prototype
  demonstrate efficient and reliable driving of multiple converter switches with low
  on-state resistance. The converter is packaged in a commercial J-Leaded Chip Car-
  rier (JLCC) for baseline validation of the proposed gate-drive and rail-generation
  techniques, and in custom PCB assemblies with manually wire-bonded chips for
  reduced path resistance and improved efficiency. The stacking capability is fur-
  ther demonstrated by integrating two modules on board and combining their out-
  puts through an off-chip half-bridge power stage. Measurements confirm that the
  converter can synthesize variable-frequency and/or variable-magnitude half-sine
  waveforms while delivering up to 6 A to a resistive load with a peak efficiency of
  98.5%.

                                       56
• Chapter 9 presents an on-chip battery balancing solution based on two comple-
 mentary half-bridge converters driven by only two open-loop control signals. The
 proposed design supports balancing across four cells and can be readily extended
 to larger battery strings. All control functions, including dead-time generation,
 voltage level shifting, and gate driving, are fully integrated within a 3.4 mm2 chip
 fabricated in a 130-nm BCD process with 5-V NLDMOS switches. For valida-
 tion, two chips are connected to eight external balancing capacitors in a dual-star
 configuration. Experimental results demonstrate robust performance over a wide
 operating range, with switching frequencies from 1 kHz to 1 MHz and input cell
 voltages from 2.5 V to 4.3 V. The circuit delivers up to 2.5 A average balancing
 current under a 0.75 V voltage difference between any cell and the overall average.




                                      57
Chapter 2


Multicell Power Converters and Battery
Balancing Circuits

This chapter reviews the underlying motivations for multilevel voltage synthesis. It also
examines state-of-the-art multilevel power electronic converter architectures, including
self-balancing converter topologies and hybrid topologies with a reduced-component
count. Their performance metrics are compared and analysed based on findings reported
in the technical literature. Furthermore, the chapter presents an overview of BMSs,
with particular emphasis on battery balancing techniques. Various approaches are dis-
cussed, including capacitor-based, converter-based, and transformer/inductor-based bal-
ancing circuits.
The chapter concludes by identifying the limitations of existing converters in terms of
their integration potential and practical feasibility. This includes the hardware require-
ments for implementing these converters and battery balancing circuits, the associated
device ratings, and other relevant topological considerations.


2.1     Principles of Sinusoidal Waveform Synthesis
If the a sinusoidal waveform is approximated by a half-wave symmetric square wave-
form as shown in Fig. 2.1. Symmetry plays a crucial role in generating periodic inverter
waveforms, particularly when approximating a sinusoidal shape. By designing a wave-
form with half-wave symmetry, all even-order harmonics, including the second harmonic,
which is challenging to filter due to its proximity to the fundamental frequency, are inher-
ently eliminated as predicted by Fourier decomposition. This advantage makes half-wave
symmetric patterns a preferred choice for inverter waveform synthesis [47].

      In switched-mode power conversion, unwanted noise can be introduced into the sup-
ply and ground lines, a phenomenon known as conducted EMI. Additionally, radiated
EMI occurs when noise is transmitted through the air. A PWM-controlled voltage can be
represented as a trapezoidal waveform, characterized by its rise time (tr ), fall time (t f ),
and pulse width or on-time (tp ). For simplification, the rise and fall times are assumed
to be equal, denoted as trf = tr = tf . The PWM signal operates at a specific switching
frequency ( fsw ), with a corresponding period T = f1sw . The electromagnetic emissions
associated with this signal can be estimated by analysing its frequency spectrum as given




                                             60
by [48]:
                                                                   

where V̂ is the pulse magnitude in V.

   It represents the Fourier transform of the time-domain PWM waveform and depicts
the envelope of the harmonics present in the trapezoidal signal. The unit used is dBµV
because the voltage is referenced to 1 µV, a standard reference when measuring con-
ducted emissions. This expression is shown in Fig. 2.2 for a specific set of switching
parameters. The first and second corner frequencies of the spectrum highlighted on the
spectrum plot are given by:
                                           1                1
                                  fc1 =       ,    fc2 =                               (2.10)
                                          πtp              πtrf
The noise spectrum consists of three distinct parts, each displaying intuitive characteris-
tics. The first part corresponds to a constant emission level Vn,o , defined by the magnitude
V̂ and the period T of the PWM voltage. This shows that EMI emissions increase with
both the magnitude V̂ and the switching frequency fsw = T1 . The second and third parts
represent asymptotes with slopes of -20 dB and -40 dB per decade, respectively. The first
corner frequency is determined by the PWM pulse width tp . The shorter the pulse width,
the higher the corner frequency. A similar relationship applies to the second corner fre-
quency, which is also related to the rise-fall time trf . Sharp switching transitions push the
corner frequency to higher values, making the EMI noise worse [48, 49].

   EMI noise can manifest in both the voltage and current domains. Voltage-domain
noise arises from rapid voltage transitions that generate currents in the parasitic capaci-
tance of the power devices and their interconnections, leading to a broad-band emission
spectrum as shown in Fig. 2.2. Rapid current changes excite parasitic inductances, re-
                                              di
sulting in voltage spikes expressed by VL = L dt , and cause ringing in parasitic resonant
tanks [49].

   Another important criterion when synthesising AC waveforms using semiconductor
switches is the dynamic loss of the switches that will be used to synthesise the output
voltage. The total switching loss is the sum of both losses and can be approximated
by [50]:
                                       1
                              Pswitch = Vds Ids (ton + toff ) fsw ,                    (2.11)
                                       2

                                              61
Figure 2.2: EMI spectrum of a trapezoidal PWM waveform with 50 µs pulse width, rise-fall
            slewing rate of 4 V/ns, and pulse magnitude of 400 V at 10 kHz switching frequency.


   where Vds and Ids are the drain-source voltage and drain current at the initial instants
of switching, toff and ton are the turn-off and turn-on times, and fsw is the switching
frequency. This shows that high switching frequency PWM and large DC-link voltage
will lead to heavy power losses. This highlights the need for high performance, expensive
switches that need to block high voltage and operate at high switching frequencies, whilst
handling the load current efficiently.

   Given all these factors, several techniques have been used to improve the quality of
voltage synthesis. These include harmonic elimination methods, harmonic cancellation
methods, and multilevel based voltage synthesis.




2.2     Harmonic Reduction Techniques
Minimising the harmonic content in the synthesised output voltage or current is crucial
for various applications. Harmonics not only degrade the power factor of the AC port but
can also disrupt the functionality of converters and other devices by introducing noise
into control circuits. Additionally, harmonics can excite mechanical resonances in elec-
tromechanical loads, leading to acoustic noise and increased energy losses [47].

   The effectiveness of a low-pass filter is significantly lower when used in DC-AC
converters compared to DC-DC converters. This is because, in a DC-DC converter, the
switching frequency is infinitely higher than the input or output frequency, whereas in

                                              62
a DC-AC converter, this ratio is finite. Consequently, in a DC-DC converter, ripple at-
tenuation is mainly constrained by the physical size of the inductor or capacitor and the
required control bandwidth. In contrast, for a DC-AC converter, the size and efficiency
of the filter elements depend on factors such as the allowable attenuation or phase shift
in the fundamental frequency [47].

   To address this, active harmonic reduction techniques are often employed in DC-AC
converters by controlling the switching operation. This section explores two such meth-
ods: harmonic elimination, where switching is controlled to remove specific harmonics,
and harmonic cancellation, where outputs from multiple converters are combined to
neutralize certain harmonics. Furthermore, multilevel converters are discussed, which
trade-off higher design and control complexity for high performance in terms of ratings
of components, power losses, EMI, THD, and fault tolerance capabilities.




2.2.1    Harmonic Elimination


To reduce the harmonics in the synthesised output voltage, harmonic elimination tech-
niques are proposed in [51] and [52]. These techniques are based on deriving optimal
switching patterns to eliminate selected higher-order harmonics from the inverter output
waveforms. To eliminate an M number of harmonics, an equal number of pulses must
be synthesised every half-cycle. Figure 2.3 demonstrates this synthesis process when the
fifth harmonic is eliminated from an output waveform that is originally free of the third
harmonic.
As more harmonics are eliminated, precise switch timing becomes increasingly crucial,
leading to higher switching losses. Additionally, at frequencies that are high multiples
of the fundamental, the turn-on and turn-off times of switches make it challenging to
accurately position the extra pulses in the waveform, posing practical limitations. It is
also important to note that increasing the number of pulses per half-cycle to eliminate
low-order harmonics results in greater high-frequency harmonic content. However, since
high-frequency components are easier to filter, this trade-off is often considered accept-
able [47].

                                           63
Figure 2.3: (a) The third-harmonic-free waveform, is overlaid with a fifth-harmonic sine wave.(b)
            A square wave modified with notches to eliminate both the third and fifth harmonics.
            A fifth-harmonic sine wave demonstrates that its product with Vx (t) results in a net
            area of zero.



2.2.2    Harmonic Cancellation

In this technique, the outputs of multiple converters are added such that their resultant
output has certain harmonics cancelled, as demonstrated in Fig. 2.4.

    Recalling (2.5), and shifting the fundamental component by an angle of ω0t0 :

                                       ∞
                            Vx (t) = ∑ Vn sin(nω0 (t − t0 ) + φn )                        (2.12)
                                     n=1
                                     n odd


This results in a phase-shift of nω0t0 to the nth harmonic:

                                      ∞
                           Vx (t) = ∑ Vn sin(nω0t + φn − nω0t0 )                          (2.13)
                                    n=1
                                    n odd


    For example, the third-harmonic reduced waveform can be obtained by summing

                                               64
two square waves, each with an amplitude of Vdc /2, phase-shifted by 60◦ relative to
each other. This configuration can be implemented using two voltage-source square-
wave inverters. When the switching of one circuit is delayed by 60◦ with respect to the
other, the resulting waveform Vout (t) eliminates the third harmonic. Alternatively, the
absence of the third harmonic in Vout (t) can be understood as the result of two harmonic
components of the same frequency (third harmonic) and amplitude, but with a phase
difference of 3 × 60◦ = 180◦ , leading to their cancellation. The same principle applies
when cancelling the fifth harmonic: two fundamental waveforms are time-shifted by a
phase difference of 36◦ , resulting in a 180◦ phase shift in the fifth-harmonic component,
thus cancelling it from the resultant waveform [47].
Although the harmonic-cancellation synthesis circuit requires twice as many switches
compared to the single output, each switch is subjected to only half the voltage stress.
This reduction in voltage stress can be particularly advantageous in high-voltage AC
applications.


2.3     Multilevel Voltage Synthesis Techniques
In multilevel voltage synthesis techniques, DC voltages of different magnitudes are se-
lectively switched at the output to generate a multilevel waveform. This process typically
involves dividing the input DC link into smaller voltage steps, for instance, by using ca-
pacitors in configurations such as those found in NPC and FCC converters. However, in
battery-powered drivetrains, the DC sources are inherently available in the form of indi-
vidual battery cells, which can be arranged in series and parallel combinations to achieve
the desired DC-link voltage and system capacity. This configuration provides direct ac-
cess to the intermediate battery taps, allowing the multilevel synthesising circuit or MLC
to utilise these voltages directly to generate high-quality multilevel outputs. Converters
employing this technique offers several advantages over traditional converters, particu-
larly in medium- and high-power applications. Some key advantages include: (i) the
ability to connect directly to a medium-voltage network without requiring transformers;
(ii) a significant reduction in THD and EMI due to lower harmonics (refer to (2.8)) and a
lower rate of output voltage variation (refer to (2.9)); (iii) the capacity to achieve higher
power levels without necessitating series or parallel configurations of switches; (iv) offer-
ing of redundant switching states for higher fault-tolerance capability., and (v) the ratings
of the semiconductor devices can be significantly smaller than the total operating voltage

                                             65
Figure 2.4: Harmonic cancellation achieved by summing time-shifted voltages generated by two
            identical synthesisers: (a) introducing a delay of δ T to the fundamental waveform,
            and (b) cancelling both the third and fifth harmonics by applying a fundamental wave-
            form delay of 36◦ , which produces a 180◦ phase shift in the fifth-harmonic compo-
            nents.



of the system.
Most commonly used converters today operate with two or three voltage levels and con-
tribute significantly to THD. The extent of THD reduction is closely linked to the switch-
ing frequency of power semiconductors, which imposes limitations on low- and medium-
power conversions. These factors have driven substantial interest in MLCs.
By employing a series connection of switches, multilevel converters enable the use of
semiconductor devices with lower voltage ratings. Such switches exhibit reduced con-
duction losses and can function at relatively higher frequencies. Increasing the switching
frequency whilst utilising multiple voltage levels leads to improved voltage waveform

                                               66
quality.
However, MLCs also present several conventional challenges that must be considered
when selecting them for power conversion applications. Their modulation strategies are
considerably more intricate than those of traditional converters, and standard microcon-
trollers or digital signal processors may lack the computational and hardware capabilities
required to implement modulation for a large number of levels or submodules, thereby
complicating practical implementation. This limitation can be mitigated through opti-
mised algorithms and the integration of specialised external analogue and digital circuits
designed specifically for modulation tasks.

   Furthermore, control complexities arise depending on the application, such as main-
taining voltage balance across capacitors in FCCs or stabilising the neutral-point volt-
age in NPCs [53]. Other critical challenges in MLCs include the high cost of hardware
implementation, the requirement for floating driver-supply rails, the need for isolated
PWM signals, and the overall complexity of integrating these elements whilst maintain-
ing a high-power-density design that minimises losses and leakage within a reasonable
cooling-system size.

   These challenges become particularly significant as the number of voltage levels to
be synthesised approaches the number of battery cells connected at the converter input.
For example, a battery pack may be configured as 192S7P (as in Tesla’s Cybertruck),
where 192 cells are connected in series in groups of seven parallel cells to achieve the
desired system DC voltage and capacity. If these cells are to be processed individually
or interfaced with the motor through decentralised converter units operating at the cell
level, the approach offers several advantages; however, it also introduces a substantial
increase in the number of required switches. Consequently, this issue cannot be effec-
tively addressed solely through fully onboard solutions based on discrete switches and
conventional gate-driving techniques.

2.3.1      One-Branch Converter
Typically, the output voltage waveform is formed as a staircase pattern composed of mul-
tiple discrete voltage steps. These steps are activated and deactivated through the con-
figuration of semiconductor switches in conjunction with designated DC voltage sources
within the overall topology. Figure 2.5 illustrates the schematic representation of voltage
source inverters with varying levels, each utilising a single switch branch. A conven-

                                              67
tional two-level generator, as depicted in Fig. 2.5(a), produces an output voltage with two
distinct levels: 0 and VDC . In contrast, a three-level generator, shown in Fig. 2.5(b), can
generate three voltages: 0, VDC , and 2VDC . In general, an n-level generator is shown
in Fig. 2.5(c). The number of semiconductor devices required to implement different
switching states is directly proportional to the number of voltage levels. It is evident that
increasing the number of DC sources results in a greater number of output steps. This
relationship between the number of sources and levels can be expressed as:


                                          s = n − 1,                                   (2.14)


where n denotes the total number of voltage levels produced.

   In addition to level generators that operate on series-connected battery cells, another
class of synthesisers functions by configuring the DC sources inherently through their
switching states. In this approach, each DC source is either inserted into the conduction
path to contribute to power delivery or bypassed through the switching network. Such
topologies can generate either positive voltage levels, as illustrated in Fig. 2.5(d), or both
positive and negative levels, as in Fig. 2.5(e).


2.3.2    Two-Branch Converter

To further increase the number of voltage levels, two branches of the previously described
topologies are combined, forming the well-known H-bridge (HB) converter. This config-
uration connects two of the earlier topologies whilst sharing a common set of DC sources,
effectively doubling the number of output levels generated. The schematic representation
of this topology is shown in Figure 2.6 [53]. Within this topology, the concept of ”redun-
dant levels” emerges. The H-bridge configuration includes multiple redundant switching
combinations that yield the same output voltage level. For instance, the zero-voltage
level can be achieved by connecting the load between L1 and L1′ or, more generally, be-
tween Lk and Lk′ . Similarly, the voltage level Vout = 2VDC can be generated by connecting
L3 − L1′ or, in general, Lk+2 − Lk′ . Negative voltage levels are obtained in the reverse
                                                                  ′
manner; for example, Vout = −2VDC can be achieved between Lk and Lk+2 . In general,
all voltage levels generated by the inner switches of either branch are redundant, which
means redundancy occurs between L2′ and Ln−1
                                         ′   (or between L2 and Ln−1 ). Fig. 2.6(a)
marks with blank/white dots all switch positions that can be removed, as they contribute

                                              68
Figure 2.5: Basic multilevel synthesisers: (a) two-level, (b) three-level, (c) n-level, (d) n-level
            with isolated DC sources, and (e) positive and negative level generator with isolated
            DC sources.




only to redundant levels. These switches can be eliminated in either of the two branches.
After their removal, only one redundant level remains, specifically, the state L1 − L1′ ,
which generates the same level as Ln − Ln′ (i.e., Vout = 0). The total number of redundant
levels can be determined directly from Fig. 2.6(a). Each removed switch, corresponding
to n − 2, contributes to generating nR redundant levels. The overall count of redundant
levels is thus given by:
                                       nR = n(n − 2) + 1,                                   (2.15)

                                                69
                    Figure 2.6: Two-branch multi-level converter topology




where n denotes the number of voltage levels in a single branch. Equation (2.15) also
accounts for the redundant states L1 − L1′ and Ln − Ln′ .



2.3.3    n-Level Three-Phase Converter

A Y-connected three-phase voltage synthesiser with a floating-neutral point is shown in
Fig. 2.7 [53]. This configuration produces a higher number of voltage levels at the load,
as the phase-to-neutral voltages are determined by the interaction of all three converter
legs. Each converter leg (phase) can generate n equally spaced voltage levels, denoted
by the set S = {0,VDC1 ,VDC2 , . . . ,VDC(n-1) }. The phase-to-phase voltage is obtained as
the difference between two phase voltages, as in the case of the H-bridge synthesiser
discussed earlier. Since Va , Vb , and Vb can independently assume any of the n DC voltage
levels, the set of all possible differences in the phase-to-phase voltages contains 2n − 1
distinct values (for equally spaced voltage levels). Therefore, the number of phase-to-
phase voltage levels is given by:
                                       np-p = 2n − 1,                               (2.16)

where np-p denotes the number of phase-to-phase voltage levels, and n represents the
number of voltage levels generated by a single synthesiser leg.

   For a Y-connected configuration with a floating neutral, the phase-to-neutral voltage

                                             70
can be expressed as:

                                     Va +Vb +Vc 1
                        VaN = Va −             = (2Va −Vb −Vc ) ,                   (2.17)
                                         3      3

where Va , Vb , and Vc correspond to the phase-leg output voltages. Because VaN depends
on a linear combination of three independently generated voltages, the number of distinct
levels increases further. The integer combination (2Va −Vb −Vc ) can take 4n − 3 unique
values for equally spaced levels, yielding:


                                   np-N = 2np-p − 1 = 4n − 3,                       (2.18)


where np-N denotes the number of phase-to-neutral voltage levels for any of the three
phases (VaN , VbN , or VcN ).

    Equation ( 2.18 ) can be further understood by analysing the effect of the floating
neutral on the phase voltages. In a Y-connected configuration with a floating neutral
point, the neutral potential VN is not fixed but instead assumes the average potential of
the three phase voltages, ensuring that the sum of instantaneous phase-to-neutral voltages
remains zero, i.e.

                                Va +Vb +Vc
                        VN =               ,        VaN +VbN +VcN = 0.              (2.19)
                                    3

Substituting this expression for VN into the definition of the phase-to-neutral voltage
yields:
                                          Va +Vb +Vc 1
                  VaN = Va −VN = Va −               = (2Va −Vb −Vc ) .              (2.20)
                                              3      3

    This relationship highlights that each phase-to-neutral voltage depends not only on
its corresponding leg voltage Va , but also on the voltages of the remaining two legs,
Vb and Vc . Consequently, the potential of the floating neutral dynamically adjusts to
maintain voltage balance among the three phases. From a synthesis perspective, this
coupling effect causes each phase-to-neutral voltage to become a linear combination of
three discrete voltage variables. As a result, even though each converter leg can generate
only n equally spaced levels, the interaction among the three legs produces a substantially
higher number of distinct phase-to-neutral voltage levels, quantified by np-N = 4n − 3.

    For example, a two-level inverter (n = 2) produces np-p = 3 and np-N = 5 voltage lev-

                                               71
                    Figure 2.7: Three-phase multilevel voltage synthesiser


els, whilst a three-level inverter (n = 3) yields np-p = 5 and np-N = 9 levels, confirming the
increased resolution of the phase-to-neutral voltages in the floating-neutral configuration.

   The multilevel converters discussed thus far require semiconductor switching devices
such as Metal-Oxide-Semiconductor Field-Effect Transistor (MOSFET), SiC, or IGBTs
for operation. However, these components have certain limitations and cannot fully repli-
cate the ideal switches described in earlier sections. Their drawbacks include unidirec-
tional operation, restricted switching speeds, and power losses. Additionally, they are un-
able to seamlessly transition between power sources, necessitating specialised multilevel
topologies, which will be explored in the following sections for both series-connected
and isolated input DC sources.



2.4     Multilevel Converters with Series-Connected DC
        Sources
The state-of-the-art MLIs operating with input DC sources connected in series are sum-
marised in Fig. 2.8. These include classical topologies such as the Flying Capacitor Mul-
tilevel Converter (FCML) inverter and the NPC inverter as shown in Fig. 2.8(a-b) which
were proposed in [34, 33].

                                             72
   Each phase leg of the NPC inverter consists of 2n−2 switches and 2n−2 anti-parallel
diodes, along with 2n − 4 clamping diodes to generate an n-level line-to-line output volt-
age. Additionally, the DC link comprises n − 1 capacitors connected in series.

   The FCML converter structure is similar to that of the NPC, but instead of using
clamping diodes, it employs clamping capacitors. Each phase consists of 2n switches and
2n anti-parallel diodes, along with ∑ni=1 (n − i) clamping capacitors to generate an n-level
