line-to-line output voltage. Additionally, the main DC link consists of n − 1 capacitors.
The output voltage of the FCML inverter resembles that of the NPC inverter but provides
more available switching states. This redundancy enables enhanced and more flexible
control of the capacitor voltages. The inverter output voltage vao consists of four levels:
VDC , 0.75VDC , 0.5VDC , 0.25VDC , and 0. Unlike NPC inverters, the intermediate voltage
levels (0.75VDC , 0.5VDC , 0.25VDC ) can be achieved through multiple switching combi-
nations, allowing for greater flexibility in controlling the individual voltages across the
flying capacitors.

   In many cases, the MLI is structured so that one section generates multiple levels with
a single polarity, whilst an H-bridge circuit is responsible for converting this unipolar
waveform into a bipolar one suitable for AC loads. These two sections are referred to
as the ”level-generation part” and the ”polarity-generation part”. The power switches
within the polarity-generation section are rated for a voltage at least equal to the overall
operating voltage of the MLI [54].

   Switching losses in a converter depend on factors such as current, blocking volt-
age, and switching frequency, as was demonstrated using the dynamic losses formula
in (2.11). To reduce these losses, high-voltage power switches should ideally operate
at lower frequencies, preferably at the fundamental power frequency, whilst maintain-
ing the desired output waveform quality. A switch can function at the fundamental fre-
quency if it remains ON throughout an entire half-cycle (either positive or negative) and
remains OFF for the next half-cycle, allowing the multilevel waveform to be generated
at the load terminals. Therefore, fundamental frequency switching is a modulation con-
trol feature, provided the topology supports it. Additionally, when a topology includes
switches with varying voltage ratings, switching losses should be distributed efficiently.
Higher voltage-rated switches should ideally operate at lower frequencies, whereas lower
voltage-rated switches should function at higher frequencies. If the topology allows, the


                                            73
switching frequency should be strategically adjusted to optimise performance. Further-
more, if the level-generation section of a topology can produce a zero-voltage level, then
the polarity-generation switches can operate at line frequency [54].

    Various MLI topologies implementing these features are reviewed in Fig. 2.8(c-
i). These converter architectures are named as found in prior art and they include
the P2 topology in Fig. 2.8(c) [55], T-Type topology in Fig. 2.8(d) [56, 57, 58], the
Cascaded Bipolar Switched Cells (CBSC) topology in Fig. 2.8(e) [59], the Multilevel
Module (MLM) topology in Fig. 2.8(f) [60], the Reversing Voltage (RV) topology in
Fig. 2.8(h) [60], and the Two-Switch Enabled Level-Generation (2SELG) topology in
Fig. 2.8(i) [61].

    The P2 converter is capable of independently balancing each voltage level without re-
lying on inverter control or load characteristics. Conventional multilevel inverters, such
as diode-clamped and capacitor-clamped configurations, can be derived from this gener-
alised topology. Furthermore, this topology inherently maintains voltage level balance
across different levels, irrespective of whether the power conversion is active or reactive,
eliminating the need for additional circuitry [55].
An M-level inverter can be constructed using a fundamental cell. The generalised M-level
phase leg forms a horizontal pyramid structure composed of these basic cells. Since each
fundamental cell functions as a two-level phase leg, this topology is referred to as the P2
MLI. To illustrate its operational principle and analyse its behaviour, a five-level circuit is
examined in Fig. 2.8(c). This circuit integrates power devices, diodes, and capacitors to
generate the required voltage waveforms whilst ensuring capacitor voltage clamping and
balancing. In a P2-level converter, the number of required switching devices and diodes
is given by M(M − 1), whilst the number of capacitors is M(M − 1)/2, which directly
corresponds to the pyramid structure. Each component experiences a voltage stress of
one unit, with all voltage levels automatically balanced through clamping switches and
diodes.
To control this topology, the following switching rules must be adhered to [55]: 1. Each
switch pole functions as an independent switching unit. 2. Any two adjacent switches
within a switch pole operate in a complementary manner, meaning that when one is on,
the other is off, and vice versa. 3. Once the state of a single switch is determined, the
states of the remaining switches within that pole are automatically set due to the comple-



                                              74
Figure 2.8: Multilevel Converters with Series-Connected DC Sources




                               75
mentary switching rule.
Several existing MLI configurations can be derived from this generalised topology. For
instance, eliminating all clamping switches from Fig. 2.8(c) results in the NPC MLI.
Further removing both clamping switches and diodes from Fig. 2.8(c) yields the FCML
inverter [55].
By extending the P2 inverter’s pyramid structure, additional configurations can be de-
veloped from this generalised topology. One such example is the P3 inverter, which
utilises three-level basic cells. A specific case is the P3D MLI, based on a three-level
diode-clamped phase leg. Similarly, a P3C MLI is derived from a three-level capacitor-
clamped phase leg. In these configurations, each component still experiences a voltage
stress of one level. Compared to the P2 topology, the P3D and P3C inverters require
fewer clamping switches, diodes, and capacitors whilst maintaining the same number of
levels [55].
In a P3D M-level converter, the required number of switching devices and diodes is
(M 2 − 1)/2, whilst the number of capacitors or clamping diodes is (M 2 − 1)/4. For a
P3C M-level converter, the required switching devices and diodes count is (M 2 − 1)/4,
and the number of capacitors is (M 2 − 1)/8. Compared to the P2 converter, both the
P3D and P3C topologies offer a reduction in the number of components. For example,
whilst a P2 five-level inverter requires 20 switching devices/diodes and 10 capacitors per
phase leg, a P3D five-level inverter requires only 12 switching devices/diodes and six
capacitors [55].

   The T-Type inverter, CBSC, and MLM require switches with bidirectional capa-
bility to be able to isolate the battery cells.   The decision to use symmetric, i.e.,
Vdc,1 = Vdc,2 = Vdc,3 = Vdc,n = Vdc , or asymmetric input DC sources depends on the topol-
ogy’s capability to synthesise all possible additive or subtractive voltage level combina-
tions at the load terminals. In T-Type, CBSC, MLM, RV, and 2SELG topologies, either a
positive or negative voltage for some levels can be synthesised, but not both. Therefore,
they must employ symmetric input sources. Moreover, equal load sharing (for energy
balancing) among the input voltage sources is not possible for these topologies due to
the limited number of valid switching states. This stems from the fact that not all source
combinations contribute to each output voltage cycle. This constraint poses challenges in
maintaining voltage balance when a DC-link is formed using connected capacitors/cells.



                                           76
   In most of these topologies, the blocking voltages and switching frequencies for the
switches are not simple to formulate as they are dependent on their location in the cir-
cuits. For example, in the CBSC topology, different switches experience varying voltage
stresses. Specifically, the outermost bidirectional switches Sa5 , Sm5 , Sa1 , and Sm1 must
be rated to block a minimum of 4Vdc each (for n = 4). Meanwhile, the inner switches
Sa4 , Sm2 , Sa2 , and Sm4 should have a blocking capability of at least 3Vdc , and switches
Sa3 and Sm3 must withstand a voltage stress of 2Vdc .



2.5     Multilevel Converters with Isolated DC Sources
Another major class of MLCs involves DC input sources having no direct series connec-
tion between them. This could add flexibility to address the drawbacks discussed in the
series-connected MLCs such as the unequal load sharing, high blocking voltages, and
switching frequencies. This is enabled by the ability to reconfigure the input sources to
be connected in series and/or in parallel based on different topological states.

   From an on-chip integration perspective, the isolated DC sources require signal iso-
lation and floating supplies for gate drivers. This makes utilising the battery cells for the
driving purposes harder than the series DC sources. Chapter 3 will look into these issues
in more details.

   These topologies include classical dual-star MMC shown in Fig. 2.9(a) [62, 63, 64]
and the Cascaded H-Bridge (CHB) topology in Fig. 2.9(b) [65, 66]. Other configurations
include the Multilevel DC-Link (MLDCL) topology in Fig. 2.9(c) [67], the Switched
Series/Parallel Sources (SSPS) in Fig. 2.9(d) [68], Series-Connected Switched Sources
(SCSS) in Fig. 2.9(e) [69], and Packed-U Cell (PUC) in Fig. 2.9(f) [70, 71, 72, 73].

   The MMC has been validated as a viable main converter for BEVs, whilst also en-
abling battery recharging through a cell-charging equalisation strategy [30, 74]. In mo-
toring operation, this approach theoretically removes the need for a traditional BMS.
Instead, cell balancing is performed concurrently with energy conversion by dynamically
modulating the load on individual cells, imposing a higher load on cells with a higher
SOC and a lower load on those with a lower SOC [30, 74]. However, this technique may
introduce operational risks because it relies on Coulomb counting to estimate cell SOCs.
Accumulated estimation errors may lead to repeated overcharging or over-discharging,
which could compromise the cells’ State of Health (SOH) over time. Furthermore, the

                                             77
inclusion of buffer inductors increases the physical size of the converter.

   Recent research has focused on increasing the integration of the power electronics
with each battery cell or group of cells, resulting in what has been termed a Battery-
Integrated MMC (BI-MMC) [75]. However, the computational burden rises substantially
in cell-level converter designs, where a large number of submodules must each be pro-
vided with separate control signals and floating driver supplies. Moreover, the large
number of devices in the conduction path may lead to high conduction and battery power
losses.

   The CHB topology, shown in Fig. 2.9(b), has likewise been employed as a BIMI, in
which the voltage per H-bridge is significantly reduced to allow the use of low-voltage
MOSFET switches, hence reducing THD, EMI, and enabling both inversion, charging
and balancing functionalities within a single converter [37, 75]. Recently, [41] conducted
a comparative assessment between a two-level inverter implemented using SiC devices
and a CHB employing low-voltage switches (approximately 30 V). The results indicate
that, at the nominal operating point, power losses (conduction) are reduced by a factor
of five in the two-level SiC-based design. BIMIs exhibit lower total losses only when
the phase currents are very small (below roughly 15%), owing to the switching losses
inherent to the two-level inverter. Moreover, because BIMIs operate in a phase-modular
manner, they introduce a battery power-flow pulsation at twice the mains frequency, re-
sulting in roughly double the battery losses at the nominal operating point compared with
a two-level system, and even greater losses at lower output voltages [41].



2.6       Battery Management Systems
The BMS is a vital component in battery-powered drive systems, such as BEVs. It plays
a critical role in ensuring the safety, reliability, and performance of the battery pack
by managing several essential functions. These include monitoring the SOC and SOH,
addressing thermal management, and providing various protective measures. A typical
BMS consists of several key units, as demonstrated in Fig. 2.10:

   1. Cell Monitoring Unit (CMU): Responsible for parameter detection such as
      cell/pack voltage, discharge/charge current, and cell/ambient temperature. It also
      provides state estimations such as SOC, SOH, and State of Function (SOF). All
      these parameters and states are relayed to the control unit.

                                            78
Figure 2.9: Multilevel Converters with Isolated DC Sources



                           79
   2. Control and Management Unit: This unit controls cell balancing, charge and
      discharge, and thermal management based on inputs from the CMU. It employs
      active or passive battery balancing circuits.
   3. Protection Unit: Responsible for fault diagnosis in batteries, insulations, sensors,
      and actuators. Additionally, it provides safety control against overcharge and over-
      discharge voltages and short-circuit currents.
   4. Communication Unit: Includes a Controller Area Network (CAN) transceiver, se-
      rial Electrically Erasable Programmable Read-Only Memory (EEPROM) for data
      recording, user interfaces, SOC, SOH indicators, and failure alarms.

   In a battery pack, cells are typically configured in series and parallel to meet the
voltage, current, and capacity requirements of the drive system. Over time, repeated
charging and discharging cause imbalances in the capacities of these series-connected
cells. This results in variations in cell voltages and SOC across the battery string. The
root causes of these imbalances include differences in the cells’ electrical properties, such
as capacitance, internal impedance, and self-discharge rate—as well as non-uniform self-
discharge due to intercell and ambient temperature gradients, leakage currents, and the
cells’ chemical characteristics.

   To ensure the safety and longevity of the battery whilst maximising energy utilisation,
it is crucial to minimise voltage imbalances. Addressing these imbalances prevents a sin-
gle low-voltage cell from triggering a premature system shutdown, which would reduce
the effective range of the drive system. Similarly, it avoids a high-voltage cell limiting
the charging current, which would prolong the charging time.

   These challenges can be effectively managed by implementing either passive or active
battery balancing strategies. Passive balancing methods rely on dissipative components to
discharge excess energy from overcharged cells, whilst active balancing methods provide
a more efficient and faster solution by redistributing energy among the cells. They offer
different energy transfer scenarios, such as from cell-to-cell (adjacent or direct), cell-
to-pack, pack-to-cell, or cell-to-pack-to-cell. Active balancing includes capacitor-based,
inductor/transformer-based, and converter-based balancers [45].

   In this section, state-of-the-art battery balancing circuits are critically evaluated based
on design complexity, modularity, scalability, balancing speed, component count and
size, device ratings (voltage/current stress), control complexity, and overall efficiency.


                                             80
                     Figure 2.10: Functional block diagram of a BMS



2.6.1    Battery Cell Types and Characteristics

Battery cells are the most expensive component of the powertrain in BEVs. Conse-
quently, selecting the appropriate battery type for a specific drive application requires
a thorough evaluation of the various types and their features. Prominent types of bat-
tery cells include Lithium-ion (Li-ion), Lithium Iron Phosphate (LFP), and solid-state
batteries.

    Table 2.1 provides a comparative overview of the most common battery chemistries
employed in LDVs, evaluated in terms of cost, energy density, thermal stability, and cycle
life. These include Lithium Cobalt Oxide (LCO), LFP, Lithium-ion Manganese Oxide
(LMO), Nickel Manganese Cobalt Oxide (NMC) 622, NMC 811, Nickel Cobalt Alu-
minium Oxides (NCA), and Lithium and Manganese Rich NMC (LMR-NMC) [76]. As
observed, LCO offers moderate energy density and cycle life but suffers from high cost
and poor thermal stability, making it less attractive for large-scale BEV applications. In

                                           81
        Table 2.1: An overview of different battery chemistries typically used in LDVs

 Material       Cost            Energy          Thermal         Cycle Life      Charging
                                Density         Stability                       Cycle Life
 LCO            High            Moderate        Poor            Moderate        500–1000
 LFP            Low             Low             Good            Good            2000–4000
 LMO            Low             Moderate        Good            Poor            500–1000
 NMC 622        High            High            Moderate        Good            ∼1500
 NMC 811        High            High            Poor            Moderate        ∼2000
 NCA            High            High            Poor            Moderate        300–500
 LMR-NMC        Moderate        High            Moderate        Poor            ∼500



contrast, LFP demonstrates outstanding thermal stability, long cycle life, and low cost,
making it one of the most robust and commercially mature chemistries for practical LDV
deployment. Its primary drawback lies in its relatively lower energy density. LMO ex-
hibits comparable cost advantages and good thermal performance but is constrained by
relatively short cycle life. NMC chemistries, particularly NMC 622 and NMC 811, pro-
vide a balanced compromise between energy density and stability, offering high specific
energy and moderate-to-good cycle life, albeit with increased cost and reduced thermal
robustness at higher nickel content. NCA delivers one of the highest energy densities but
remains limited by its short cycle life and poor thermal stability, requiring advanced ther-
mal management systems. Finally, LMR-NMC materials promise high energy density
and moderate thermal stability but currently face challenges related to cycle life degrada-
tion. Overall, the table highlights the inherent trade-offs among cost, performance, and
durability across chemistries, with LFP standing out as a cost-effective and thermally sta-
ble solution offering a strong balance between safety, longevity, and economic viability
for mainstream LDV applications [76, 44, 77].


2.6.2    Design Principles of Cell Balancing

Figure 2.11 illustrates the principal battery balancing strategies. These include passive
methods, in which excess or redundant energy is dissipated through resistive elements
such as transistors or resistors; active methods, which redistribute energy among cells
through various transfer modes depending on the balancer topology and control strategy;
and approaches that regulate cell charging and discharging via the operation of the main
converter, as commonly implemented in MMCs. Alternatively, energy balancing can
be achieved by assigning an isolated DC-DC converter to each cell, enabling individual

                                             82
control of power flow during operation. In this configuration, the auxiliary converters
provide additional current paths for the cells, allowing precise regulation of the current
drawn from each cell based on its SOC or terminal voltage [78].

   According to the path of energy transfer, active battery balancers can be further cat-
egorised into four fundamental principles. The first principle involves energy transfer
between adjacent battery cells, referred to as Adjacent Cell-to-Cell (AC2C). The second
principle enables energy transfer between any two cells in the pack, known as Direct
Cell-to-Cell (DC2C). The third principle enables energy transfer between arbitrary cells
within a pack, known as Any Cell-to-Any-Cell (AC2AC). The fourth principle facili-
tates energy transfer between individual cells and the entire battery pack or vice versa,
termed the Cell-to-Pack (C2P) and Pack-to-Cell (P2C) types. Lastly, the fourth principle
manages energy transfer between individual cells and the source/load, designated as the
cell-to-bus type [45]. The AC2C method transfers energy between two adjacent cells
and features simple control; however, its efficiency is low if the highest and lowest volt-
age cells are far apart. The DC2C method overcomes this issue but suffers from slower
operation due to transferring energy between only two cells per cycle. Additionally, it
has low efficiency due to its complex switching network and high-power losses. The
C2P method transfers energy from a high-voltage cell to the battery pack, though it may
waste energy by charging already full cells, reducing efficiency and potentially damaging
the pack. Conversely, the P2C method transfers energy from the battery pack to a low-
voltage cell, offering fast balancing compared at increased control complexity. Among
all, the AC2AC method is the most efficient, as it enables simultaneous energy transfer
from multiple high-voltage cells to low-voltage cells in each cycle [46].
From the perspective of energy transfer elements, battery balancers can be classified
into three circuit types: capacitor-based, magnetic element-based, and converter-based.
Converter-based circuits primarily utilise conventional DC/DC converters, such as buck-
boost and Ćuk converters. It should be illustrated that multiport converters incorporating
multi-winding transformers fall under the magnetic element-based category [45]. Var-
ious voltage equaliser circuits have been introduced in recent years, each designed for
specific applications. These circuits are compared based on balancing speed, size, com-
ponent count, cost, voltage stress, reliability, and scalability. Selecting the most suitable
voltage equaliser depends on application-specific requirements. Additionally, ongoing
research aims to address existing limitations and develop more efficient equalising cir-

                                             83
                   Figure 2.11: Overview of battery balancing techniques


cuits to achieve optimal performance [45, 46].



2.7     Capacitive Battery Balancing Techniques
Unlike dissipative balancing schemes, switched-capacitor balancing circuits transfer en-
ergy directly between battery cells during both charging and discharging operations. In
these systems, energy transfer is facilitated by capacitors, which can be arranged in vari-
ous configurations as shown in Fig. 2.12. Each configuration involves trade-offs in terms
of balancing speed, energy transfer mechanism, voltage and current stress on switches
and capacitors, component count, driver circuit complexity, and control strategy (ranging
from open-loop to algorithm- or sensor-based control).

   In Single-Tiered Switched Capacitor (STSC) method shown in Fig. 2.12(a), energy is
transferred between adjacent cells using capacitors as intermediaries. For a battery pack
with N cells, N − 1 capacitors and 2N switches are required. The switches actively con-
nect the positive and negative plates of each capacitor to the corresponding terminals of
the battery cells, enabling energy exchange through the capacitor [79].

                                            84
Figure 2.12: Switched-capacitor battery balancers




                       85
This method supports energy transfer during both recharging and discharging processes.
The balancing current depends on three factors: the switching frequency fsw , the capaci-
tance value, the path resistance, and voltage difference between the cells.
One of the key advantages of this technique is its simple control mechanism; a single
control signal can be used to manage the entire circuit stack. Additionally, the switches
and capacitors experience relatively low voltage stress, as the voltage across any single
switch or capacitor is limited to the voltage of an individual cell.
However, the primary limitation of this scheme is its slow balancing speed. Since en-
ergy transfer occurs only in the AC2C mode, the method is inefficient when addressing
voltage imbalances between non-adjacent cells.

   To enhance the speed of equalisation between non-adjacent battery cells in a string,
a second tier of capacitors can be added to the STSC balancing circuit, as shown in
Fig. 2.12(b). This second tier creates a direct energy transfer path between non-adjacent
cells, reducing the charge shuttling time from two cycles (in the single-tier scheme) to
a single cycle, thanks to the bridging capacitors. This significantly decreases the overall
balancing time for the battery string [80].
For a battery string with an odd number of cells N = 2k + 1, where k = 1, 2, . . . , r, the
hardware requirements include 2N switches and 3r capacitors. For example, balancing 7
cells requires 9 capacitors and 14 switches. The second-tier capacitors must have voltage
ratings of 2VDD , where VDD is the maximum voltage of the battery.Whilst this approach
reduces equalisation time by efficiently connecting non-adjacent cells, it comes at the
cost of increased hardware complexity, higher implementation costs, and lower power
density.

   Another scheme uses only a Single-Switched Capacitor (SSC) to perform battery
balancing, as shown in Fig. 2.12(c), following the DC2C energy transfer principle. The
capacitor is charged and discharged during each cycle, either in continuous mode or
selective mode, using N + 5 switches.
In continuous mode, the controller sequentially opens and closes switches to transfer
charge between the battery cells. In the selective or detecting mode, the BMS monitors
cell voltages and sends the data to a controller. A balancing algorithm identifies the
cell with the highest SOC or voltage and uses the capacitor to transfer charge to the
cell with the lowest SOC or voltage. This process continues until all cells are balanced



                                              86
within a predefined threshold, typically a SOC difference of 1–2%. Compared to the
continuous mode, the sophisticated algorithm of the selective mode significantly reduces
the equalisation time.
The switches in this configuration are high voltage switches, and the blocking voltage of
each switch varies depending on their location and the applied switching sequence.

   A Chain Switched-Capacitor (Chain SC) proposed in [81], is depicted in Fig. 2.12(d).
In this design, the top and bottom cells of the battery string are connected via an addi-
tional capacitor and four switches. This configuration allows energy exchange between
distant cells, hence improving balancing speed as compared to the original STSC circuit.
However, for long battery strings, the additional switches experience high voltage stress,
which is nearly equal to the total string voltage. As a consequence, this structure is more
practical for smaller battery strings.
In an alternate variant of this design, the added high-voltage switches are removed, and
the capacitor is directly connected in parallel with the first tier of capacitors. Whilst this
approach eliminates the need for high-voltage switches, it increases the voltage rating
of the additional capacitor and slightly reduces the balancing speed due to the higher
resistance of the energy transfer path.

   In the switched-capacitor configuration shown in Fig. 2.12(e), the number of balanc-
ing capacitors matches the number of battery cells, along with an additional 4N switches,
to achieve energy balancing based on the AC2AC principle. The balancing process op-
erates in two phases within each switching cycle [82]. In the first phase, the first array
of switches, located closer to the battery cells, is turned on, allowing the battery cells to
exchange energy with the balancing capacitors. In the second phase, the first array of
switches is turned off, and the second array is activated, connecting the capacitors in par-
allel. This parallel connection enables charge to flow from capacitors with higher voltage
to those with lower voltage.

   At a high switching frequency, this configuration achieves fast balancing speeds that
are largely independent of the number of cells and the initial voltage mismatch between
them. However, the method has its drawbacks. The use of additional switches with high
voltage capability, especially for long battery strings, increases system complexity and
cost.

   A Star Switched-Capacitor (Star SC) battery balancer was proposed in [83], as shown


                                             87
in Fig. 2.12(f). The same number of capacitors and switches as in the STSC circuit is
needed. This architecture allows AC2AC exchange of energy. This increases the balanc-
ing speed tremendously, especially for larger battery strings. This is because it provides
paths between all cells of the string via the star-connected capacitors. Moreover, the
balancing speed is claimed to be independent of the number of cells and their initial
voltage distributions.
Another version of this circuit was proposed in [84], where the bottom capacitor con-
necting to the under most Single Pole Double Throw Switch (SPDT) output is removed
and the connection is directly made not via a capacitor. It is claimed that this improves
the balancing speed by N2 . Although this reduces the capacitors by 1, it increase the stress
across the capacitors in the entire circuit, which is not suitable for large packs.
Furthermore, the authors of [85, 86] proposed extensions to the star-configured circuit by
increasing the number of balancing capacitors, hence creating additional balancing paths
and reducing the overall switched-capacitor impedance of the network. They employed
the switching-limit impedance analysis method introduced in [87] to estimate both the
Slow Switching Limit (SSL) and Fast Switching Limit (FSL) resistances, enabling eval-
uation of the impedance reduction achieved in the proposed configurations. These new
topologies, termed mesh and delta switched-capacitor balancers, derive their names from
the corresponding network architectures.
Overall, these topologies improve balancing speed, they have simple control, and balanc-
ing can be carried out during charging or discharging processes. The major drawbacks of
these topologies, however, lie in the requirement for numerous high-voltage capacitors
when applied to large battery strings. Additionally, the topmost and bottommost cells
get disconnected for half of the switching cycle, resulting in a discontinuous balancing
current that compromises the balancing speed.
To address this limitation, [88] proposes a full-bridge extension of the half-bridge mod-
ule, in which the balancing circuit in Fig. 2.12(f) is duplicated and operated in a comple-
mentary manner. This configuration maintains continuous current flow into and out of the
cells throughout the entire switching cycle, thereby achieving a significant improvement
in balancing speed and a higher average balancing current relative to the cells’ Root-
Mean-Square (RMS) current. Consequently, balancing efficiency is enhanced, and cell
self-heating is reduced. However, these benefits come at the cost of doubled hardware
requirements, making the approach less practical for discrete implementations due to the

                                             88
increased switching losses and large number of switches, gate drivers, and high-voltage
capacitors, where 2N capacitors and 4N switches are required, with the stress across the
capacitors reaching (N − 1)VDD /2.




2.8     Magnetic-Based Battery Balancing Techniques
This category of balancing circuits utilises inductors and/or transformers as the primary
balancing elements, supplemented by switches, such as single and multi-switched induc-
tors, single- and multi-winding transformers, as well as multiple transformers topolo-
gies [89, 90, 91, 92, 93, 94, 95, 96, 97, 98, 99, 100, 101, 102, 103]. Compared to ca-
pacitive balancers, transformer- and inductor-based balancers could offer high current-
handling capabilities, resulting in fast balancing speeds. However, this approach has no-
table drawbacks, including the bulky size of magnetic components, core losses, and the
requirement for higher switching frequencies to reduce component size. In this review,
two balancing methods employing magnetic elements will be examined.

   Figure 2.13 illustrates a DC2C-based balancing strategy designed for a battery pack
composed of N cells. The system is composed of three main sections: (1) a Cell Selection
Network (CSN) constructed from N + 1 bidirectional switches, (2) a conventional two-
winding transformer T , and (3) a selector circuit with two bidirectional switches [90],
[91].

   Each pair of MOSFETs in the CSN is arranged in a totem–pole configuration and
driven by a single control signal, ensuring that both devices switch ON and OFF si-
multaneously. The CSN is responsible for connecting the selected cells, those requiring
either charging or discharging—to the remainder of the equalisation circuit. The second
component is a standard transformer with two windings connected in opposite polari-
ties. The third component, the selector circuit, consists of four independently controlled
MOSFETs also configured in a totem–pole structure. These selector switches determine
which transformer winding is connected to a particular cell during the magnetisation and
demagnetisation intervals.

   The converter operates cyclically through four distinct intervals: a magnetisation
phase, a demagnetisation phase, and two short dead-time intervals separating them. After
each demagnetisation interval, a brief non-conduction period occurs before the next cycle

                                           89
begins. For example, in a system with N = 5 cells, consider transferring energy from the
source cell B1 (at a higher voltage) to the target cell B3 (at a lower voltage).

Magnetisation Period During the magnetisation period, switches S1 , S2 , and selector
switch S7 are turned ON, connecting the source cell B1 across the upper transformer
winding. This configuration applies the voltage of B1 to the primary winding, allowing
current to build up linearly in the transformer and thereby storing magnetic energy.

Dead-Time Interval Before the energy transfer to the receiving cell begins, switches S1
and S2 must be turned OFF to prevent a short circuit between cells B1 and B3 . Only af-
ter this dead time can switches S3 and S4 —associated with cell B3 —be turned ON. The
duration of this interval is determined by the MOSFET turn-off time under maximum cur-
rent conditions. During the dead-time, both transformer windings are effectively open-
circuited, causing voltage spikes across them. To suppress these transients, each winding
is equipped with a pair of antiparallel Zener diodes. In this configuration, one diode con-
ducts for positive voltage spikes while the other clamps negative ones, limiting the peak
voltage and protecting the switching devices.

Demagnetisation Period Once switches S3 and S4 are activated (and S1 , S2 are OFF), the
sink cell B3 is connected across the lower transformer winding through the conducting
selector switch S10 . The magnetic energy previously stored in the transformer is then
released to charge B3 . During this interval, current flows through switches S3 , S4 , and S8 ,
completing the energy transfer path.

   This sequence of operations enables controlled energy exchange between selected
cells via the transformer, allowing efficient voltage equalization within the battery pack
while safeguarding switching components against overvoltage stress.

   The transformer and selector circuit could be replaced with an H-bridge configura-
tion, hence eliminating the need for a secondary winding. However, each H-bridge switch
would need to be bidirectional, as demonstrated in Fig. 2.13, which can lead to increased
conduction and switching losses.

   A modularised architecture for balancing a large number of cells is proposed in [92].
However, a major limitation of this approach is the need for a sophisticated control algo-
rithm to accurately estimate the final SOC of each module.

   Another technique utilises a Multiwinding Transformer (MwT) with N windings to

                                              90
 Figure 2.13: Cell selection network with LC resonant converter or transformer energy carrier


balance N battery cells, as shown in Fig. 2.14 [104]. The system includes N capaci-
tor output filters across each battery cell, along with N series switches connected to the
transformer windings. All the switches are controlled by a single signal. The capacitors
placed in parallel with the transformer magnetising inductance, enable the magnetising
currents to reset and achieve soft switching through resonance.
When the switches are turned on, energy flows from higher voltage cells to the corre-
sponding transformer windings. Conversely, energy flows from the transformer to lower
voltage cells when required. By switching on and off, the cells are coupled to the multi-
winding transformer, and their voltages equalise across the windings. In fact, this bal-
ancer applies the AC2AC energy transfer mode.
The gate-driving signals for the switches can be shifted to the corresponding voltage
domains using a multi-winding isolation transformer. Alternatively, a multiple-output
voltage level shifter, as proposed in Chapter 4, can be used to simplify the hardware and
reduce complexity. This approach is also compatible with chip-scale implementation.
Additionally, battery taps can serve as gate driver supplies, further reducing complexity.
A primary disadvantage of this method is its scalability. Balancing tens or hundreds of
battery cells is impractical due to the complexity of designing and implementing a multi-
winding transformer for such a large system. To overcome this, a modular circuit design

                                             91
               Figure 2.14: Multi-winding transformer-based battery balancer


can be adopted. For example, the circuit can balance smaller groups of cells (e.g., 10–12
cells) independently. External balancing circuits can then be used to equalise the voltage
between these groups, as previously discussed for capacitive balancing systems. Finally,
the symmetry of the transformer windings is crucial to ensure accurate balancing results.




2.9     Converters-Based Battery Balancing Techniques
Full control over the balancing process in the BMS can be achieved through the use
of various power converter topologies. These include, among others, full-bridge, buck-
boost, flyback, quasi-resonant, ramp, and Ćuk converters [105, 106, 107, 108, 109, 110].
Whilst these methods provide precise and efficient energy management, implementing
them within the BMS results in relatively higher system complexity and cost compared
to the other techniques discussed.

   A balancing strategy that inherently manages the charging and discharging processes
by employing isolated battery cells is shown in Fig. 2.15 [111]. The method involves
a distributed Battery Energy Storage System (BESS) architecture, for which an energy-
sharing SOC balancing controller is applied. Unlike conventional battery configurations
where individual cells are connected in series to form a string and the overall string volt-
age is regulated by a single high-power DC–DC converter rated at X, this architecture
decouples the cells electrically. Each cell is instead interfaced with its own lower-power
DC–DC converter rated at X/N, where N is the total number of cells in the series string.
As shown in Fig. 2.15, the outputs of these individual low-power converters are connected
in series to generate a high-voltage DC bus that supplies power to the system load. This
structure effectively forms a chain of Battery Power Units (BPUs), where each BPU
comprises a single battery cell and an open-loop DC–DC converter. In this configuration,

                                            92
each converter operates without an independent closed-loop controller, yet its current and
voltage measurements are monitored and fed into an external energy-sharing controller.
To reduce hardware cost and system complexity, multiple cells may be connected in se-
ries or parallel within each BPU, though this may result in a moderate reduction in overall
performance.
The centralised energy-sharing controller generates control commands, specifically, the
duty cycles for each of the N DC–DC converters, to regulate the BPU output voltages,
manage the charge and discharge rates of individual cells for SOC equalisation, and main-
tain the DC bus voltage at its reference value. In this arrangement, the small DC–DC
converters fulfil both SOC balancing and DC bus voltage regulation functions, eliminat-
ing the need for two separate converter systems.
Each converter in a BPU must be bidirectional to support both charging and discharg-
ing processes. Isolation may also be required depending on the system design; however,
for simplicity, a conventional non-isolated bidirectional buck–boost converter is typically
employed. During discharging, the converter operates in boost mode, using the battery
cell as the input and the DC bus as the output. Conversely, during charging, it functions
in buck mode, where the DC bus supplies energy back to the cell. The selection of a suit-
able converter topology depends on various design parameters, including the target BPU
power rating, power conversion efficiency, power density, cost, packaging and integration
considerations, and EMI constraints.


Discharging Operation During discharge, any deviation of a cell’s SOC from the ref-
erence value, representative of the average SOC of all cells, leads to a corresponding
adjustment in the output voltage reference of its associated BPU. Since the load current
is common to all BPUs in the series connection, varying the output voltage of an in-
dividual unit alters its power contribution and thus its discharge rate. This mechanism
continues automatically until all cells reach balanced SOC values. In essence, by regu-
lating each BPU’s output voltage whilst keeping the total DC bus voltage constant, the
system controls the energy drawn from each cell. This energy-sharing control method
achieves fast and automatic SOC equalisation across the entire battery pack.


Charging Operation During charging, a DC bus voltage is applied to the system, and
each converter operates in buck mode, drawing energy from the bus and supplying it to its
corresponding cell. The same energy-sharing control principle is employed to maintain

                                            93
                    Figure 2.15: Modular buck/boost converter equaliser


balanced SOC levels among all cells. The controller initially enforces a Constant-Current
Charging Mode (CCCM) until the cell voltage reaches a predefined maximum threshold
(for instance, 4.3 V for a Li-ion cell). Once this threshold is reached, the controller tran-
sitions to Constant-Voltage Charging Mode (CVCM), which continues until the charging
current falls below a specified fraction (e.g., 5%) of the cell’s nominal capacity. Since
the input current Iin is identical for all BPUs, the charging power delivered to each cell
depends on the input voltage of its converter. The SOC-balancing control loop ensures
that charge distribution remains uniform throughout the process [111].
Despite its technical advantages, this topology entails significant hardware and control
complexity. It requires voltage and current sensing for all BPUs to provide feedback to
the energy-sharing controller, especially during discharging operation. The controller it-
self employs two nested control loops: one for regulating the BPU output voltage and
another for maintaining SOC balance among cells. Current sensors are essential for es-
timating cell SOC using the conventional coulomb-counting method. Similarly, during
charging, current sensing is required for precise control and balancing. Consequently, the
architecture achieves good performance and flexibility/modularity at the cost of increased
hardware requirements and computational burden.

   A modular architecture that employs low-voltage (LV) DC–DC “bypass” convert-
ers to achieve active cell balancing while simultaneously supplying current to auxiliary
loads is proposed in [110]. This approach eliminates the need for a centralised BMS

                                             94
balancing controller and a separate high step-down HV-to-LV converter. As illustrated in
Fig. 2.16, the proposed system distributes the functionality of the conventional high step-
down converter across multiple isolated DC–DC bypass modules, each associated with
a single battery cell. These bypass converters are configured in a series-input, parallel-
output arrangement, with each input connected directly in parallel to its respective cell.
Consequently, the total LV bus load current is shared among all cells through their corre-
sponding bypass converters.

   Each bypass converter operates independently in a fully distributed manner, using
the shared LV bus voltage as a common reference. By regulating its cell voltage to
match a scaled version of this reference, every converter drives the cell voltages toward
equalisation, thereby achieving cell balancing.

   The proposed architecture can operate with either unidirectional or bidirectional con-
verters. In unidirectional mode, balancing is achieved passively through the auxiliary
LV bus load, avoiding the additional losses typically associated with active charge re-
distribution. However, this mode may be insufficient in applications where the auxiliary
load is not always available or large enough to balance cells with significant capacity
mismatches—for instance, during fast charging of an idle vehicle. In such cases, bidi-
rectional converters can be adopted to enable both LV bus load sharing and active charge
redistribution among cells.

   Moreover, the control objective of the system can be adapted to account for estimated
cell SOC and SOH, allowing more advanced balancing strategies. The bypass converters
can be implemented using various isolated DC–DC topologies. For example, the system
shown in Fig. 2.16 can employ isolated Dual Active Bridge (DAB) converters, controlled
via phase-shift modulation, or alternatively, bidirectional Flyback Converter (FBC) con-
figurations.

   This balancing method offers high efficiency and fast balancing speed. However, it
suffers from low power density and high implementation costs due to the various trans-
formers required.




                                            95
      Figure 2.16: Isolated bypass dual-active bridge DC-DC converter balancing system



2.10      Summary and Research Gaps
This chapter has investigated multilevel voltage synthesis techniques and battery balanc-
ing strategies. The motivation behind adopting multilevel conversion has been thoroughly
discussed, establishing the need for high-quality output voltage and current waveforms
with negligible THD and EMI filtering requirements. Furthermore, considerations related
to switching losses and their dependence on terminal voltages and switching frequencies
have been examined.

   Accordingly, the necessity for converter architectures with optimised switching and
voltage-blocking characteristics for each device has been emphasised. Such optimisation
aims to enhance overall power conversion efficiency and minimise power losses. Vari-
ous converter topologies have been reviewed: some operate with isolated DC sources,
configuring these sources according to switching states by inserting or bypassing certain
sources based on the required output voltage magnitude. Others rely on series-connected
DC sources, synthesising the output by selecting appropriate voltage taps and connecting
them to the output terminals through a set of switches.

   Key design features have been discussed, including switch ratings, switching speeds,
switch types (e.g., bidirectional/unidirectional), topology flexibility in terms of redundant
states for improved fault tolerance, and the capability for equalised load sharing among
DC sources to ensure uniform energy extraction.

   In addition, battery balancing circuits have been examined with respect to their main
balancing element, energy transfer mode, component count, implementation cost, and
balancing speed.

                                             96
Based on this critical review, the following conclusions can be drawn.

On Multilevel Converters
Several issues and challenges persist in existing MLI topologies, particularly when scaled
to a large number of levels (e.g., 128):

    • NPC converter: Requires an excessive number of clamping diodes as the number
      of output levels increases, rendering it impractical for configurations exceeding
      three or four levels.
    • FCC converter: Necessitates maintaining intermediate capacitor voltages near the
      DC bus voltage for higher-level operation, leading to a large number of capacitors
      rated at or near the DC source voltage.
    • CHB converter: Presents challenges in driving high-side switches that require
      floating power supplies—these are typically bulky, costly, and unsuitable for on-
      chip integration. Additionally, each H-bridge contributes two switches to the con-
      duction path, resulting in increased conduction losses as the number of levels rises.
      The number of control signals also scales directly with the number of switches.
    • MMC: Shares similar challenges to the CHB, with additional concerns regarding
      the size and cost of buffer inductors, as well as the complexity and computational
      burden of current and voltage sensing as well as the inaccuracy of SOC estimation
      for control algorithms.
    • Reduced switch-count topologies: Common drawbacks include loss of modular-
      ity and scalability, inconsistent relationships between switch blocking voltages and
      switching frequencies, reliance on costly bidirectional switches, and limitations in
      operating polarity generation at line frequency.

   To address these challenges, this thesis proposes a novel multilevel converter architec-
ture capable of scaling to a large number of levels whilst maintaining manageable imple-
mentation cost and design complexity. The proposed binary-tree multilevel architecture
features a simple control strategy with modest computational requirements. It strategi-
cally distributes switching frequencies and voltage stresses across devices: high-voltage
switches operate at low switching frequencies to minimise both dynamic and conduction
losses (facilitated by paralleling multiple devices), whilst low-voltage switches operate
at progressively higher frequencies, exploiting their superior dynamic performance.

   The architecture further optimises off-chip and on-chip integration domains. High-

                                            97
speed, low-voltage switches are integrated on-chip using lateral, high-performance semi-
conductor devices, whereas high-voltage, low-frequency switches are implemented off-
chip and optimised for low conduction losses. This hierarchical approach achieves high
power density and low switching and conduction losses whilst maintaining scalability to
a large number of voltage levels.


On Battery Balancers

For systems with a large number of cells, discrete balancer implementations based on
magnetic or converter-based energy transfer are often unsuitable due to high cost, bulky
design, complex control requirements, low power density, and limited integration poten-
tial with smart BMS features.

   Discretely implemented switched-capacitor balancing networks are also impractical
for the following reasons:

   1. Parasitic resistance (Rpar ) directly limits the achievable balancing current at high
      switching speeds.
   2. High voltage ratings of capacitors necessitate operation at very high frequencies to
      achieve practical capacitance values and maintain reliability.
   3. Parasitic inductance (Lpar ) constrains the switching frequency, hence reducing bal-
      ancing speed.
   4. The overall component count is excessive, requiring numerous switches, gate
      drivers, signal isolators, and floating driver supplies.
   5. Suitable discrete switches are unavailable for low-voltage operation (e.g., 2–4.3 V
      typical for Li-ion cells), leading to suboptimal designs with increased switching
      losses, higher leakage, and complex gate-drive requirements.
   6. Integration with BMS functionalities becomes challenging due to the lack of com-
      pact and modular design options.

   To overcome these limitations and realise compact, decentralised, and highly inte-
grable balancing circuits, this thesis proposes an on-chip design approach for battery bal-
ancers. The proposed circuits integrate a large number of integrated switches to enhance
balancing effectiveness, improve energy transfer performance, and facilitate integration
with smart BMS functionalities.


In summary, the insights gained from this literature review have guided the develop-

                                             98
ment of the proposed converter and balancer architectures, both of which are designed
to address the scalability, efficiency, and integration challenges identified in conventional
designs.




                                             99
Chapter 3


Semiconductor Devices and Circuit
Building Blocks

This chapter provides an overview of key semiconductor device technologies and their
integration into advanced converter architectures. It first examines the characteristics of
vertical power devices and lateral on-chip power devices with high-voltage capability.
Subsequently, it explores essential circuit building blocks for multilevel converter inte-
gration, including switch stacking techniques for enhanced voltage blocking capability,
voltage level shifters, dead-time generation circuits, and driver-rail generation methods.
The chapter concludes with a discussion of packaging technologies and chip bonding
techniques that facilitate reliable electrical, thermal, and mechanical integration with ex-
ternal substrates.


3.1     Discrete Switch Technologies
Power semiconductor devices serve as the essential building blocks of power conversion
systems. These components are engineered to optimise both conduction and switching
performance, enabling improved power conversion efficiency and higher power density.
For more than fifty years, silicon (Si) has been the dominant material in power semi-
conductor technology, benefiting from a highly mature manufacturing process, excellent
reliability, and cost-effectiveness [53].
Initially, the Bipolar Junction Transistor (BJT) was employed as a primary power switch-
ing device. However, by the mid-1970s, the power MOSFET emerged, offering enhanced
switching performance. In 1985, the IGBT was introduced, combining the high current
and voltage handling capability of bipolar devices with the insulated gate structure of the
MOSFET. Continuous advancements in design and material technology have sustained
improvements in IGBT performance, contributing to its strong market presence. Later,
in 1998, the super-junction MOSFET was commercialised, featuring significantly faster
switching speeds and reduced on-state resistance at high breakdown voltages compared
with conventional power MOSFETs [49].
The growing need for superior power conversion efficiency and increased power density
has driven the exploration of alternative semiconductor materials such as SiC, GaN, and
Gallium Arsenide (GaAs), collectively known as WBG materials [53]. The key charac-
teristics of these materials will be discussed based on Table 3.1 [112].
A commonly used figure of merit (FOM) that reflects both conduction and switching
losses in power transistors is the product of the on-state resistance Rds,on and the total
gate charge Qgate required to activate the device. Typically, WBG-based devices such
as SiC and GaN exhibit a lower FOM compared to conventional silicon counterparts,
enabling higher switching speeds, reduced losses, and improved power handling capa-
bilities.




3.1.1       Standard and Superjunction Silicon MOSFETs

Figure 3.1(a) illustrates the cross-sectional structure of a conventional silicon power
MOSFET. The device features a planar configuration that supports vertical current con-
duction, with the source and gate terminals positioned on the upper surface of the semi-
conductor substrate, and the drain terminal located at the bottom. Vertical current flow is
a typical characteristic of discrete power devices, as it allows current to traverse a large
cross-sectional area. This results in lower on-state resistance and enhances both electri-
cal and thermal contact with the package. Unlike lateral devices, where current spreads
across the surface, vertical MOSFETs distribute power dissipation throughout the vol-
ume of the semiconductor. The structure shown in Fig. 3.1(a) is also known as a vertical
Double-Diffused Metal Oxide Semiconductor (DMOS). The term refers to the manufac-
turing process sequence in which the P-type body region is diffused first, followed by the
diffusion of the highly doped N+ source layer.

    The device’s voltage-blocking capability is primarily governed by the thickness of
the lightly doped N− drift region. However, as the drift region becomes thicker to sup-
port higher voltages, the on-resistance also increases. This inherent trade-off is a key

                                            101
limitation in conventional power MOSFET design.

   The development of the superjunction MOSFET, shown in Fig. 3.1(b), overcame this
inverse relationship between on-resistance and breakdown voltage in traditional silicon
power devices. This improvement is achieved by introducing alternating P- and N-doped
columns (or trenches) within the drift region. The P-doped regions generate a charge-
compensated depletion zone, known as the superjunction, that balances the N-type dop-
ing concentration. This design modifies the electric field distribution from a triangular
to a nearly rectangular profile, which enhances the breakdown voltage whilst allowing
higher doping levels in the N-region. As a result, superjunction devices combine low
on-resistance with high-voltage capability [49, 53, 113].



3.1.2    IGBT Switch

The IGBT is designed to handle very high current levels whilst maintaining low on-
state resistance. Its internal structure, shown in Fig. 3.1(c), closely resembles that of the
DMOS. The key distinction lies in the inclusion of a P-type layer at the drain terminal,
which forms the emitter of an embedded PNP bipolar transistor. Conceptually, the IGBT
can be represented as a hybrid device combining the operating principles of both a BJT
and a MOSFET.

   Functionally, the IGBT behaves like a MOSFET connected in series with a diode that
models the emitter–base junction of the internal PNP transistor. During conduction, this
diode introduces a forward voltage drop, causing the IGBT to exhibit a larger voltage drop
at low collector currents. Consequently, conduction losses are typically higher than those
of a power MOSFET, which lacks a forward-biased PN junction. Because of this series
diode, current conduction in an IGBT is unidirectional, and an external freewheeling
diode is often added to provide a path for reverse current during inductive switching
events. In the on-state, the equivalent circuit can be viewed as an on-resistance (similar
to that of a MOSFET) in series with a forward-biased junction. Although IGBTs switch
more slowly than MOSFETs, they are highly efficient and reliable for medium- to high-
voltage applications.

   The device’s turn-off dynamics differ substantially from those of MOSFETs, as they
are governed by the recombination of minority carriers, specifically holes, within the N-
type drift region. When the gate–emitter voltage is reduced to zero or driven negative,

                                            102
Figure 3.1: Cross-sectional structures of power transistors: (a) standard planar silicon power
            MOSFET; (b) superjunction MOSFET; (c) non-punch-through IGBT; and (d) punch-
            through IGBT.




                                             103
the gate capacitance discharges, and the channel current is interrupted. The remaining
current then decays gradually as minority carriers recombine, a process that may take
several microseconds when carrier lifetimes are long. This slow recombination leads to a
delayed turn-off response and higher switching losses, limiting the maximum operating
frequency of IGBTs.

   To mitigate these effects, modern IGBT designs incorporate a thinner drift region
and an additional N-type field-stop layer adjacent to the P-type collector, as illustrated in
Fig. 3.1(d). This field-stop structure modifies the electric field profile from a triangular
to a trapezoidal shape, enhancing breakdown voltage whilst enabling thinner overall chip
dimensions. As a result, lower on-state resistance can be achieved without compromis-
ing voltage-blocking capability. Furthermore, this configuration minimises stored charge
in the drift region and accelerates charge removal during turn-off, thereby improving
switching speed and efficiency [113, 114, 49].



3.1.3    WBG Technologies

WBG semiconductor devices, composed of compounds formed from elements belong-
ing to different groups of the periodic table, exhibit material properties that are highly
advantageous for high-power and high-frequency applications. Representative examples
include GaN, SiC, GaAs, and Indium phosphide (InP). Among these, GaN and SiC are
of particular relevance to modern power conversion systems.

   Table 3.1 highlights the key material properties of silicon and GaN and SiC. The
significantly larger band-gap energies and breakdown electric fields of SiC and GaN en-
able devices with higher blocking voltages, lower conduction losses, and superior high-
temperature capability compared with silicon. In addition, the higher thermal conduc-
tivity of SiC supports improved heat removal, whereas the higher electron mobility of
GaN facilitates high-frequency switching. These combined attributes justify the growing
adoption of WBG devices in high-performance automotive and industrial power convert-
ers [112, 17, 115, 116].

   Modern enhancement-mode (e-mode) GaN devices are typically implemented as lat-
eral structures, as illustrated in Fig. 3.2. To reduce fabrication cost and leverage es-
tablished semiconductor processes, GaN layers are often grown on silicon substrates,
a configuration commonly referred to as GaN-on-Si [49]. In these devices, a hetero-

                                            104
junction is formed between GaN and Aluminium Gallium Nitride (AlGaN) layers. At
the GaN/AlGaN interface, electrons accumulate to form a Two-Dimensional Electron
Gas (2DEG) with high carrier mobility and density, resulting in very low specific on-
resistance.

   The gate structure is realised by locally thinning or removing the AlGaN barrier
and depositing a new, thinner layer. The inherent GaN/AlGaN interface produces a
normally-on (depletion-mode) device, whereas most power applications prefer normally-
off (enhancement-mode) behaviour. A common method to achieve e-mode operation
involves growing a p-doped GaN (P-GaN) or P-AlGaN layer above the barrier, which
depletes the 2DEG. Applying a positive gate-source voltage repopulates the channel and
turns the device on [49].

   Compared with silicon MOSFETs and SiC devices, GaN transistors have a low
threshold voltage, typically below 2 V, with the target on-resistance reached around
VGS = 5,V. The narrow gate voltage margin makes gate driving particularly critical, es-
pecially at high switching speeds where parasitic effects become significant [49]. The
superior performance of GaN devices stems from their high critical electric field, which
allows for a thinner drift region and therefore a lower area-specific on-resistance. How-
ever, the 2DEG channel exhibits a relatively large positive temperature coefficient of
approximately 9000 ppm/K, leading to stronger parameter variations with temperature
compared with silicon-based technologies [49]. A notable advantage of GaN transistors
in power conversion is the absence of intrinsic p–n junctions. Without a body diode, GaN
devices inherently eliminate reverse-recovery losses, since no depletion charge must be
removed during switching transitions [49].

   SiC MOSFETs, by contrast, are vertical unipolar devices that enable high-voltage
operation and efficient heat dissipation from the chip to the heat sink. Unlike GaN High-
Electron-Mobility Transistors (HEMTs), SiC MOSFETs contain no interfacial layers,
which simplifies their structure and improves thermal performance. Their normally-
off configuration, voltage-controlled gate, and integrated body diode with low reverse-
recovery losses have made SiC MOSFETs highly attractive for commercial power con-
version systems. Devices with rated voltages from 600 V to 15 kV have been demon-
strated, whereas conventional silicon MOSFETs are typically limited to about 300 V due
to their higher on-state resistance [53].


                                            105
              Table 3.1: Pure silicon and compound semiconductor properties


  Material                                      Unit         Si        SiC     GaN
  Band-gap (Eg )                                eV          1.12       3.2      3.4
  Electron mobility (µe )               cm2 /(V · s)       1350       1140     1250
  Hole mobility (µh )                   cm2 /(V · s)        480         50      850
  Thermal conductivity (κ)              W/(cm · K)           1.5       3.7      1.3
  Breakdown field (Ecrit )                MV/cm              0.3       3.5      2.0
  Power density                           W/mm2              1.5           4   4–12

   The adoption of WBGs devices in Electric Vehicles (EVs) power electronic convert-
ers provides several system-level advantages, including reduced power losses, higher
switching frequencies, and higher temperature operation. These benefits contribute di-
rectly to increased efficiency, extended driving range, reduced cooling requirements, and
improved system reliability [16, 17].

   Despite these significant advantages, several challenges currently limit the full ex-
ploitation of WBG technologies. The most critical issues can be summarised as fol-
lows [117, 118, 119]:

    • comprehensive device characterisation and modelling,
    • advanced device and module packaging,
    • minimisation and control of parasitic inductances,
    • robust and fast gate-driver design, and
    • mitigation of EMI, cross-talk, and high-frequency harmonic effects.


3.2     Integrated Circuit Technologies
Many IC technologies include high-voltage MOS transistors designed specifically for
power applications. In contrast to discrete power devices, these transistors are imple-
mented in a lateral configuration, allowing high integration alongside analogue, digital,
and mixed-signal components within the same chip. Their terminals are accessible di-
rectly from the IC surface, simplifying interconnection and fabrication.

   One of the main advantages of on-chip power integration is the flexibility in device
sizing. Designers can adjust transistor widths individually to optimise the balance be-

                                           106
                 Source            Gate                             Drain
                                   P-GaN
                                                      AlGaN
                                           2DEG

                                       GaN Buffer

                                       P-Substrate


                            Figure 3.2: GaN-on-silicon structure



tween on-resistance and parasitic capacitances, achieving efficient trade-offs between
conduction and switching performance.

   Integrating high-voltage power transistors with control circuitry enables the realisa-
tion of complete power systems on a single chip, where protection, monitoring, and con-
trol features can be embedded into one IC. This high level of integration offers benefits
in terms of cost efficiency, compactness, and system reliability.

   Common high-voltage devices available in standard IC processes include the Drain-
Extended MOSFET (DEMOS) and the Lateral Diffused MOSFET (LDMOS) transistors.


3.2.1    DEMOS and LDMOS Switches

The DEMOS transistor employs a lightly doped drain extension to increase breakdown
voltage by reducing the electric field beneath the gate near the drain. This structure is
achieved using an extended N-well region without adding process complexity. DEMOS
transistors exhibit threshold voltages similar to standard CMOS devices. However, their
relatively high on-resistance limits their use to medium-voltage applications rather than
true power switching [120].

   The LDMOS transistor is the standard power device in integrated power technolo-
gies. It achieves lower on-resistance through a more complex process involving double
diffusion of the N+ source and P-type body. The separation between source and drain
defines the drift region, which allows high drain–source voltages.

   Figure 3.3(a) shows the LDMOS structure, where current flows laterally across the
surface, enabling co-integration with other control functions. To support high-voltage

                                            107
operation, where the source–body terminal of the device is biased above the substrate
potential, a buried N-layer (NBL) is employed as a junction-isolation technique. The N-
Buried Layer (NBL) electrically separates the device from the substrate and thus enables
high-side switch operation in LDMOS technologies. As the source terminal is isolated,
it can swing below ground potential, whilst the drain must remain positive to maintain
reverse bias with respect to the P-substrate. A few parasitic diodes are thus inherent to the
LDMOS structure: a body diode between drain and source, an isolation diode between
NBL and substrate, and a diode between the body-source of the device and the NBL.

   Although the NBL approach offers a cost-effective isolation solution, it necessitates
careful management of bipolar parasitic effects (discussed later in this chapter) and in-
creases the required layout area of the switch. Alternative isolation techniques developed
to enhance performance and mitigate these limitations use DTI and SOI technologies;
these are described next.

   To achieve lower on-resistance, multiple unit transistors, referred to as fingers, are
connected in parallel. Each unit corresponds to a single LDMOS finger, similar to the
cross section shown in Fig. 3.3(b). The symmetrical geometry of each finger allows
adjacent drain regions to overlap, enabling a compact and highly regular transistor layout.
The channel length, however, is predefined by the technology for a given breakdown
voltage and cannot be altered through circuit design [49, 114, 120, 121, 122, 123].



3.2.2    SOI Technology

Instead of using an NBL isolation, BCD technologies can be implemented on an SOI
substrate, as illustrated in Fig. 3.3(b) [122]. In this case, DTI structures are also employed
to isolate the field regions between devices.

   SOI technology offers substantial flexibility for series stacking of devices, since it
improves breakdown capability and supports floating source/body operation. Addition-
ally, the high-resistivity SOI substrate reduces substrate losses, and the overall switch
area is smaller than in bulk BCD processes because no bulky rings are required for isola-
tion. SOI processes are inherently immune to latch-up due to the suppression of parasitic
bipolar conduction paths, making them well suited for high-voltage and highly integrated
circuits. Another key advantage is the ability to support negative (below-ground) supply
voltages and to maintain robust immunity against negative transients [120].

                                             108
Figure 3.3: Cross-sectional views of (a) an LDMOS device with junction isolation and (b) a
            power transistor implemented in an SOI technology, isolated by DTI and a BOX
            layer.


   However, the insulated substrate of SOI technologies leads to higher fabrication costs.
Furthermore, the BOX layer exhibits a higher thermal resistance than silicon, which can
degrade heat dissipation. As a result, SOI-based devices may experience elevated junc-
tion temperatures during high-power operation due to both dynamic and static power
losses [124, 125, 126].


3.2.3    Parasitic Structures and their Effects

Figure 3.4 illustrates the cross section of a high-voltage Complementary Metal-Oxide-
Semiconductor (CMOS) inverter, which inherently contains two parasitic bipolar tran-
sistors, one PNP and one NPN. Together, these form an unintended silicon-controlled
rectifier (SCR) structure. Because the P- and N-regions of CMOS transistors possess fi-
nite resistances, a parasitic current can forward-bias the base–emitter junctions of these
transistors. When the voltage drop across either region exceeds approximately 0.7 V, both
parasitic transistors may enter conduction. The latch-up condition occurs when the prod-
uct of their current gains creates a regenerative feedback loop that sustains conduction.

                                           109
                Figure 3.4: Cascode cross-section with parasitic bipolar devices




   Latch-up can be triggered by several factors, including leakage paths, capacitive cou-
pling, transient voltage spikes, electrostatic discharge, electromagnetic interference, or
radiation-induced charge injection. Once initiated, the parasitic SCR can conduct a large
current between the power rails. For example, if the parasitic PNP draws collector cur-
rent from VDD through the N-well resistance Rn , a voltage drop exceeding 0.7 V activates
the device. The resulting current reinforces the voltage across the P-well resistance Rp ,
keeping the NPN transistor active even after the initial disturbance is removed. This
self-sustaining condition leads to excessive current flow, localised heating, and potential
device failure. The only way to stop the latch-up is by disconnecting the power supply.

   To ensure reliability, devices are tested for latch-up susceptibility using standardised
procedures such as JEDEC JESD78, where controlled current pulses are applied to each
I/O pin whilst monitoring supply current for abnormal increases.

   Latch-up prevention relies on careful layout and process design. Key design practices
include [49]:

    • Ensuring low-resistance connections to the substrate and wells through frequent
      contacts, providing short carrier return paths.
    • Surrounding sensitive N- and P-wells with guard rings tied to appropriate bias po-
      tentials to collect injected carriers.
    • Increasing the spacing between wells to widen the effective transistor base region,
      hence reducing current gain.
    • Introducing highly doped buried layers (e.g., N+ ) to suppress parasitic PNP gain
      and lower the likelihood of triggering.

                                               110
3.3     Circuit Techniques for Higher-Voltage and Floating
        Operation




As discussed in the previous sections, multilevel power converters are capable of oper-
ating with series-connected or isolated DC sources, which can be inserted or bypassed
depending on the desired output voltage level. The implementation of these converter
topologies introduces several challenges in terms of integration, particularly regarding
how the control and power stages can be realised using highly integrable circuit design
techniques.

   The key challenges in converter circuit design can be categorised as follows:

   1. Power Stage: The primary challenge lies in the realisation of high-voltage
      switches. This can be achieved either by stacking multiple low-voltage devices
      in series or by employing a single device with sufficient drain-to-source voltage
      blocking capability. Each approach presents trade-offs in terms of performance,
      reliability, and complexity of the gate-driving scheme.
   2. Control and Driving Stage: The objective here is to ensure precise control of the
      turn-on and turn-off transitions of the power switches within the converter topol-
      ogy. In multilevel converters, the large number of switches, many of which operate
      under floating conditions, complicates the driving mechanism. Specifically, the
      source terminals of these switches are not referenced to a fixed potential, but rather
      vary dynamically during operation. Consequently, floating power supplies are re-
      quired to provide adequate gate-drive voltages. Numerous floating-supply circuits
      have been proposed in the literature to address this issue, and these will be dis-
      cussed in detail in this section.
      Furthermore, an additional challenge arises in the isolation of control signals. Since
      the PWM control signals are generated within the logic voltage domain, referenced
      to a fixed DC supply and a common logic ground, these signals must be appropri-
      ately level-shifted to the voltage domains corresponding to the operating switches.
      Therefore, effective voltage level-shifting and isolation techniques are essential to
      ensure reliable and safe control of the converter.

                                           111
3.3.1    Switch Stacking Techniques

Figure 3.5 illustrates various techniques used to extend the operating voltage range of
power switches to approximately 2VDD –3VDD , where VDD denotes the maximum voltage
that can be sustained by a single device within the stack.

   In switch stacking configurations, one of the main challenges is ensuring reliable
voltage distribution across the series-connected devices so that none of them exceeds its
breakdown limit. To minimize global and local mismatches that could lead to voltage
overstress, all stacked transistors should be relatively large with identical dimensions and
characteristics.

   To double the blocking voltage, two switches are typically connected in series, as
shown in Fig. 3.5(a). A single driver supply, often derived from a charge pump, can be
used to control both devices. During the turn-on process, the lower switch, denoted as
M1 , is activated first, which pulls VD1 down to VSSH . At this point, one drive voltage
(VDDH with respect to VSSH ) is applied across M2 , turning it on. Consequently, the gate-
to-source voltage for both M1 and M2 equals the drive voltage VDD once both devices are
fully turned on.

   During turn-off, the driver first disables M1 , causing VD1 to rise towards VDDH −Vth ,
where Vth is the threshold voltage, which in turn drives M2 into cutoff. At the begin-
ning of this transition, the drain-to-source voltages can be expressed as VDS1 = VDD −Vth
and VDS2 = VDD + Vth . Due to subthreshold conduction effects, these voltages tend to
equalize as the drain of M1 gradually approaches VDDH , provided the switching period is
sufficiently long.
