   Overall, the switch-stacking technique enables low-voltage devices to collectively
achieve a blocking capability twice that of a single device. According to [127], this ap-
proach can reduce switching losses by a factor of 2–3.6, whilst also offering a significant
area advantage.

   The circuit shown in Fig. 3.5(b) is commonly employed in half-bridge configurations
of buck converters. It incorporates an additional static bias rail, denoted as Vb1 , together
with a level shifter and a separate gate driver for the third transistor M3 . Devices M1
and M2 operate in the same manner as described previously. The transistor M3 is driven
through its own level-shifting and driver circuitry, powered by two voltage rails VDDH =
VDD and Vb1 = 2VDD . The additional bias rail Vb1 is typically provided from an external

                                            112
source [128, 127].

    The configuration presented in Fig. 3.5(c) removes the need for the extra supply rail,
level shifter, and driver required in Fig. 3.5(b). Instead, it employs two resistive divider
networks and two auxiliary transistors to generate the required bias for M3 . Specifically,
the gate bias of M3 is established using transistors M4 and M5 , together with resistor pairs
(R1 , R2 ) and (R3 , R4 ). In the off -state, the gate voltage of M3 is determined by the divider
(R1 , R2 ), which is enabled by M5 operating in the triode region, whilst M4 remains cut
off as defined by (R3 , R4 ). During the on-state, M5 disables the (R1 , R2 ) divider, and the
gate of M3 is then biased through M4 using the fixed bias voltage from M2 . However,
this design introduces continuous static current through the resistor networks, and its
performance depends on the absolute blocking voltage of the transistor stack, both of
which limit its efficiency and applicability [129, 127].

    In Fig. 3.5(d), a bootstrap capacitor and a diode are introduced to provide the nec-
essary gate bias for M3 during the off -state. The bootstrap capacitor is charged when
the stack is conducting, ensuring that each device maintains a gate-to-source voltage of
VDDH − VSSH = VDD . The capacitor thus stores a voltage of approximately VDD , which
is subsequently used in the off -state to balance the drain potential of M2 , ensuring sym-
metrical VGS and VDS across all stacked devices. The bootstrap capacitor Cbs is typically
designed to be 5–10 times larger than the gate capacitance of M3 [49, 127]. Neglecting
the forward voltage drop of diode D1 , the gate of M3 alternates automatically between
VSSH + 2VDD and VSSH + VDD . This approach can be extended to a four-device stack by
incorporating an additional diode–capacitor pair [127]. To improve voltage equalization
during switching transients, shunt resistors can be placed across each switch, as illus-
trated in Fig. 3.5(f) (proposal).

    The configuration shown in Fig. 3.5(e) has similar implementation cost to that of
Fig. 3.5(d), but operates differently. In the off -state, shunt resistors are used to achieve
uniform voltage distribution across the drain–source terminals of all devices in the stack.
The voltage developed across R1 and R2 is then employed to charge the capacitor through
the diode, which subsequently provides the necessary bias to turn on M3 in the on
state [130, 131].

    For such high-voltage stacked configurations, the devices are typically implemented
within deep n-well or NBL regions, or ideally on SOI substrates, otherwise, excessive


                                              113
 Figure 3.5: Series stacking of MOSFETs to increase drain-source voltage blocking capability



voltage stress appears across their drain-to-body junctions if that body is connected to
the lowest potential as in standard CMOS technologies. Therefore, the body of each
transistor should be connected to its respective source terminal to ensure proper isolation
and safe operating conditions.

   In general, stacked-circuit topologies that rely on bootstrap capacitors become im-
practical for low-speed operation or circuits without periodic switching. Moreover,
switch stacking may introduce additional design sensitivities. The power stage becomes
more susceptible to noise coupling, which can induce transient glitches and degrade
switching performance. There remains a risk of exceeding device voltage ratings, partic-
ularly during start-up, necessitating careful design to guarantee safe and reliable opera-
tion [49].


3.3.2    Voltage Level-Shifting Techniques

Voltage level shifters act as interfaces between the control signal logic domain and
higher-voltage domains. The main performance metrics for level shifter circuits include
propagation delay (speed), static and dynamic power consumption, voltage compliance,
dVSSH /dt immunity, and overall circuit layout area.

   A variety of level shifter architectures have been reported in the literature. These are

                                            114
Figure 3.6: Voltage level shifter circuits, (a) low-voltage cross-coupled level-shifter, (b) HV
            cross-coupled level shifter with floating VSSH , (c) clamped version of (b), and (d)
            pulse-triggered high-voltage floating level shifter



generally categorized into active-coupled level shifters (AC-LS) and capacitive-coupled
level shifters (CC-LS) [132, 133, 134, 103, 135, 136, 137, 138, 139, 140]. Figure 3.6 illus-
trates several AC-LS circuit implementations, encompassing both low-voltage and high-
voltage variants, which are applicable to gate-driver control-signal interfacing circuits. In
the low-voltage configuration shown in Fig. 3.6(a), the circuit employs a cross-coupled
topology with complementary pull-up and pull-down networks to toggle between logic
states. Consider the case where Vd1 is high. In this state, the gates of transistors M1
and Mp2 are both at a high potential. For successful state transition, M1 must be sized
relative to Mp2 such that, when the gate of M1 is driven to VDDL and the gate of M3 re-
mains at VSSL , voltage Vd1 can be pulled at least |Vtp | below VDDH . Otherwise, the latch
cannot switch efficiently. By symmetry, Mp1 and M2 are dimensioned identically. Under
these operating conditions, M1 operates in the active region (assuming similar threshold

                                             115
voltages for both P- and N-channel devices and VDDL < VDDH ), whilst Mp2 operates in
the triode region. To ensure good yield, the NMOS-to-PMOS ratio is determined for the
slow-N/fast-P process corner with maximum VDDH and minimum VDDL , as described in
[138].

   For floating high-side gate-driver applications, the circuit in Fig. 3.6(b) is typically
adopted. Here, the pull-down devices are N-type DMOS high-voltage transistors, each
placed within an isolated P-well and separated from the substrate by an N-well isolation
layer [138].

   In this high-voltage (HV) configuration, the ratio between the pull-down DMOS tran-
sistors (M3 /M4 ) and the pull-up PMOS devices must be carefully optimised. This can be
understood by considering the case where node Vs3 is pulled by Mp2 to VDDH . When M1
turns on, pulling Vd1 toward VSSL , the voltage Vs3 tends to follow Vd1 through M3 . As Vs3
drops, however, M3 becomes weaker, making the strength ratio between the pull-down
and pull-up devices a critical design parameter, expressed as follows [138]:

                           W
                             
                           L pd     Kp′ (VDD − |Vtp |) |Vtp |

                               = 2   ′ (V                      2
                                                                  ,                   (3.1)

   where VDD = VDDH − VSSH , Vtp and Vtpd are the threshold voltages of the pull-up
(PMOS) and cascode (PDMOS) devices, respectively. K ′ = µCox , with the subscript
indicating the device type. From the denominator of (3.1), it follows that the floating
voltage domain (VDDH − VSSH ) must exceed (|Vtp | + |Vtpd |). Otherwise, M3 enters the
subthreshold region during pull-down, resulting in an excessively large device ratio. For
improved production yield, sizing is typically performed under the slow-P process corner
[138].

   The NDMOS transistor M1 is also scaled relative to Mp2 according to:

where Vtn and |Vtp | denote the threshold voltages of the N-channel and P-channel transis-

   An enhancement to this topology involves adding two transistors that actively pull
down the source voltages of the PDMOS devices to VSSH , as shown in Fig. 3.6(c). In


                                            116
this configuration, M5 /Mp2 and M6 /Mp1 form a pair of latching inverters, whilst the
PDMOS transistors M3 /M4 are responsible for toggling the latched state. Since the
PDMOS devices cannot initially pull their source voltages all the way down to VSSH ,
the auxiliary transistors must be designed to be significantly weaker than the PMOS
pull-up devices to allow the latch to switch states reliably. This consideration becomes
particularly important under the strong-N/weak-P process corner [138].

   Another configuration of a floating-voltage level shifter is illustrated in Fig. 3.6(d)
[133]. The circuit begins with a pulse-generation stage that responds to transitions of the
input signal Vin . For each input transition, only one of the two signal paths is activated,
producing a short pulse either at Vin1 or Vin2 . During the rising edge of Vin , the node
Vin1 momentarily goes high, which turns on transistor M1 . At this instant, Mp3 mirrors
the current through Mp4 , thereby pulling up node N2 . As the potential at N1 surpasses
the trigger voltage of the latch formed by I3 and I4 , node N1 is driven to VSSH . The
positive feedback inherent to the latch further accelerates the voltage rise of N2 toward
VDDH . Once this transition completes, the latch maintains the output states at N1 and N2 ,
ensuring that Vout1 remains held at VDDH even after M1 turns off at the end of the Vin1
pulse. Consequently, a rising edge on Vin sets the latch such that N2 is fixed at VDDH and
N1 at VSSH .

   To toggle the latch state, a falling edge is applied to the input signal. This transition
produces a pulse at node Vin2 , initiating a complementary sequence through M2 , Mp1 ,
and Mp2 . As a result, N1 is pulled up to VDDH , whilst Vout1 is driven down to VSSH , thus
completing the switching operation [133].

   This level-shifter configuration achieves a better compromise among speed, power
dissipation, and layout area compared to high-voltage cross-coupled level shifters. How-
ever, when considered for implementation in power conversion applications, several as-
pects still present opportunities for further optimisation. One limitation is the asymmetry
between rising and falling propagation delays, which stems from the fact that the rising-
edge signal follows a different conduction path than the falling-edge signal. Another
issue is the limited immunity to dVSSH /dt slewing. According to [133], post-layout sim-
ulation results demonstrate that rising-edge transitions fail to propagate reliably when
the VSSH slew rate exceeds approximately 15 V/ns. A final issue is related to poten-
tial mismatches in the current sources that could lead to higher power dissipation during


                                            117
transitions. To address these shortcomings, [133] introduces an improved level-shifter
design in which propagation-delay symmetry is enhanced and VSSH slew-rate immunity
is increased through the addition of N-type current mirrors. However, this leads to more
design complexity and layout size, especially if the circuit has to be used extensively
in the chip. Further advancements on this level-shifter design have been proposed in
[141], targeting operation at significantly higher slew rates (exceeding 100 V/ns) whilst
maintaining low propagation delays and accommodating wider negative voltage toler-
ance levels (down to approximately −1.5 V). These enhancements, however, come at the
expense of increased circuit complexity and a larger silicon footprint. Consequently, such
designs are well suited for high-speed and high-performance switching devices, includ-
ing GaN and SiC technologies, but are less practical for large-scale integration across
multiple half-bridge modules as in multilevel converter circuits. The level-shifter circuit
shown in Fig. 3.7(a) comprises input-controlled current mirrors operating within the 5 V
domain and an output-stage inverter (I1 ) that functions between VDDH and VSSH [142]. A
digital input signal Vin , originating from the low-voltage domain bounded by VSSL and
VDDL , is translated to the higher-voltage domain between VSSH and VDDH , producing the
output Vout1 . The input to the inverter I1 is node VH , whose voltage is determined by the
drop across resistor RH .

    When Vin is at a logic-high level, transistor MIN turns ON, allowing the reference
current Iref to flow through the current mirrors MP1 , MP2 and MN1 , MN2 . This current
generates a voltage drop of (Iref · RH ) across RH , resulting in a logic-low voltage at node
VH , which in turn drives the inverter output Vout1 to VDDH . Conversely, when Vin is low, no
current flows through RH , and node VH is pulled up to VDDH , causing the inverter output
Vout1 to switch to logic low at VSSH .

    The current Iref is a trimmed reference, and the values of Iref and RH are selected to en-
sure sufficient switching margin for the inverter across process, voltage, and temperature
corners. Although the current mirrors operate in the low-voltage domain, a high-voltage
NMOS transistor MNH1 provides electrical isolation between node VH and the drain of
MN2 . Transistor MNH1 remains continuously ON, with its gate biased at the low-voltage
supply VDDL . For a 5 V VDDL domain, if VSSH is 28 V, then VDDH becomes 33 V, thereby
translating an input swing of 0–5 V into an output range of 28–33 V [142].

    For level shifting from the high-voltage domain to the low-voltage domain, the circuit


                                             118
Figure 3.7: (a) shift-up current-source based voltage level shifter, (b) shift-down version of (a),
            (c) HV capacitive-coupled level shifter with PMOS cross-coupled pair, (d) NMOS
            cross-coupled pair version of (c) with (VDDH − VDDL ) rail generator, and (e) Fully
            latched capacitive-coupled level shifter



illustrated in Fig. 3.7(b) is employed [142]. This configuration operates on the same
underlying principle as the circuit in Fig. 3.7(a), but performs signal translation in the
opposite direction, i.e., from the high-voltage side to the low-voltage side. In this design,
isolation between the high- and low-voltage domains is provided by a P-channel transistor
MPH1 , whose gate is tied to VSSH [142].

    A capacitive level-shifter circuit is illustrated in Fig. 3.7(c). It is implemented using a
cross-coupled PMOS pair (MP1 and MP2 ) and operates in conjunction with a complemen-
tary signal generator that produces two opposing control signals, Vin1 and Vin2 , within the
VDDL voltage domain. Two coupling capacitors are employed to transfer these control sig-
nals, which are used to drive the high-side PMOS transistor MHS . In this configuration,

                                               119
the level-shifter directly drives the gate of the high-side power device MHS . However,
due to charge sharing between the coupling capacitors (C1 /C2 ) and the input gate capac-
itance (Cp ) of the power switch, the voltage swing of the level-shifted signal is reduced.
To mitigate this effect and prevent degradation of the power switch on-resistance, large
off-chip coupling capacitors are typically required to minimize the loss in voltage swing.
Furthermore, directly charging and discharging the large gate capacitance of the power
transistor limits the achievable improvement in propagation delay [137, 143].

   To overcome these limitations, [137] proposes an alternative topology based on an
NMOS cross-coupled pair, as depicted in Fig. 3.7(d). This design generates a voltage
level of (VDDH −VDDL ) from the VDDH supply, allowing the inclusion of a compact buffer-
ing stage with a small input capacitance CB to interface the level-shifter output with the
gate of the power PMOS switch. This modification substantially reduces the required size
of the coupling capacitors. Additionally, the use of NMOS transistors instead of PMOS
devices (as in Fig. 3.7(c)) offers the benefit of intrinsic protection through the body diodes
of the NMOS pair. These diodes prevent the output nodes Vout1 and Vout2 from dropping
more than a diode’s forward voltage below (VDDH − VDDL ), ensuring that all transistors
operate safely within their voltage ratings without the need for supplementary protection
or startup circuits [137, 143].

   The capacitive level-shifter circuit shown in Fig. 3.7(e) employs two coupling ca-
pacitors, C1 and C2 , to transfer the control signal Vctrl from the low-side domain to the
high-side domain [144]. The input configuration resembles that of the capacitive level-
shifter circuits illustrated in Fig. 3.7(c)–(d). However, unlike the half-latch topology used
in those designs, this circuit adopts a full-latch structure implemented with the invert-
ers I3 and I4 on the high-voltage side. The coupling capacitors C1 and C2 propagate the
signals VC1 and VC2 to the inputs of I3 and I4 . When the input capacitance of I3 and I4
is small and their input/output node impedances are high, the output voltages Vout2 and
Vout1 closely track VC1 and VC2 . Once Vout2 or Vout1 reaches the switching threshold of I3
or I4 , the latch toggles its state through positive feedback. The inverter I5 then buffers the
signal to generate a strong logic-level output suitable for driving the gate driver (GD) of
the high-side power switch. [145, 49].

   The coupling capacitors C1 and C2 must withstand voltages up to VDDH , which corre-
sponds to the sum of the DC-link voltage VDC and the gate-drive voltage level VDRV . In


                                             120
low-power applications, the capacitance values of C1 and C2 can be reduced to approx-
imately 50 fF to minimize energy consumption during level shifting. However, smaller
capacitances increase the circuit’s susceptibility to noise [49].

   Although the complementary signal transfer mechanism enhances the robustness of
this design, reliable operation is not guaranteed if the output voltages Vout2 and Vout1
fail to cross the switching thresholds of I3 and I4 . Ensuring correct toggling behaviour
requires careful sizing and timing design, as analysed in detail by [145], where both
inverter stages were modelled independently, excluding transient dynamic effects.

3.3.3       Dead-Time Generation Circuits
For high-current switching operations involving pull-up and pull-down transitions, it is
essential to ensure that the existing conduction path is completely turned off before es-
tablishing a new one. Failure to do so can lead to shoot-through currents, where both
the high- and low-side switches conduct simultaneously. Such unintended current flow
can severely damage semiconductor devices and interconnect metal layers, reduce con-
version efficiency, and cause significant self-heating [146]. To prevent this, a suitable
dead-time (or non-overlapping time interval) is introduced between the switching tran-
sitions. This interval ensures that both switches remain off for a brief period, thereby
minimizing power dissipation, mitigating device stress, and preventing excessive thermal
rise. Figure 3.8 illustrates basic dead-time generator circuits that can be employed for
this purpose. For improved flexibility and optimised performance, the dead-time can be
made programmable—allowing it to be tuned according to the switching characteristics
or operating conditions. This programmability can be achieved by incorporating multi-
ple selectable delay intervals and using a multiplexer to choose the appropriate dead-time
duration.

   The circuit depicted in Fig. 3.8(a) is intended for half-bridge converters utilizing a
P-channel device as the high-side switch and an N-channel device as the low-side switch.
It produces two non-overlapping control signals with a defined dead-time using a combi-
nation of NAND and AND logic gates. The block labelled T0 functions as a pass-gate to
balance the propagation delays introduced by the inverter on the complementary branch.
The key operational concept relies on the fast transition of the AND gate output, which
switches to a low logic level whenever the input VIN AND is driven low. This behaviour
is illustrated in Fig. 3.8(c), where the input control signal is divided into two branches.

                                            121
Each branch experiences a propagation delay (Td1 and Td2 ) within the feedback loops,
creating distinct rising and falling edge delays. These delayed transitions establish the
desired dead-time intervals at both switching instants.

   Alternatively, the circuit shown in Fig. 3.8(b) is suitable for half-bridge configurations
employing dual N-channel switches. This topology also relies on NAND-based logic to
generate two complementary control signals with non-overlapping transitions. Its oper-
ation is governed by the principle that the NAND gate output transitions to a high logic
level whenever at least one of its inputs is becomes low. If one input transitions high
whilst the other remains constant, the output retains its previous state until both inputs
change, at which point the output toggles accordingly. This behaviour is illustrated in
Fig. 3.8(d), where the falling edges of the control signals are intentionally delayed by a
specified duration (Td ). This ensures that both transistors are never conducting simul-
taneously, effectively preventing cross-conduction in the power stage. Other logic gate
implementations, such as NOR-based circuits, can also be employed depending on the
desired polarity and control logic [147, 148].

3.3.4    Driver-Rails Supply Generation
For discrete (off-the-shelf) converter implementations, several approaches exist to pro-
vide floating power supplies and isolated control signals for gate drivers, as illustrated in
Fig.,3.9. A widely adopted method employs a multi-winding transformer with a push-
pull driver, shown in Fig.,3.9(a). This configuration delivers isolated gate-drive signals
through secondary windings, ensuring reliable operation and galvanic isolation between
the control and power domains. However, this solution becomes bulky, expensive, and
difficult to scale or modularize as the number of converter levels or battery cells increases.


   Alternative techniques therefore rely on optical isolation combined with dedicated
DC–DC converters to supply the isolated gate drivers. In the first variant, Fig. 3.9(b),
the battery cells cannot directly power the drivers due to insufficient cell voltage. For in-
stance, Li-ion cells typically reach only up to about 4.3 V, which is inadequate for driving
discrete MOSFETs that generally require 12–20 V gate-source voltage. A boost converter
IC is therefore employed to generate the necessary supply for the gate driver circuits. A
second configuration, shown in Fig. 3.9(c), applies when the battery cells themselves
provide a sufficiently high voltage (typically 12–20 V), allowing them to supply the gate

                                             122
Figure 3.8: Dead-time generator circuits



                 123
Figure 3.9: Conventional implementations of battery balancers with capacitively/magnetically-
            coupled stacked half-bridge and full-bridge sub-modules using transformers based
            and optical (opto-coupler) based signal isolation techniques




drivers directly. This approach is only feasible in topologies where the half-bridge or full-
bridge submodules share a common reference with their corresponding battery domain,
eliminating the need for isolation interfaces.

   Although robust, these solutions introduce significant cost, volume, and assembly ef-
fort in systems with large numbers of cells. Integrating such circuitry into larger drive
systems therefore becomes challenging. This motivates the use of more compact and
highly integrated techniques based on smaller discrete components such as switches, ca-
pacitors, and diodes. These approaches enable scalable balancing and driver architectures
capable of supporting a very high cell count with manageable complexity, reduced size,
and increased power density.

   The rest of this subsection examines several approaches for generating isolated driver-
rail supplies, with an emphasis on methods that enable higher levels of integration in
multilevel converter systems. In such converter architectures, the presence of multiple
floating switches and complex switching sequences inherently increases design complex-
ity. Consequently, compact and high power–density auxiliary supply circuits become es-
sential to facilitate integration, reduce the footprint of the overall system, and enhance
operational efficiency.

                                            124
   A general overview of the considered techniques is provided in Fig. 3.10 [43], with
schematic representations in Fig. 3.11. The techniques are broadly categorized according
to whether their operation is dependent upon the switching behaviour and conduction
paths of the main power stage. As a result, certain methods are applicable only to specific
converter topologies, whilst others are more universally compatible.

   A widely adopted method for supplying a floating rail to a high-side gate driver is
the bootstrap (BS) charge-pump configuration, illustrated in Fig. 3.11(a) [149, 150]. This
solution provides a cost-effective and simple means of generating the required gate bias
in systems characterized by repetitive switching patterns, such as pulse-width modulated
(PWM) converters. For example, PWM-based balancing schemes described previously
are well suited to this approach. Conversely, drive systems such as motor inverters, which
may experience irregular PWM patterns (e.g., during regenerative braking), are generally
incompatible with pure bootstrap solutions [149, 49].

   In this scheme, a capacitor is charged when the high-side device is off and the low-
side device is on, storing energy to supply the floating driver when the high-side device
turns on. The bootstrap voltage is defined as Vbs = Vdrv − VDbs , where Vdrv is the driver
supply and VDbs is the diode drop. Since the driver circuitry only experiences Vdrv , lower-
voltage semiconductor devices are used. Typical bootstrap capacitance values are on the
order of 100 nF, which is generally too large for monolithic integration, necessitating
off-chip implementation [151]. Neglecting static consumption and assuming that the
stored charge is dedicated solely to charging the high-side gate, the minimum value of
the bootstrap capacitor may be approximated as:


                                                      Vdrv
                                  CBS = Cgate-SW1 ·                                    (3.3)
                                                      ∆VBS

   where ∆VBS represents the allowable voltage droop during the on-time interval.

   Due to the eventual discharge of CBS through leakage and static currents, conven-
tional bootstrapping cannot sustain extended high-side conduction periods. To address
this limitation, a charge-pump mechanism may be incorporated, enabling replenishment
of the bootstrap capacitor without requiring the switching node to return to ground po-
tential, as shown in Fig. 3.11(b) [151].

   Additional design considerations include start-up behaviour and the diode forward-


                                            125
                       Figure 3.10: Gate driver power supply techniques



voltage drop, both of which can compromise system performance. At low supply volt-
ages, the diode drop may significantly degrade the effective gate drive voltage, reducing
conduction efficiency. Furthermore, reliable start-up must be ensured even when the con-
verter has not yet established switching activity. An enhanced configuration addressing
these concerns is presented in Fig. 3.11(c) [151, 49, 127]. A small auxiliary transistor,
activated by a power-on-reset signal, temporarily forces the switching node low to pre-
charge the bootstrap path. Alternatively, a large resistor (e.g., 100 kΩ) may be connected
between Vdc and CBS , combined with a Zener clamp. Although effective, this solution
incurs small current leakage [151, 49].

   To mitigate diode-related losses, the architecture incorporates an auxiliary bootstrap
capacitor, Caux , and diode, which power a secondary switch that operates synchronously
with the main power stage. When the high-side device conducts, this auxiliary switch,
M1 , is enabled, eliminating the diode drop in the primary charging path. When the high
side is off, the main bootstrap capacitor is recharged through the body diode of M1 . For
safe operation, M1 must be rated to withstand Vdc +Vdrv .

   Another method capable of supplying the floating rail without requiring VSSH to return
to ground is illustrated in Fig. 3.11(d). In this configuration, a single control signal, Vcnt ,
drives a CMOS inverter I1 referenced to the ground-based supply Vdrv [152, 153, 154].

   During operation, the capacitor C1 is initially charged from the reference node VSW

                                             126
Figure 3.11: Overview of floating gate-driver supply architectures: (a) conventional bootstrap
             supply, (b) enhanced bootstrap with auxiliary charge-pump, (c) switch-based boot-
             strap with start-up mechanism, (d) low-power charge-pump with push-pull rec-
             tification, (e) capacitively isolated supply for full independence from the main
             power stage, (f) self-powered driver supply, (g) shunt-regulated floating supply, (h)
             low-drop charge-pump architecture independent of the main power stage, (i) self-
             boosting charge-pump topology.



through diode D1 whilst the switching node Vx is held at ground potential. When Vx is
subsequently driven to Vdrv , the lower terminal of C1 experiences a voltage step equal to
Vdrv , causing the stored charge to be transferred to the output capacitor Co . The associated
waveforms for the charging phases of C1 and Co are also illustrated in the figure. For
correct operation, the pulse applied to Vx must occur whilst VSW is high, and its duration
must be sufficiently long to complete charge sharing between C1 and Co , as dictated by
their RC time constant [152].

    This charge-pump topology reduces bottom-plate parasitic losses by limiting the volt-
age swing, a benefit that becomes more pronounced at high input voltages. Furthermore,
the effective forward-voltage penalty is decreased from three diode drops to two. The
scheme is particularly suited to systems in which the floating reference node VSW re-
mains constant or exhibits a limited voltage swing not exceeding approximately Vdrv .

                                              127
Simulation and experimental studies indicate that, for integrated low-power drivers, this
architecture achieves a good trade-off between conversion efficiency, silicon area, and
output ripple, provided that Co is sized to approximately twice C1 . Only C1 must be
implemented as a high-voltage device [152].

   When the gate-driver supply relies on the main power stage as its current delivery
path, its applicability becomes limited by the converter topology. In particular, certain
switching states may interrupt the current flow required to maintain the supply, such as
during high-side or low-side conduction intervals, or when adjacent devices are simul-
taneously turned off. These conditions can prevent power delivery to the gate-driver
circuitry, thereby constraining the operation of the main converter. To overcome this de-
pendency and enable fully autonomous driver-supply operation, Fig. 3.11(e) illustrates a
floating charge-pump architecture employing capacitive isolation [43].

   This topology comprises three functional blocks: a capacitor-drive stage on the pri-
mary side, a rectification and energy-storage network on the secondary side (including
rectifier diodes, output capacitor, and Zener clamp), and a pair of coupling capacitors
linking the two domains. Power transfer occurs through capacitive coupling, whilst the
DC blocking characteristic of the capacitors isolates the floating output from the primary
reference. Moreover, multiple isolated floating supplies can be derived from a single
capacitor-drive circuit by paralleling multiple secondary stages, as demonstrated in the
figure [43].

   The operation proceeds in two phases. During the transition from Mode 2 to Mode 1,
a “reset” current flows through the coupling capacitors C1 and C2 and diode D2 , estab-
lishing the appropriate voltage across the coupling network; in this phase, no energy is
delivered to the output. Following this, when the circuit transitions from Mode 1 back to
Mode 2, a “forward” current path is formed through C1 , C2 , and diodes D1 and D3 , trans-
ferring charge to the output capacitor Co . The secondary-side voltage Vo increases until it
reaches the Zener clamp level, after which excess current is diverted through DZ , main-
taining Vo ≈ VZ . Importantly, neither mode requires a conduction path through the main
power stage. As a result, this architecture enables continuous power delivery independent
of converter topology or switching sequence [43].

   A self-powered gate-driver supply architecture was introduced in [155] and is illus-
trated in Fig. 3.11(f). In this design, a Zener diode DZ , biased through resistor RZ , estab-


                                             128
lishes a regulated gate-bias voltage for the control transistor MR . When the main power
switch turns off, the full DC-link voltage Vdc appears across the circuit, activating MR and
allowing the energy buffer capacitor Cs to charge via diode Ds . This method removes the
need for a shared auxiliary supply rail, in contrast to conventional driver-power strate-
gies. However, its applicability is constrained by the allowable input-voltage range, a
limitation that becomes particularly relevant in multilevel converter configurations. This
constraint is discussed later in the section when reviewing the circuit’s behaviour in mul-
tilevel driver-supply implementations.

   A floating driver supply can also be realised using a voltage-limiting regulator, as il-
lustrated in Fig. 3.11(g). This approach is suitable when a relatively high floating voltage
is already available and can be locally regulated to the required driver-rail level.

   The circuit operates by employing a Zener diode DZ in conjunction with a shunt
capacitor to establish a stable reference for a source-follower transistor (pass device)
MR . The bias resistor RZ is dimensioned such that only a few microamperes of current
are drawn, ensuring the Zener diode maintains its breakdown voltage. To minimise output
variation under changing load conditions, the pass device MR is sized with a large width-
to-length ratio, providing high transconductance and thus limiting fluctuations in VGS so
that Vo remains within the acceptable tolerance band.

   This structure is well suited for on-chip integration. The achieved output voltage is
approximately equal to the Zener reference minus the gate-source voltage drop of MR .
If higher output is required, this offset can be compensated using an auxiliary diode-
connected device of the same type but with a significantly smaller W /L ratio [156, 49].

   A charge-pump supply for a floating gate driver is illustrated in Fig. 3.11(h) [157,
127, 49]. Unlike approaches that rely on conduction intervals of the power stage, this
topology operates independently of the converter switching paths. The circuit is driven
by a single control clock Vcnt . During the charging interval (Vcnt = 1), the flying capacitor
CF is charged to the supply voltage Vdrv , whilst the output capacitor Co provides energy
to the high-side driver and associated circuitry.

   When the clock transitions low (Vcnt = 0), CF is re-configured to share its charge with
Co , thereby periodically updating and refreshing Co . Through this mechanism, Co can
maintain a continuous bias for the high-side device, ensuring uninterrupted gate drive.
Although the schematic displays body diodes for completeness, the energy transfer path


                                             129
is implemented entirely with active switches, preventing any diode-related voltage degra-
dation. The inverter I1 biases MP3 relative to the floating high-side supply domain (VDDH ,
Vsw ). Under ideal conditions, the output voltage approaches Vdrv [157, 127, 49].

   Despite its simplicity, several device-stress considerations must be observed. (1) With
Vcnt = 0, the source of MP2 can rise to Vsw +VCF ≈ Vdc +Vdrv , potentially requiring a level-
shifting interface for MP2 depending on the operating voltages. The transistors MN1 and
MN3 , in contrast, may be driven directly from a ground-referenced control signal. (2)
When Vcnt = 1, the drain of MP3 is forced to ground through MN3 whilst its source rises
to Vdc + Vdrv , imposing a large drain-to-source stress across MP3 . (3) Similarly, MN2
must withstand up to Vdc across its terminals. Thus, when operating at high DC-bus
voltages or in processes that do not provide sufficiently high device ratings, diodes may
be substituted for the stressed transistors, although this introduces increased conduction
losses [157, 127, 49].

   Therefore, for high Vsw voltages, the circuit in Fig. 3.11(i) uses diodes and a resistor
to replace MN3 , MP1 , MP3 , and the inverter I1 . when the control signal is high (Vcnt = 1),
transistor M2 isolates the switching node Vsw from the bottom terminal of CF . In this state,
the gate of M1 is pulled to ground, forcing M1 into the off condition. Consequently, the
flying capacitor CF is recharged from the driver supply voltage Vdrv via diodes D1 and D3
[158, 49].

   During the complementary phase, when Vcnt = 0, M1 turns off and the pull-up resistor
Rpu raises the gate of M2 . This action connects the bottom plates of CF and Co , allowing
CF to transfer charge to Co through diode D2 . The use of a single clock signal, combined
with passive gate-boosting through Rpu , simplifies the control scheme and makes this
charge-pump configuration appealing for integrated implementations [158, 49].

   However, the circuit exhibits two main limitations. First, the pull-up resistor Rpu
introduces static power consumption dependent on the switching-node voltage Vsw . Sec-
ond, the cumulative diode voltage drops substantially reduce the attainable output level,
ideally yielding Vo ≈ Vdrv − 3Vdiode . In addition, diodes D1 and D2 , as well as transistors
M1 and M2 , must be rated for high voltage operation [158, 49].

   These floating gate-supply techniques can be employed in multilevel power convert-
ers to enhance integration and enable converters with a higher number of voltage levels.
Such topologies require a large number of semiconductor switches, resulting in costly


                                             130
gate-drive circuits and complex layouts.

   Figure 3.11 shows the use of a conventional bootstrap (BS) circuit in a neutral-point
diode-clamped multilevel converter and a flying-capacitor converter [42, 159]. In these
configurations, the lowest DC-bus node is fixed, allowing a single supply referenced
to this bus to charge all bootstrap capacitors. The circuit must establish valid current
paths to ensure proper charging of each BS capacitor. In this arrangement, CBS1 –CBS4
are energized directly from the external auxiliary supply, whilst CBS5 –CBS7 are charged
through CBS4 . Capacitor CBS4 charges only when switches S1 –S4 are simultaneously
gated on. This prevents short-circuit paths, which is why the upper capacitors cannot be
charged through lower-leg switches. This limitation arises from the operating principle of
the flying-capacitor topology. For example, switches S4 and S5 cannot be simultaneously
turned on to charge CBS5 , since this would short-circuit the flying capacitor C10 [159].

   In diode-clamped converters, only one valid switching state exists for a given output
voltage level. In contrast, flying-capacitor converters may allow multiple switching states
for the same voltage reference. The required BS capacitance is given by [42]:


                                       idrive tBS + Ns Qgate
                               CBS =                         ,                         (3.4)
                                                ∆Vgate

   where idrive is the current drawn by the gate-driver and signal-isolation circuits, tBS is
the maximum discharge interval determined by the modulation period, Ns is the number
of switching transitions within tBS , Qgate is the device gate charge, and ∆Vgate is the
allowable voltage ripple across the BS capacitor.

   For an m-level converter, low modulation indices may prevent the upper bootstrap
capacitors from receiving sufficient charge. To maintain proper operation, the lowest
switch must periodically conduct to recharge all BS capacitors. Therefore, the BS tech-
nique becomes unsuitable for multilevel converters operating with modulation indices
below [42]
                                           m−3
                                               .
                                           m−1

   The capacitively isolated floating supply shown in Fig. 8.1(e) is applied to the FCC
and NPC MLI configurations illustrated in Fig. 3.13. As reported in [43], this approach
enables operation that is functionally independent from the NPC and FCC power stages.
In practice, however, several implementation challenges arise. Due to switching activity

                                            131
        Figure 3.12: NPC and FCML converters with bootstrap floating driver supplies



in the main power circuit, pulsed leakage currents flow through the supply path, poten-
tially imposing current stresses on the components that exceed their ratings. The magni-
tude of this leakage current depends on the coupling-capacitor value and the secondary-
side dv/dt, the latter being difficult to predict accurately in advance. To mitigate this
issue, current-limiting resistors Rlim are incorporated.

   Additionally, common-mode leakage currents may lead to malfunction, requiring de-
sign countermeasures. One such measure is to realize the power-supply and isolation
circuits separately, with the power-supply components placed on the main-circuit side.
In many cases, isolation between the main converter stage and the control circuitry is not
essential; however, when isolation is necessary, a dedicated isolated power supply must
be included [43].

   A hybrid floating-supply scheme is illustrated in Fig. 3.14 for the level-generation
section of the MLDCL topology shown in Fig. ??, where the supply circuits from
Fig. 8.1(e) and (g) are employed. For the low-side switch, a shunt voltage regulator is
used to step the battery voltage (e.g., Vdc1 ) to a level suitable for the gate driver and
signal-isolation circuitry. If the battery voltage is already within the acceptable range
for the switch driver, it can be connected directly, eliminating the need for the regulator.
For the high-side switch, the same capacitive-isolation approach described in Fig. 3.13
is adopted. Signal-isolation blocks may be implemented using magnetic coupling (high-
frequency transformers), capacitive coupling, or high-voltage level-shifter circuits.

   Figure 3.15 illustrates the implementation of Charge Pump (CP) circuits in diode-
clamped and flying-capacitor converters. For series-connected H-bridge converters, CP

Figure 3.13: NPC and FCML converters with capacitively-isolated floating driver supplies

fixed. In this case, individual CP circuits are connected to each main switching device
[42].

   The operation of the CP circuits is independent of the main power circuit, even in

                                            135
3.4       IC Packaging and Assembly
Designs aiming for high power density must address both electrical and thermal chal-
lenges that arise from processing substantial power within a limited chip area. Conse-
quently, the packaging design for high-power converters becomes a pivotal aspect of
the prototype and product development cycle. Achieving an optimal balance between
superior electrical performance and compact, high-power-density design requires careful
consideration of the power IC packaging strategy. The package fulfils several essen-
tial functions, including efficient heat dissipation and removal from the die, mechanical
protection and stability, and redistribution of IC-level pads and connections into a man-
ageable fine-pitch format [49].
In this section, various packaging technologies are examined in terms of their cost, elec-
trical and thermal performance, design complexity, and availability. Depending on the
production volume, certain packaging options may offer greater cost-effectiveness than
others.
Package design encompasses several critical elements, including interconnection meth-
ods, die-attach technologies, and substrate materials. The substrate acts as the structural
foundation of the IC package, providing both mechanical support and an interface for
electrical interconnections.



3.4.1     Package Types
IC packages are available in various forms, each tailored to specific performance, cost,
and design requirements. Common types include the Quad Flat Package (QFP) and its
variants such as Thin QFP (TQFP), Low-Profile QFP (LQFP), the (Thin) Shrink Small
Outline Package (TSSOP/SSOP), and the Metric QFP (MQFP). Other package types in-
clude Quad Flat No-lead (QFN), Thin Small Outline Package (TSOP), and Small Outline
Integrated Circuit (SOIC) packages. These package families differ in terms of manufac-
turing cost, input/output (I/O) density, compactness, and thermal dissipation capability
[160, 161, 162, 163].
Within each package, the semiconductor die is mounted onto the central pad of the sub-
strate. Bond wires—discussed in the next section—connect the die’s metal pads to the
lead frame, which ultimately forms the package leads or pins that are soldered to the
PCB. In thermally enhanced designs, the exposed pad on the underside of the package

                                           136
facilitates efficient heat transfer to the PCB ground plane through thermal vias.
QFP packages are typically fabricated from plastic or ceramic materials, providing both
mechanical protection and environmental durability. The inclusion of an exposed metal
pad at the bottom of the package further improves thermal dissipation, rendering QFPs
suitable for heat-sensitive ICs. The visible leads simplify optical inspection and electrical
testing; however, the fine pitch and fragility of these leads make them prone to damage
during handling or assembly. Once bent or deformed, repair is often impractical. More-
over, the relatively high parasitic inductance and resistance of the leads can introduce
signal integrity issues such as ringing and limit current-carrying capability [164].
In contrast, QFN package is a leadless surface-mount technology (SMT) option that of-
fers a compact footprint and improved electrical characteristics. Its absence of protruding
leads minimises package size, making it well-suited for space-constrained applications.
QFN packages also provide superior electrical performance owing to shorter interconnect
paths and reduced inductance. Furthermore, they are compliant with modern lead-free
manufacturing standards and offer straightforward handling during automated assembly
processes [165].
For ICs requiring a large number of pins or operating at high frequencies and currents,
advanced packaging technologies such as Ball Grid Array (BGA) and Wafer-Level Re-
distribution Chip-Scale Package (WLCSP) are often preferred. These approaches replace
traditional wire bonding with a copper Redistribution Layer (RDL), which routes sig-
nals to an area array of solder balls (or bumps). Such configurations enable flip-chip
assembly, wherein the die is mounted face-down with the bumps directly contacting the
carrier. This technique significantly reduces parasitic resistance and inductance between
the die and package pins, resulting in enhanced electrical and thermal performance whilst
supporting smaller overall form factors and reduced production costs [49].
For surface mountable packages that have an area no more than 1.2 times that of a single
die can be classified as CSPs. They come in different technologies such as Customized
lead-frame-based CSP (LFCSP), Flexible substrate-based CSP, Flip-chip CSP (FCCSP),
Rigid substrate-based CSP, Wafer-level redistribution CSP (WLCSP) [163], [166], [167].




                                            137
3.4.2     Die-to-Package Connectivity

The interconnection between the IC and the package substrate can be achieved using
various technologies [168]. One of the most widely adopted methods is wire bonding,
primarily due to its cost-effectiveness, high reliability, and process flexibility. Depending
on the bonding technique, wire bonding can be classified into two major types: ball
bonding and wedge bonding.

   In ball-type wire bonding, a small spherical ball is formed at the end of the bond-
ing wire through the combined effects of elevated temperature, pressure, and ultrasonic
energy. Gold (Au) and copper (Cu) are the most commonly used materials for bonding
wires in this process. Unlike wedge bonding, ball bonding does not require the wire to
remain perfectly straight during the initial bond, thereby reducing the likelihood of die
displacement during bonding. This method is also preferred in cases where the under-
lying circuitry beneath the bond pads is sensitive to mechanical stress, as ball bonding
exerts lower pressure on the surface and is less likely to cause cratering. However, since
the process involves localized heating, it may pose a risk to temperature-sensitive ICs,
potentially leading to performance degradation or damage.

   The general procedure for ball-type wire bonding can be summarised as follows:

   1. A gold or copper wire is melted using an Electronic Flame Off (EFO) system to
        create a spherical ball at the tip of the capillary. The bonder then aligns this ball
        precisely over the target bond pad.
   2. The die and the bond pad are preheated to the appropriate bonding temperature,
        which depends on the material properties and wire specifications.
   3. Bond formation is achieved by applying a controlled combination of pressure and
        ultrasonic energy to the capillary for a defined bonding duration.
   4. The capillary then moves in a loop, following a predetermined shape, toward the
        location of the second bond pad.
   5. The second bond is formed, typically in the shape of a crescent or a stitch, depend-
        ing on the application requirements.
   6. Finally, the capillary retracts, breaking the wire and leaving a small tail, which will
        be used to form the next bonding ball.

   The number of bonding wires connected in parallel is determined by the maximum
current-carrying requirement of the circuit. This, in turn, depends on both the diameter

                                               138
                             Figure 3.17: IC bonding methods



and material of the bonding wires.

   Wedge-type wire bonding is performed at or near ambient temperature and relies on
the combined application of pressure and ultrasonic energy to establish the electrical and
mechanical connection between the wire and the bond pad. This technique is particularly
suitable for materials such as aluminium (Al) and gold (Au) wires and is often preferred
in applications that require fine-pitch interconnections or operation under low thermal
budgets.

   The general procedure for wedge-type wire bonding can be summarised as follows:

   1. The wedge tool grips the aluminium or gold wire and positions it precisely over
      the designated bond pad on the chip, package, or substrate.
   2. The wire is pressed against the pad surface whilst ultrasonic energy is applied,
      initiating intermetallic formation and creating a strong metallurgical bond.
   3. The wedge tool then moves along a straight path to the second bond location. Main-
      taining a linear wire trajectory is crucial to minimise stress at the bond heel and to
      prevent die movement or mechanical damage.
   4. Once the second bond is completed, the tool retracts, forming the bond heel and
      leaving a short wire tail in preparation for the next bonding cycle.

   In both bonding techniques, stitch bonding can be employed to interconnect or join
multiple bond pads. The trajectory or loop profile followed by the bonding tool can
be programmed to produce customized loop geometries, each offering distinct electrical

                                           139
and mechanical characteristics. This flexibility is advantageous in applications where
specific loop heights or shapes are required to meet design constraints or reliability con-
siderations.

   For higher current or high-frequency applications, thick-wire or ribbon bonding is of-
ten utilised to enhance current-carrying capability and reduce inductance. Ribbon bond-
ing, a variant of wedge bonding, employs flat wires or ribbons instead of round wires.
In this method, the chip pads must be compatible with the wedge bonding process. For
instance, Circuit-Under-Pad (CUP) designs are generally unsuitable for wedge or ribbon
bonding due to the potential risk of mechanical damage to the semiconductor structures
located beneath the bond pads.

   Flip-chip attach: this technique provides a direct method of connecting an IC die
to a substrate. In this configuration, the active surface of the die faces the substrate, and
electrical interconnections are formed directly through conductive bumps, such as cop-
per pillar bumps illustrated in Fig.3.18. Flip-chip attachment offers several advantages,
including excellent electrical performance, compact package size, high-frequency capa-
bility, and reduced EMI, since no wire loops are present. Additionally, it supports a high
I/O pin count and is compatible with a wide range of substrate materials such as laminate
PCBs, polyimide, ceramics, glass, silicon, and plastic lead frames.

   Despite these benefits, flip-chip technology also presents certain challenges. The
process involves relatively high assembly costs and requires exceptionally flat mounting
surfaces. Moreover, differences in thermal expansion between the die and substrate can
induce mechanical stress, potentially leading to cracking of the bump interconnections.
Consequently, careful consideration of substrate, bump, and underfill material properties
is essential for reliable flip-chip assembly. The attachment method is particularly critical
and is summarised in Figure 3.17, whilst Figure 3.18 illustrate a typical flip-chip structure
based on copper pillar and solder cap configuration.

   The general process flow for flip-chip solder joint formation can be outlined as fol-
lows:

   1. Following wafer fabrication, a Under-Bump Metallisation (UBM) layer—typically
        composed of Cr/Cu/Au—is deposited onto the chip surface.
   2. Solder material is then electroplated or deposited onto the silicon wafer to form the
        bump layer.


                                            140
                              Figure 3.18: Flip-chip structure



   3. The silicon wafer is diced into individual dies or ICs.
   4. Each die is flipped and precisely aligned with its corresponding substrate.
   5. The solder bumps undergo a reflow process, forming the first-level solder joints
      (also referred to as controlled collapse chip connections, or C4) at approximately
      250 °C for SnPb solder.
   6. An underfill material is subsequently applied to provide mechanical support and
      thermal stress isolation.
   7. The assembled ICs are finally mounted onto PCBs using BGA solder balls, which
      form the second-level interconnections at a lower melting point (approximately
      180 °C for eutectic SnPb solder).

   One major advantage of this process is that it introduces minimal mechanical stress
on the circuits located beneath the bump pads during solder melting, reflow, and solidifi-
cation. This characteristic enables significantly higher I/O density, as interconnects can
be placed directly on active silicon regions where transistors or circuit elements reside
beneath the pads.

   An alternative approach, known as stud bumping, can be performed on bare dies using
a conventional wire bonding machine. The process is similar to ball-type wire bonding;
however, the wires are cut immediately above the formed balls, leaving conductive bumps
on the chip pads. The resulting bump dimensions depend on the EFO power, wire diame-
ter, and tail length used during the process. Various die-attach techniques, both adhesive
and non-adhesive, can then be employed to secure the die to the substrate, as summarised
in Figure 3.17. The advantages of stud bumping are comparable to those of solder reflow
flip-chip technology, though it generally offers reduced assembly cost.

                                           141
3.4.3    Die-to-Board Electrical Model

Ringing on chip terminals arises from parasitic inductances present between the external
input terminals and the on-chip input nodes. These parasitic elements primarily originate
from the wire bonds, package leads, and PCB traces. In switched converters, both termi-
nal currents are inherently discontinuous and exhibit rapid transitions. When these fast
current transitions interact with the parasitic inductances, voltage oscillations (ringing)
occur. The ringing amplitude is proportional to the magnitude of the input current step
and inversely proportional to the transition time ∆tr [169].

   Several mitigation techniques have been proposed to suppress this ringing. One com-
mon approach involves increasing the rise and fall times of the gate-drive signals to ex-
tend ∆tr , thereby reducing the severity of the oscillations. Another method employs RC
snubbers that provide an alternative path for the transient current, effectively damping
the oscillatory response. Additionally, the inclusion of on-chip decoupling capacitors be-
tween the chip terminals or intermediate taps can reduce the resonant frequency of the
parasitic LC network. By lowering this resonance to a frequency well below the con-
verter’s switching frequency, both the magnitude and frequency of the ringing can be
significantly diminished [170, 171, 172].

   The combined parasitic effects of the lead frame, bond wires, and PCB traces can
be represented by a lumped R–L–C equivalent circuit, as illustrated in Fig. 3.19. In this
model, the resistance and inductance of the lead frame and bond wires are consolidated
into single lumped elements R and L, respectively. When coupling between adjacent pins
is non-negligible, a more accurate representation may incorporate mutual inductance (M)
and coupling capacitance (Cm ) [173, 174].

   The current-carrying capabilities of gold (Au) bonding wires are summarised in Ta-
ble 3.2 [175]. To minimise wire-bond resistance and inductance, multiple bonding wires
can be connected in parallel to each chip pad. This is often practical, as the bond-
ing pads can be enlarged to accommodate additional wires as required by the electrical
and thermal specifications. Given the moderate operating frequencies typical of power
converters, the resulting increase in pad capacitance does not adversely affect perfor-
mance. Alternatively, ribbon bonding can be employed—using flat wires such as 1×2 mil
(25 µm × 50 µm)—which provides shorter and straighter interconnects. However, this ap-
proach requires wedge bonding rather than ball bonding which may introduce restrictions

                                             142
                          Figure 3.19: Power delivery network model


on the bonding process.

Table 3.2: Current-carrying capability of Au wires in Amperes for different wire lengths and
           diameters

                           Wire diameter (mils)   1 mm    2 mm 3 mm
                           0.7                    0.612   0.355 0.254
                           1.0                     1.12   0.625 0.435
                           1.3                     1.80    0.97 0.665
                           2.0                    4.075    2.11 1.425




3.5     Conclusions
This chapter has reviewed devices relevant to the proposed designs in this thesis, in-
cluding both vertical discrete power devices and lateral power devices. Vertical devices
encompass standard and superjunction MOSFETs, IGBT switches, and emerging wide-
bandgap technologies. The review indicates that lateral devices excel in dynamic per-
formance, high switching frequencies, compactness, cost efficiency, the integration of
smart features (monitoring, protection, etc.), and co-integration with analogue and dig-
ital circuitry. In contrast, discrete vertical devices are not well suited for highly inte-
grated, large-scale converter implementations where a large number of switches is re-
quired. Their bulkiness, the need for dedicated gate drivers and isolated supplies, and the

                                              143
reduced reliability arising from numerous electrical connections, joints, and soldered in-
terfaces limit their applicability. Therefore, a key theme pursued in this thesis is reducing
the number of discrete devices and migrating the high-switch-count portion of multilevel
converters onto the chip, whilst retaining only a small number of off-chip switches that
handle high voltage but operate at low switching speeds.

   The reviewed MLI topologies and their driver-rail generator circuits face challenges
related to physical size, assembly complexity, and operational limitations. These issues
limitations either from relying heavily on the main converter circuit or from requiring
high-voltage devices to achieve a greater degree of independence.

   Based on the observations presented in Chapters 2 and 3, this thesis proposes novel
power-converter and battery-balancing topologies that enable a high level of integration.
The proposed solutions target miniaturisation, improved power density, reduced losses,
and lower overall system cost.




                                            144
Chapter 4


Circuit Designs for Modular
Battery-Balancing Architectures


4.1     Introduction

Multi-output voltage level shifters are critical components in systems that operate with
multiple voltage supplies, where control signals must be up-converted from the logic cir-
cuit’s reference domain to the various supply voltage domains. A prominent example of
such systems is battery balancing circuits, which manage large numbers of rechargeable
battery cells connected in series and in parallel [80]. These circuits play a crucial role in
balancing the state of charge among individual batteries, compensating for differences in
the cells’ internal characteristics and the circuit’s operating conditions. By keeping the
cells balanced, these systems help to fully utilize the battery string’s capacity, ensure the
safety of the cells, and extend their operational lifetime [176].

   High-speed battery balancing circuits often require numerous power switches to in-
terface with series-connected battery cells [83]. Each switch demands a dedicated gate
driver powered by its own floating or isolated DC supply [177]. Conventional gate driv-
ing solutions for such systems are bulky and increase the component count considerably.
Traditional designs, such as in [83] and [176], rely on discrete components that require
separate control circuits for control signals generation.
To meet the growing demand for compact, integrated power electronic systems, battery
balancing circuits can be implemented with low-voltage silicon switches integrated onto
a single chip. This approach requires adopting chip-scale balancing topologies. Fig-
ure 4.1 (a) illustrates a typical battery balancing system architecture interfacing with a
Figure 4.1: (a) Three-phase voltage source traction inverter interfaced with a battery balancing
            block. (b) Key design parameters of multiple-output gate drivers and voltage level
            shifters.


battery string B1 ...Bn , as used in a multi-cell two-level three-phase traction inverter.
The multiple-output voltage level shifting for balancing applications must introduce min-
imal delay, consume low power, occupy a small silicon area, and support modularity and
scalability, as highlighted by the critical design parameters in Fig. 4.1(b). Modularity and
scalability are critical when extending single-chip modules to support larger systems.
 Voltage level-shifters can be categorized into active-coupled circuits—including con-
tinuous, pulse-based, and cross-coupled designs [135], [138], [140]—and capacitive-
coupled circuits [139], [143]. Existing high-voltage level shifters are typically designed
for single-output operation, necessitating the division of the high voltage supply rail into
smaller voltage sub-ranges to utilize low-voltage silicon devices.
A common type is the cross-coupled high-voltage level shifter with CMOS (PMOS/NMOS)
intermediate pairs [178], [179]. While effective, these circuits require isolating N-wells
for devices and precise PMOS-to-NMOS ratio tuning under worst-case process con-
ditions (e.g., Slow N/Fast P). This architecture occupies a large layout area [138], in-
troduces significant delays as voltage levels increase, and limits multi-chip stacking
capability, making it unsuitable for applications with a large stack of battery cells.
For multi-output applications, the internal nodes in cross-coupled structures must serve
as outputs to drive subsequent stages. However, these nodes often exhibit reduced volt-
age swings by around one threshold voltage, increasing static power consumption in

                                             146
subsequent buffers and limiting the driving capability of the level shifter devices. These
issues effectively slow down the charging and discharging of the internal and output node
capacitances.
A split-bias structure, as proposed in [178], enhances the drive strength of the interme-
diate nodes and thereby improves the speed of the level shifter. In this approach, the
PMOS gate is biased at a voltage one threshold below the original gate voltage, while the
NMOS gate is biased one threshold above it. This biasing scheme effectively mitigates
the impact of threshold voltage reduction. However, implementing this structure requires
two distinct voltage ranges, which presents significant challenges in cell-based applica-
tions where generating such ranges is not straightforward, in particular under imbalanced
conditions.
A multiple-output cross-coupled level shifter was first introduced in [179], with further
performance enhancements, at the cost of more area and hardware complexity, proposed
in [180], for use in electrically erasable programmable read-only memory applications.
These designs employ cross-coupled PMOS transistors at the middle nodes to pull up
and latch each output node when the opposite output node stabilizes at its desired voltage
level. This configuration improves voltage swing, enabling intermediate outputs to effec-
tively drive subsequent stages. However, it also increases sensitivity to process, voltage,
and temperature (PVT) variations. The added cross-coupled PMOS pairs impose higher
requirement on the pull-down strength of the input device, which must overcome the
combined pull-up effect of the PMOS devices in the stack. Consequently, the input
device must be sized to accommodate the total current of the pull-up PMOS transistors.
Additionally, placing PMOS transistors in separate N-wells increases the layout area
and design complexity. Improper sizing of these transistors can worsen performance,
leading to incorrect state latching, metastability, wrong outputs, and higher static power
consumption.
To address these challenges, this chapter proposes two new multi-output voltage level-
shifter circuits, evaluated against a cross-coupled reference architecture. The circuits are
modular and scalable and offer minimal layout area, sub-nano-second propagation delay,
wider battery voltage range of 5 V to 1.8 V, and very high robustness against process
and temperature variations. Moreover, the designs integrate non-overlapping signal gen-
erators at the inputs to reduce the overall gate driver circuit area and enable dead-time
programmability for higher performance optimisation.

                                            147
Figure 4.2: Architectures of multiple power-trains based on half-bridge sub-modules. (a) Con-
            ventional structure with local dead-time circuits for each sub-module. (b) Proposed
            CMOS (PMOS/NMOS) architecture with a single dead-time circuit at the input of
            the level-shifting module. (c) Proposed dual-NMOS architecture with input dead-
            time control and gate drive supply generators.




The chapter is organized into four sections. Section 4.2 introduces and compares different
integrated powertrain architectures. Section 4.3 presents the circuit design, implemen-
tation, and post-layout simulation results. Section 4.4 provides fabrication and experi-
mental results and compares the proposed circuits with prior work. Finally, Section 4.5
concludes the paper.




4.2     Multiple-Output Gate Driver Architectures

A multiple-output gate driver consists of several key stages, including a voltage
level-shifting stage, a dead-time generation stage, low-side and high-side gate driver
stages, and the gate voltage supply circuits. The choice between PMOS/NMOS or
NMOS/NMOS configurations for the power train sub-modules depends primarily on the
area and quality of the PMOS devices in a given technology, as well as the current and
speed requirements of the circuit. Three different gate driver architectures based on half-
bridge sub-modules are discussed next.

                                             148
4.2.1    PMOS/NMOS Sub-Modules with Multiple and Single Dead-
         Time Generator(s)

The first topology for implementing half-bridge or full-bridge switching modules em-
ploys a PMOS switch on the high side and an NMOS switch on the low side, as depicted
in Fig. 4.2 (a). This architecture simplifies the driving of the high-side switch by utilizing
the DC source or battery to provide the gate drive voltage, thereby eliminating the need
for a high-side level-shifting stage and high-side gate driver supply in each sub-module.
Two techniques can be used to introduce non-overlapping control signals in this con-
figuration. The first involves level-shifting the control signals before adding dead-time
circuits, as in Fig. 4.2 (a). Placing dead-time circuits within each module in the stack
allows them to adapt to the gate capacitances of the switches. However, this approach
requires significant layout area due to the use of isolated devices for high-voltage im-
plementations and the inclusion of delay elements such as cascaded CMOS inverters or
RC circuits. Additionally, this method complicates dead-time programmability, as it de-
mands extra control signals and level shifters, reducing the overall flexibility of the gate
driver circuit.
The second proposed architecture, shown in Fig. 4.2 (b), addresses these limitations by
placing the dead-time generator block at the input of the level shifter stage. This design
facilitates the programmability of the dead-time duration while significantly reducing the
overall area of the gate driver circuit. However, this approach necessitates high-speed
level shifters with strong resilience to process, temperature, and supply variations, which
is the aim of this paper.


4.2.2    Dual-NMOS Sub-Modules with a Single Dead-Time Generator

Although the PMOS/NMOS configuration may be justified to simplify gate driving and
signal level-shifting based on circuit specifications and the quality of the high-voltage
PMOS in the technology employed, its area penalty in high-current circuits can outweigh
the total area required for gate supply and level-shifting stages in a high-side NMOS
implementation, as shown in Fig. 4.2 (c).
For N-type power switches, the high-side gate driver requires a boosted gate overdrive
voltage to ensure proper turn-on. This voltage is typically provided by a bootstrap ca-
pacitor or a charge pump circuit. In the case of series-connected batteries, this voltage is

                                             149
readily available for the low-side switch from the battery itself. For the high-side switch,
the upper battery in the string can be leveraged to provide the gate overdrive voltage for
the first (n − 1) sub-modules. This can be achieved with the addition of a small single-
pole double-throw (SPDT) switch synchronized with the control logic of the high-side
and low-side sub-modules, as demonstrated in Fig. 4.2 (c).
An additional level-shifting block is included before the low-side drivers, to maintain
symmetry in circuit delays. This ensures that the dead-time period remains consistent
across the high-side and the low-side of the driver.
For the top-most, or nth sub-module, either a PMOS/NMOS stage using the same tech-
nique as in Fig. 4.2 (b) or a simple bootstrap charge-pump circuit can be implemented to
provide gate supply for the high-side switch.
The decision to adopt dual-NMOS stages for implementing the power-trains depends on
the maximum current required, as this determines the speed of the system. The total area
of the additional circuits required must be weighed against the area saved by using an
NMOS instead of a PMOS device on the high side.
An important consideration in dual-NMOS implementations is the synchronization of the
switching between the high-side gate drive generators and the sub-modules to prevent
overstressing the driver devices and the pull-up devices in the floating high-side level
shifters. Small DC-link capacitors can also be introduced to suppress voltage spikes on
the high-side gate drive supplies, enhancing circuit reliability and performance.




4.3     Proposed Multiple-Output Voltage Level-Shifting
        Circuits
In this section, the proposed voltage level-shifting circuits are analysed and compared
based on the criteria outlined in Fig. 4.1 (b). The proposed circuits are designed to up-
shift the required logic control signals to seven battery voltage domains, enabling the
control of multiple gate drivers, as discussed at the architecture level in Section II.

   Depending on specific application requirements, the circuits can be selected based
on the trade-offs between power budget, layout area, and speed. For example, in level
shifters powered by a stack of battery cells, static power consumption can be traded off for
gains in speed, compactness, a wider battery operating range, and improved robustness

                                            150
             Figure 4.3: Cross-section of 5 V asymmetric HV NMOS/PMOS devices




against process and temperature variations. Conversely, for applications with stringent
power constraints, designs with zero static current can be chosen, maintaining the same
advantages at the cost of a slight increase in circuit area.
The circuits presented in the following sections are implemented using 130 nm BCD
technology, employing high-voltage laterally-diffused drain MOS (LDMOS) devices. A
cross-sectional view of these devices is illustrated in Fig. 4.3. The isolation of the ND-
MOS and PDMOS bulks from the substrate is achieved through an N-buried layer. In
the case of the NDMOS, this layer is connected to the highest potential, while for the
PDMOS, it is tied to the source and bulk of the device.
Figure 4.4 demonstrates the physical layout of the cross-coupled level-shifter, continuous
active level-shifter, and the capacitive level-shifter circuits (excluding the dead-time gen-
erators).
The circuits layouts are extracted for RCC parasitics and simulated under different op-
erating conditions to verify their functionality and robustness against PVT variations.
Finally, Monte-Carlo statistical simulations are carried out to show the 3σ spread of the
preset dead-time period, the energy per transition, and the propagation delay with process
and mismatch. The key results and features of the proposed circuits are summarised in
Table 4.1 and further discussed and compared to prior work in Section IV.
The simulation testbench used to produce the results is shown in Fig. 4.5. Each circuit
is driven by a 0/3.3 V digital signal with a 1 MHz frequency and 1 ns rise and fall times.
A dead-time generator, as proposed earlier, is placed at the input of each circuit to split
the control signal into two complementary signals with a dead-time duration of 3.5 ns.
The circuits are loaded with two cascaded inverters, with a total input gate capacitance
of 194 fF.
