4.3.1    Cross-Coupled Level-Shifter with Multiple Differential Out-
         puts
Figure 4.6 (a) illustrates the conventional cross-coupled level shifter circuit, which con-
sists of a differential input pair driven by complementary low-voltage signals. This circuit
generates two complementary output signals, taken at nodes A and B. The high supply
voltage, VDDH , is divided into lower voltage levels that directly bias the shared gates of the
NMOS/PMOS pair structures. This voltage division facilitates reliable high-voltage level
shifting while utilizing low-voltage devices that are isolated in separate n-wells from the
substrate.

   Assuming node A is initially high at VDDH and node B is low at V2 + |Vtp |, where
|Vtp | is the absolute value of the pull-up PMOS threshold voltage, the sequence shown
Fig. 4.7 illustrates the state transitions. When device MN1 is activated by applying VDDL
to its gate, it enters the active/saturation region, pulling node VD1 strongly to the ground,
VSS1 , and turning on device M1 . Consequently, node G follows VD1 , driving MN3 into
the cut-off region and setting node E to V1 + |Vtpd |, where |Vtpd | is the absolute value of
the PDMOS device threshold voltage. This process continues sequentially, turning on
the NDMOS devices and cutting off the PDMOS devices in the same branch, until node

                                             153
A is pulled down by at least one threshold voltage below VDDH . At this stage, node B in
the opposite branch begins charging slowly towards VDDH . A similar process occurs in




               Figure 4.7: Node transitions for cross-coupled level shifter circuit



the opposite branch (right branch), where the PMOS devices are activated in the active
region while the NMOS devices are driven into the cut-off region. This continues until
the floating node VD2 is pulled up to V1 −Vtnd .
From this sequence, the circuit’s asymmetric switching behaviour becomes evident. One
branch exhibits fast switching due to the strong pull-down of the NDMOS and PDMOS
devices, whereas the pull-up process is substantially slower due to the weaker pull-up
PMOS devices. Furthermore, the source voltages of the NMOS and PMOS, Vs,NMOS and
Vs,PMOS , devices are constrained within the following range:


                                   Vs,NMOS ≤ Vgate −Vtnd ,                            (4.1)

                                   Vs,PMOS ≥ Vgate + |Vtpd |,                         (4.2)


where Vgate is the shared gate voltage bias applied to both transistors.

   This reduction in the intermediate node voltages by one threshold voltage results in
a lower current-driving capability for the devices, thereby increasing the charging and
discharging of the intermediate node capacitances during the pull-down and pull-up tran-
sitions. This means the inverters used to restore the full voltage swing may pass cross
currents for a short time following the switching events, and hence increasing the power
consumption.

   According to the analysis of a two-output cross-coupled level shifter in [138], for
correct DC operation, the aspect ratios of the PDMOS and NDMOS devices to the PMOS

   where:

    • Vtp : The threshold voltage of the PMOS device.
    • Vtnd : The threshold voltage of the NDMOS device.
    • Vtpd : The threshold voltage of the PDMOS device.
    • VDDmax : The maximum voltage difference between battery taps.

   These design ratios assume that the input NDMOS devices operate in the active region
while the PMOS cross-coupled devices operate in the linear region. The pull-up device
used in our design is the same type as the intermediate PDMOS devices. Therefore, Kp′
                         ′ and V , respectively.
and Vtp are the same as Kpd     tpd

   From these ratios, it is observed that under conditions of low battery voltages (e.g.,
VDD in the range of 2-2.5 V, as in lithium iron phosphate batteries), a fast PMOS/slow
NMOS process corner, and low temperatures, the required ratios become high to ensure
proper operation, as calculated in Table 4.2. Consequently, the layout area required for
this level shifter increases substantially. This issue is further compounded by the need to

                                                              155
isolate each device within a separate N-well and by the typically high minimum allowable
width-to-length ratio for such isolated high-voltage devices. The use of large devices
also introduces significant node capacitances, which in turn result in higher propagation
delays.

   The circuit shown in Fig. 4.6 (b) takes the intermediate outputs in the first circuit to
control multiple gate drivers operating within a VDD voltage range, and is modified to
incorporate dead-time or non-overlapping control signals, as proposed in Fig. 4.2 (b) and
(c). The dead-time circuit at the level shifter’s input introduces a delay period determined
by the time constant of the circuit, which is set by R1 and C1 . The input control signal
is split into two complementary signals, which control Mdt1 and Mdt2 . The rising edges
of these split signals are delayed by the same time constant (R1C1 ), thus introducing a
dead-time interval. This duration can be adjusted by using a digital multiplexer (mux)
with a configurable number of control bits to modify the values of capacitors C1 and C2 .

   The asymmetry in the circuit arises from the fact that the pull-down path is faster
than the pull-up. One consequence of this asymmetry is that generating non-overlapping
signals at the input becomes more challenging, particularly under PVT variations.

   From a topological perspective, the cross-coupled nature of the circuit prevents it
from being stacked as a chip module for handling a larger number of battery strings.
Therefore, the circuit is more suitable for applications that require circuits balancing 8 to
12 batteries on the same chip module.

   To validate the observations and analyses presented thus far, a 7-level cross-coupled
voltage level shifter circuit was designed, and post-layout parasitics were extracted. The
simulation results are presented in Fig. 4.8 to Fig. 4.10.

   Fig. 4.8. shows the transient waveforms of multiple output voltages under typical
process (TT), voltage (3.6 V), and temperature (27°C) conditions. The simulation results
confirm the asymmetry in the rising and falling delays. The dead-time expands by ap-
proximately 17 ns from VOUT1 to VOUT7 . Additionally, the voltage swing is shown to be
reduced by one threshold voltage, as expected. The current drawn by the level shifter
is also plotted in Fig. 4.8 (b), where a pulse current of around 2.3 mA is drawn across
the dead-time duration. During that period, the PDMOS devices in both branches of
the circuit operate in the active region, while the NDMOS devices operate in the triode
region.

    To evaluate the circuit’s behaviour under non-typical conditions, simulations are con-
ducted across various process corners (FF, FS, SF, SS) and extreme temperatures of
−40◦ C and 125◦ C. The maximum observed delay, approximately 38 ns, occurs at the
rising edge under the fast NMOS/slow PMOS process corner, as highlighted in Fig. 4.8
(b) and (c).

    The variations in propagation delay, computed as the average of rising and falling
edge delays, as well as the energy per transition, are simulated across the four process
corners and the full range of a typical Li-ion battery voltage (2.8 V to 4.2 V), as de-
picted in Fig. 4.9. The results show that the propagation delay—and consequently the
energy per transition—increases with temperature due to mobility degradation. The slow
PDMOS corners exhibit the highest propagation delays, primarily attributed to both mo-
bility degradation and an increase in threshold voltage. Notably, the energy per transition,
ET , includes the energy consumed by the whole level shifter during the dead-time period,
which explains its high value compared to level shifting circuits with no dead-time ap-
plied at the input.

    Additional simulations are performed at 125◦ C and a supply voltage of 2.8 V to as-
sess the impact of process and mismatch variations on circuit delays and energy con-
sumption. As shown in Fig. 4.10 (a) and (b), the dead-time varies significantly due to
these variations, shifting from the input pre-set value to the top-level value by an average

                                               158
Figure 4.11: Proposed multiple-output level shifting circuits for the system implementation in
             Figure 2. (a) Continuous level shifter with embedded dead-time control. (b) Level
             shifter in (a) with cross-coupled PMOS pairs to improve output voltage swing. All
             devices’ dimensions are in µm.


of approximately 27 ns with a 3σ spread of around 8 ns. Furthermore, the propagation
delay shown in Fig. 4.10 (c) and calculated as (tpr + tpf )/2, shows a variation of 4 ns
around an average of 14.5 ns. The energy consumption follows a trend similar to that of
the propagation delay.

4.3.2    Continuous Active-Coupled Voltage Level-Shifter
The cross-coupled level shifter circuit discussed in the previous section is well-suited
for applications requiring single or dual outputs with stringent power budget constraints,
such as implantable devices and memory circuits. While additional circuit techniques
proposed in [138] and [140] can address asymmetry and enhance speed, these solutions
are not feasible for applications requiring multiple outputs with precise dead-time control.
For such applications, minimizing propagation delay and reducing dead-time variations
across PVT conditions is critical. To meet these requirements, a continuous active level
shifter circuit is proposed in Fig. 4.11 (a).

   This circuit can be designed entirely with N-type devices, making it significantly
faster than the cross-coupled level shifter. It can operate as a single-input, multiple-
output circuit, ideal for systems like the one in Fig. 4.2 (a). Alternatively, it can function
as a differential-input, differential-output circuit, offering architectural flexibility. This

                                                159
adaptability makes it an excellent choice for driving a stack of switches while minimizing
circuit area and simplifying device sizing across all process corners.

   The circuit operates by distributing the total voltage, nVDD , equally across the NMOS
devices in both high and low states of the input signal, IN. When IN1 transitions low
to ground (GND), the gate-source voltage of device M1 increases, while its drain-source
voltage decreases from 2VDD towards VDD . This transition turns on the next device in the
stack, M3 , initiating a cascading effect through the stack until the top device, M2n−1 , is
activated. At this stage, the total voltage nVDD is evenly divided across the drain-source
of all devices in the stack. Conversely, when IN1 transitions high to VDD , device M1 enters
a high-impedance state, causing its drain voltage to rise towards 2VDD . The remaining
devices in the stack follow a similar mechanism, ensuring that the drain-source voltage
across each device remains constant at one supply voltage. Therefore, the devices operate
in the saturation region when the input signal is low and in the cut-off region when the
input signal is high. When the input is low, the drain voltages drop toward the gate
voltage for each device, causing the devices to behave as diode-connected transistors
with a resistance of 1/gm .

   The circuit’s power consumption is directly influenced by the size of devices in the
stack. In the proposed application, where high-capacity batteries serve as the power
source, the power drawn by the level shifter to improve speed, symmetry, and extend the
voltage operating range can be well justified. However, if a specific power consump-
tion limit is required, the W /L aspect ratio of the switches can be determined under the
assumption that the devices operate in the active region:


                              W              Pcond
                                =      2     ′ · (V
                                                              ,                        (4.5)
                              L   2n ·VDD · Knd    DD −Vtnd )

   where n is the number of battery taps or supplies, Pcond is the total DC power con-
           ′ is the process constant defined as the oxide capacitance per unit area multi-
sumption, Knd
plied by the electron mobility, VDD is the supply voltage, and Vtnd is the threshold voltage
of the NDMOS device.

   Although this circuit significantly improves speed and reduces the layout area of the
level shifter, the output voltages still suffer from a reduced voltage swing of approxi-
mately one threshold voltage. This limitation may compromise operation under process



                                            160
and temperature variations, as well as low battery voltages. To achieve a rail-to-rail
voltage swing, the circuit shown in Fig. 4.11 (b) is proposed. This design incorporates
minimum-size cross-coupled PMOS pairs to pull-up the voltages at each output node to
their corresponding supply rail. Furthermore, the circuit in Fig. 4.11 (b) has been mod-
ularized by removing the dead-time circuit and using the drains of the topmost NDMOS
switches as output terminals. This modularization enables a scalable design methodol-
ogy, making it suitable for systems with a large number of battery modules.

   The dead-time generator can be integrated into each sub-module gate driver circuit
or adjusted at the input of the level shifter, as discussed in Section II. The proposed fully
differential approach reduces the area of the gate driver circuit, which is particularly ben-
eficial in high-voltage CMOS processes where isolation rings occupy substantial layout
area.

   The circuit in Fig. 4.11(b) is simulated with post-layout parasitics for n = 7 to evaluate
its performance under the same operating conditions described in Section III-B. The tran-
sient output voltages and branch currents under typical operating conditions are shown
in Fig. 4.12(a) and (b), respectively. The output voltages demonstrate significant im-
provement, with the dead-time duration remaining nearly constant from the input to the
top-level output. A falling-edge delay of 0.8 ns is observed from the input to the out-
put waveforms, which is approximately 21 times lower than the rising-edge delay of the
cross-coupled level shifter. The level shifter draws a DC current of around 15 mA, which
can be significantly reduced depending on the maximum propagation delay requirements
of the circuit. The input and output waveforms, VOUT7 and VOUT1 are simulated across
the four process corners at temperatures of −40◦ C and 125◦ C. The maximum observed
delay was 4 ns, occurring at the Slow N-FET and Fast P-FET corner. This is approxi-
mately 9.5 times lower than the delay of the cross-coupled level shifter. (review analysis
below) The continuous active level shifter circuit demonstrates distinct trends for prop-
agation delay and energy per transition with temperature and process variations. As
shown in Fig. 4.13 (a) and (b), the propagation delay is highest for the Slow NMOS cor-
ner across all temperatures and supply voltages. The energy consumption per transition
(ET ) depends on the variation in the dead-time window and the current drawn within that
time window. For higher supply voltages and higher temperatures, the maximum combi-
nation of current and dead-time window is observed at the Fast NDMOS/Slow PDMOS

process corner, as shown in Fig. 4.13(c) and (d). The Fast NDMOS corner contributes to
high current consumption due to its high transconductance (gm ), while the Slow PDMOS
corner causes an expansion of the dead-time period, resulting in the maximum ET . Con-

effect: the current drawn is minimized by the reduced gm of the Slow NDMOS, and the
dead-time period is shortened due to the fast PDMOS pull-up. Overall, the NDMOS con-
trols both the current drawn from the supply and the size of the dead-time window, while

             show the variations in input-level and top-level dead time duration, respectively. (c)

the PDMOS contributes mainly to the size of the dead-time window.

    To evaluate the 3σ variations in dead-time, propagation delay, and energy per transi-
tion due to process and mismatch variations, Monte Carlo simulations are conducted at
a temperature of 125◦ C and a supply voltage of 2.8 V. The resulting plots are shown in
Fig. 4.14.

    The dead-time exhibits significant improvement with minimal variation around the
average value for both VOUT1 and VOUT7 waveforms. The average dead-time decreases
from 3.7 ns to 2.5 ns, primarily due to the dominance of the falling-edge delay over the
rising-edge delay. This results in a slightly narrower dead-time window.

    Additionally, the propagation delay has an average value of 0.87 ns, with a 3σ
spread of approximately 0.5 ns, demonstrating a 16x improvement compared to the cross-
coupled level shifter circuit.

    The energy per transition shown in Fig. 4.14(d), is also observed to be approximately
2x lower than that of the conventional circuit. However, the standard deviation of ET is
noticeably higher in the continuous level shifter. This increased variation is attributed
to the fluctuations in the current drawn by the circuit, which arise from variations in the
transconductance, gm . These gm variations are caused by process-induced changes in
mobility and threshold voltages.

                                               163
Figure 4.15: Proposed multiple-output capacitive level shifter with embedded dead-time control.
             All devices’ dimensions are in µm.


4.3.3    Multiple-Output Capacitive Level-Shifter
The proposed capacitive-coupled multiple-output level shifter is shown in Fig. 4.15. This
circuit is designed as a self-switched, or commutated, charge pump and extends the con-
ventional single-output capacitive level shifter by utilizing two low-voltage capacitors
and a cross-coupled P-FET or N-FET pair at each level-shifting stage. By incorporating
CMOS buffers to isolate consecutive stages, the design effectively eliminates the need
for large inter-stage pumping capacitors. This feature enables seamless scalability to
support any number of voltage levels, making the circuit highly adaptable for multi-level
voltage-shifting applications.

   The P-FET cross-coupled version, while effective, requires dedicated charging cir-
cuits to ensure a reliable start-up process [143]. In contrast, the N-FET cross-coupled ver-
sion inherently guarantees safe start-up, as the body diodes of the N-FETs assist in pre-
charging the capacitors, initiating the pumping process without additional circuitry [137].


   The input control signal, IN, is split into two out-of-phase signals, IN1 and IN2 , which
have a non-overlapping duration of R1C1 . These signals are connected to the bottom
plates of the pumping capacitors C1 and C2 , driving the level-shifting process.

   The detailed state transitions for all input signal combinations are illustrated in
Fig. 4.16: The detailed state transitions for all input signal combinations are illustrated
in Fig. 4.16:

φ1 : The capacitors are charged via the body diodes of the cross-coupled NMOS pair to
VDD − VBD , where VBD is the forward body diode voltage drop. Therefore, the charge

                                             164
              Figure 4.16: State transitions in the capacitive level shifter circuit




stored on the capacitors is given by:


                                     Q1 = (VDD −VBD ) ·C1                               (4.6)


                                       Qp1 = −VBD ·Cp1                                  (4.7)

φ2 : The bottom plate of capacitor C1 is pulled up to VDD , turning on the switch M1 ,
which, in turn, charges capacitor C2 to VDD . The charge stored on the capacitors in this
phase is given by:
                                     Q1 = (Vout1 −VDD ) ·C1                             (4.8)

                                  Qp1 = (Vout1 −VDD ) ·Cp1                              (4.9)

Consequently, the charge redistributes such that:


      (Vout1 −VDD ) ·C1 + (Vout1 −VDD ) ·Cp1 = (VDD −VBD ) ·C1 −VBD ·Cp1               (4.10)


resulting in the following voltage across the capacitor Cp1 :

                                               2C1 +Cp1
                               Vout1 = VDD ·            −VBD                           (4.11)
                                               C1 +Cp1

For C1 ≫ Cp1 , this simplifies to:


                                      Vout1 = 2VDD −VBD .                              (4.12)

                                               165
φ3 : Both capacitors are pulled down again to GND, with both M1 and M2 are cut-off,
allowing the capacitors to discharge to VDD −VBD .

φ4 : Capacitor C1 charges through device M2 , which is activated by the top plate of C2
after settling to 2VDD −VBD . The same procedure outlined above can be followed to find
the voltage Vout2

   This behaviour is consistent across all voltage domains, as the stages are electrically
isolated by the CMOS buffers. To prevent static currents and increase the speed, the
coupling capacitors C1 and C2 must be sized such that the resulting swing is sufficiently
below the threshold voltage of the buffering devices.

   The simulation results for the capacitive-coupled multiple-output level shifter are pre-
sented in Fig. 17 to Fig. 19. Starting with the time-domain output waveforms, Fig. 17(a)
and (b) illustrate the high symmetry observed in the rising and falling edges of the out-
puts, maintaining a nearly constant dead-time window of approximately 3 ns. The typ-
ical rising edge delay is 2.1 ns, which is approximately 8 times lower than that of the
conventional cross-coupled level shifter under similar operating conditions of voltage,
temperature, and load.

   The output voltage swing is demonstrated to be less than 0.3 V. This swing is suf-
ficiently small under all process corners to ensure zero static power consumption. Fur-
thermore, the circuit exhibits superior performance across temperature and process vari-
ations, with a maximum delay of 4.5 ns observed at the slow/slow process corner and
125°C temperature. This represents a 9-fold improvement over the conventional design.

   A temperature sweep reveals that the propagation delays increase at all process cor-
ners due to reduced carrier mobility with rising temperature. Similarly, the energy con-
sumption per transition follows the same trend as the propagation delay but achieves sig-
nificantly lower values compared to the other designs, recording up to a 16-fold reduction
under worst-case conditions.

   The random variations in dead-time, propagation delay, and energy per transition due
to process and mismatch effects also demonstrate excellent performance. The dead-time
varies minimally, ranging from 3.65 ns to 4.05 ns across the input and output ports, owing
to the inherent symmetry of the circuit’s operation. Additionally, the propagation delay
exhibits a 3σ variation of only 0.7 ns around an average value of 2.89 ns. The energy per
transition also displays negligible random distribution variations, further underscoring

4.4       Fabrication and Experimental Results
The proposed level-shifter circuits were designed and fabricated using a 130-nm BCD
process. The continuous level-shifter was integrated as part of the system architecture

                                                      167
Figure 4.19: Monte Carlo post-layout process and mismatch simulations of the capacitive-
             coupled level shifter circuit at a temperature of 125°C and a supply voltage of 2.8 V.

             spectively. (c) illustrates the variations in total propagation delay, and (d) depicts
             the variation in energy per transition.


illustrated in Fig. 4.2(b), whereas the capacitive level-shifter was implemented as a stan-
dalone circuit comprising five voltage levels (i.e., five battery stages).

    The layout of the complete chip, including both circuits and their wire-bonding inter-
faces, is shown in Fig. 4.20. Aluminium wire bonds with a diameter of 33 µm were used
for the continuous level-shifter circuit, while gold wire bonds with a diameter of 17 µm
were employed for the capacitive level-shifter. The chip was mounted on a custom two-
layer PCB with dedicated wire-bonding pads and connected to a test motherboard via a
PCB edge connector. The motherboard was powered by five independent power supplies,
providing voltages ranging from 2 V to 4.3 V. The complete testbench setup is depicted in
Fig. 4.21. The continuous level-shifter circuit was driven by a single control signal with
a 50% duty cycle. Internally, this signal was divided into two complementary phases with
a fixed dead-time and used to control half-bridge power train submodules, as discussed in
the simulation section. The outputs of these submodules were directly monitored using
an eight-channel Teledyne LeCroy oscilloscope.

    In contrast, the capacitive level-shifter was driven by two complementary logic sig-
nals generated by an FPGA within the B-Box microcontroller. These signals featured
a controlled overlap period and were directly connected to the input capacitors of the
level-shifter circuit.

    The measured output waveforms of the continuous level-shifter are presented in

Fig. 4.22. Measurements were performed under supply voltages of 2.5 V, 3.3 V, and
4.2 V at switching frequencies of 10 kHz and 1 MHz, which correspond to typical op-
erating conditions for battery balancing applications. The waveforms exhibit high signal
integrity across all test conditions, with propagation delays of approximately 2 ns for both
rising and falling edges at a nominal supply voltage of 3.3 V.

    For the capacitive level-shifter, results were obtained over a wider supply voltage
range of 2 V, 3.3 V, and 4.3 V, and at switching frequencies of 1 kHz and 1 MHz, as shown
in Fig. 4.23. Under a typical operating voltage of 3.3 V, the measured propagation delays
were 70 ns for the rising edge and 20 ns for the falling edge.

    The discrepancy observed in the measured delay values arises primarily from the
limitations of the oscilloscope. For the continuous level-shifter, the output signals were
captured after the strong CMOS driver stages within the power train, resulting in mea-
surements that more accurately reflect the actual circuit delays. These outputs are sig-
nificantly less affected by the oscilloscope’s input capacitance and bandwidth limitations
due to the strong drive strength of the final stages.

    In contrast, the capacitive level-shifter employs relatively weak inter-stage buffers
(CMOS inverters) to minimize coupling capacitance within the design. As a result, the
measured delays are heavily influenced by the additional loading introduced by the os-
cilloscope probes. These probes introduce capacitance that degrades the response of

                                               169
Figure 4.21: Experimental testbench showing the test board with the chip board plugged into it
             with five voltage supplies for inputs and multiple voltage probes for single-ended
             voltage measurements of different outputs




Figure 4.22: Measured output voltage waveforms of the stacked half-bridge sub-modules with
             stacked continuos level-shifter and dead-time generator



the weak buffers, causing the observed delays to be significantly longer than the actual
intrinsic circuit delays. This behaviour can be predicted in post-layout simulations by
incorporating a probe model that includes bandwidth and capacitive loading effects.

                                             170
        Figure 4.23: Measured output voltage waveforms of the capacitive level shifter




   Table III provides a comparison of the level shifters investigated in this paper with
other related works under typical operating conditions. All the proposed circuits generate
7×2 complementary output signals, with a dead-time duration configurable at the input
of the level shifter. The designs use 5 V NMOS (L = 0.37 µm) and PMOS (L = 0.32 µm)
high-voltage devices, as demonstrated in Fig. 4.3 and Fig. 4.4.

   To ensure accurate simulation results, the layouts are implemented using macro de-
vices, with accurately modelled junction diodes. This design approach significantly in-
creases the area compared to implementations that use low-voltage devices placed within
isolation rings, where placement and area usage are more optimized. For example, in
the continuous-level shifter, all devices can share the same high-voltage N-buried ring,
which can be connected to the highest battery voltage in the stack.

   Although the capacitive level shifter occupies a larger area compared to other de-
signs, it inherently includes a compact driver stage, which serves as the first stage of
the gate driver for the power switch. The capacitors used in the capacitive level shifter
are 1 pF, implemented with metal-on-metal (MoM) devices. Since low-voltage capaci-
tors are used, higher-density metal-insulator-metal (MIM) capacitors can also be utilized
to further optimize area. As the circuits are designed for multi-output gate drivers in
battery-balancing applications, the dynamic power consumption remains low. This is be-
cause balancing systems operate at relatively low frequencies, typically in the range of a
few hundred kilohertz.

4.5           Conclusions
Three voltage level-shifting circuits with multiple non-overlapping differential output
signals have been proposed for chip-scale battery-balancing applications. A modified
version of the conventional cross-coupled active level shifter is critically evaluated for
speed, area, power consumption, and robustness against process, voltage, and tempera-
ture (PVT) variations. This design is compared with two alternative approaches: a contin-
uous active-coupled level shifter and a capacitive-coupled level shifter. To support dead-
time control strategies, the proposed high-speed level shifters are designed to minimize
variations in the fixed dead-time window while maintaining excellent PVT robustness.
The circuits exhibit propagation delays ranging from around 2 ns to 4 ns with minimal
variations by process and mismatch. In comparison, the cross-coupled circuit shows a
significantly higher maximum delay of around 38 ns for a 7-level module. Among the
proposed designs, the continuous level shifter achieves the smallest area and can option-
ally operate in a non-differential configuration, halving the required hardware. It offers
low delay, a wide voltage range (5 to 1.8 V), and a DC current consumption of approxi-
mately 15 mA, which can be reduced depending on speed requirements. Conversely, the
capacitive-coupled level shifter consumes zero static current, has low dynamic power,
and provides low delay performance. Additionally, it supports a wide operating voltage
range, enabling compatibility with a broader range of battery types for drivers or battery
balancers.




                                                                      172
Chapter 5


Integrated Capacitively Coupled
Modular Half-Bridge Battery
Balancing


5.1     Introduction

The battery management system (BMS) typically comprises several main components,
such as the cell monitoring unit (CMU), control and management unit, protection module,
and communication module. The control and management unit regulates functions like
cell balancing, charging/discharging, and thermal control, relying on data from the CMU.
This unit utilizes either active or passive balancing techniques to maintain optimal battery
pack performance [181].

   Batteries connected in series often exhibit varying electrical characteristics, such as
capacitance, internal resistance, and self-discharge rates [44], [182]. These inconsisten-
cies can be further exacerbated by uneven self-discharge caused by differences in intercell
and ambient temperatures, leakage currents, and the inherent chemical properties of the
cells [44], [182]. In multilevel converter systems, the utilization of series-connected cells
varies based on the specific modulation strategy used to generate the desired output volt-
age waveform [183]. Over time, this uneven usage results in imbalance among the cells,
causing inefficient use of the battery pack’s total capacity during drive system operation
and potentially triggering premature shutdowns during the charging process. Therefore,
to ensure high system performance and longevity, the BMS must include a balancing
mechanism—either passive or active—to equalize the state of charge (SOC) among in-
dividual cells [44]. Passive balancing dissipates excess energy as heat, resulting in zero
energy efficiency. In contrast, active balancing redistributes energy between cells, achiev-
ing higher efficiency and faster balancing speeds. This is typically accomplished through
circuits employing numerous switches and intermediary components such as capacitors,
inductors, or transformers [45].

   Despite their superior performance, active balancing systems face significant chal-
lenges. Their complexity, cost, and the need for advanced control strategies hinder their
widespread adoption. Furthermore, integrating numerous switches, gate drivers, and pas-
sive components within a compact battery pack is difficult, particularly as high-speed bal-
ancing demands minimal routing resistance and stray inductance. These parasitics limit
the achievable switching frequency and thereby degrade balancing performance. Conse-
quently, the cost and spatial constraints associated with active balancing circuits restrict
their practical implementation in systems with large cell strings, such as battery electric
vehicles, unmanned aerial vehicles, or multilevel converter-based drive systems.

   For more details on circuit implementation costs, Table. 5.1 summarizes a range of
capacitive-based battery balancing circuits, detailing their basic electrical components,
including capacitors, switches and gate drivers (GDs) as a function of the the number of
battery cells, n. These circuits eliminate the need for inductors, transformers, and diodes,
while also minimizing reliance on cell voltage sensors. This category includes single-
tiered switched-capacitor (STSC) [79], single switched-capacitor (SSC) [184], double-
tiered switched-capacitor (DTSC) [80], chain-structured switched-capacitor (Chain-SC)
[81], series-parallel switched-capacitor (SP-SC) [82], star switched-capacitor (Star-SC)


           Table 5.1: Components of Capacitive-Based Battery Balancing Circuits

                        Ref.    Topology   Capacitors   Switches/GDs
                        [79]       STSC      n−1            2n
                        [80]       DTSC    3(n − 1)/2       2n
                        [184]      SSC         1           n+5
                        [81]    Chain-SC       n           2n + 4
                        [82]       SP-SC       n            4n
                        [83]    Star-SC        n            2n
                        [86]       Delta   n(n − 1)/2       2n
                        [85]       Mesh       2n            2n
                        [88]    CS2CAB        2n            4n


                                            174
[83], as well as delta-structured [86], mesh-structured [85], and complementary star
switched-capacitor topologies (CS2CAB) [88]. Each topology presents trade-offs in bal-
ancing speed, component voltage stress, efficiency, and overall circuit complexity.
The complexity of this class of balancers is largely influenced by the number of
switches and the gate-driving techniques employed. The gate-driving approaches com-
monly used in battery balancing circuit designs are illustrated in Fig. 5.1(a)–(c). The
first technique, shown in Fig. 5.1(a), employs a multi-winding transformer to provide
an isolated gate-source drive signal for each MOSFET. This approach is commonly
used [185],[104],[177], and offers robust gate-driving performance with electrical iso-
lation. This isolation decouples the control circuitry from the battery cell voltages and
balancing paths, enhancing system reliability under varying operating conditions. How-
ever, the main drawback of the transformer-based method is its bulky size, high imple-
mentation cost, and assembly complexity, factors that become particularly problematic
as the number of cells increases. For this reason, two alternative techniques are also
widely used for isolating gate driver signals in battery balancing applications. One such
alternative relies on opto-coupler-based isolation, which serves the same purpose of de-
coupling the gate driver’s control logic from the reference potentials of the sub-module
circuits (which vary according to the battery voltages). Low-current opto-coupler ICs can
provide this signal-level isolation effectively [186]. A non-isolated bootstrap (BS) gate
driver ICs for driving the half-bridge legs in each sub-module is used. In this case, a ded-
icated voltage generation IC is required to power the BS driver. As shown in Fig. 5.1(b),
this voltage source can be implemented using a non-isolated boost converter, which sup-




Figure 5.1: Conventional driving techniques for battery balancing circuits implemented with dis-
            crete gate drivers: (a) multi-winding transformer gate driver, (b) opto-coupler gate
            driver with a boost power supply integrated circuit (IC), and (c) opto-coupler gate
            driver directly powered by a battery cell compliant with the switch voltage rating.

                                             175
plies sufficient gate-drive voltage to the switches. This is particularly important when
the required gate-source voltage (typically 10 –15 V for discrete MOSFETs) exceeds the
voltage available from individual battery cells, such as lithium-ion cells, which usually
operate at lower voltages. If the battery chemistry supports higher voltages, such as 12 V
lead-acid cells, the circuit shown in Fig. 5.1(c) becomes a viable and more efficient op-
tion. In this case, the gate driver can be powered directly from the battery, eliminating the
need for an isolated or step-up power supply and thereby simplifying the design while
reducing overall system cost [186].

   Implementing active balancing circuits with discrete components increases sys-
tem cost and limits high-speed operation due to routing parasitics and assembly
constraints—particularly in large-scale applications. The complexity of gate-driving
schemes for high switch counts can further impede practical deployment and reduce the
expected gains in speed and efficiency. To overcome these challenges, active balancing
circuits can be implemented using low-voltage, cost-effective silicon switches integrated
at the chip level. Chip-scale integration provides several benefits, including high-speed
operation, reduced parasitics and leakage losses, faster equalization times, and a substan-
tially smaller circuit footprint. Compact mounting of these integrated modules alongside
battery cells further minimizes wiring complexity and power losses, enhancing both sys-
tem efficiency and power density. Additionally, tailoring device specifications to the
battery-cell voltage levels optimizes silicon area utilization and reduces manufacturing
costs, making this approach well-suited for large-scale battery systems.

   This chapter addresses these challenges by presenting the first on-chip high-current
battery balancing circuit module, developed using 130 nm Bipolar-CMOS-DMOS (BCD)
technology. The design features stacked half-bridge sub-modules interfaced with off-
chip balancing capacitors arranged in a star configuration, demonstrating the circuit’s
effectiveness in balancing battery cells. The proposed design is scalable on-chip up to
the technology’s breakdown limits and can be further extended at the board level due to
its modular and scalable architecture, making it suitable for larger battery strings.

   The rest of the chapter is organized as follows: Section 5.2 presents the proposed inte-
grated balancer, including circuit analysis, modelling of switching and conduction power
losses, and circuit design and implementations. Section 5.3 covers the experimental vali-
dation of the balancer chip, including chip assembly, characterization, and a comparative


                                            176
discussion with existing works. Finally, the conclusions are summarized in Section 5.4.




5.2     Proposed Integrated Battery Balancer
The proposed integrated battery balancer topology is shown in Fig. 5.2. The on-chip
active part is designed to support a broad range of external component configurations,
including single-tier and double-tier structures, as well as more complex networks such as
star, mesh, and delta configurations, as summarized in Table 5.1. Each topology involves
trade-offs in terms of balancing speed, implementation cost, assembly complexity, and
the voltage rating requirements of capacitors and switches.

   To highlight the key features of the on-chip multistacked half-bridge architecture, the
star configuration proposed in [83] is adopted, as depicted in Fig. 5.2. Compared to mesh
and delta networks, the star topology offers lower implementation complexity while pro-
viding faster balancing performance than traditional double-tier switched capacitor and
single-tier switched capacitor configurations. This benefit arises from the availability of
direct charge transfer paths between all cells, enabling an any-cell-to-any-cell (AC2AC)
energy transfer mode [83, 84].

   The circuit operates in two distinct phases. When the control signal is high, the
high-side switches (M1 to M2n−1 ) are turned on during the first half of the switching
period (State I). In the second half, the low-side switches (M2 to M2n ) are activated
(State II). This complementary switching scheme enables efficient and balanced energy
redistribution among all cells in the stack.

   This model accurately captures the circuit behaviour under both slow and fast switch-
ing regimes, as characterized for half-bridge capacitive balancer circuits in [84]. When
the switching period, Tsw , is approximately ten times the circuit’s time constant, ReCe ,
where the resistances of the switch and other elements are negligible, the system operates
at the boundary of the slow switching limit. Figure 5.4 illustrates the behaviour predicted
by the analytical model over a single switching cycle with a period of 200 µs, where top-
most cell voltage, V1 , is set to 4.3 V and the remaining cells are set to 3.3 V. If the switch-
ing frequency is further reduced, the average balancing current decreases, leading to
diminished balancing efficiency and slower balancing. Conversely, when Tsw < 10ReCe ,
the circuit transitions to the fast switching limit, as shown in Fig. 5.5. In this regime, the
average balancing current increases with switching frequency and is ultimately limited
by the circuit resistance, provided the switching frequency remains below the resonance
frequency of the balancing capacitor. Beyond this point, the path becomes inductive,
resulting in a decline in balancing current and a corresponding reduction in balancing
efficiency.


5.2.2    Balancer Power Loss Modelling

The primary power loss components include dynamic switching losses and conduction
losses. Dynamic power losses arise from the charging and discharging of the switches’
parasitic capacitances, namely the gate-source (Cgs ), gate-drain (Cgd ), and drain-source
(Cds ) capacitances, as highlighted in Fig. 5.2. The total dynamic power loss can be ex-

    -5                         -1.4                                               5.2

5.2.3           Circuit Implementations

The complete schematic of the proposed balancer, including the off-chip balancing ca-
pacitors, is shown in Fig. 5.7. The circuit consists of four stacked half-bridge converter
sub-modules, denoted as HBC1 to HBC4 . For implementation, 5 V NDMOS and PDMOS
switches are employed, which are isolated within an N-buried layer to enhance perfor-
mance and reliability. Owing to this isolation capability, the design can be scaled on-chip
to support up to eight sub-modules, enabling the balancing of eight battery cells. Further-
more, it can be extended at the board level due to its modular and scalable architecture,
making it well-suited for larger battery strings.

   The circuit is driven by an input control signal with a duty cycle of 0.5. This signal
is divided into two non-overlapping phases with an inserted dead-time interval and is
subsequently fed into a voltage level shifter, which generates multiple differential output

                                               184
Figure 5.7: Top-level architecture of the capacitive-based balancing circuit, including off-chip
            balancing capacitors and the detailed implementation of stacked half-bridge sub-
            modules using dual-NMOS power trains, with a multi-output voltage level shifter
            and input dead-time generator.



signals.

   The dead-time generation circuit comprises two devices, Mdt1 and Mdt2 , along with
resistors Rdt1 and Rdt2 , capacitors Cdt1 and Cdt2 , and CMOS inverters. The duration of
dead-time is determined by the Rdt1Cdt1 time constant, which delays the rising edge of the
signals to achieve the desired dead-time. Various NAND- or NOR-based non-overlapping
clock generation techniques may also be employed.

   The output signals from the dead-time circuit are then level-shifted using a multiple-
output voltage level shifter. This circuit consists of stacked NDMOS devices (M1 to M7
and M2 to M8 ). The circuit operates by distributing the total voltage, Vcell1 , equally across
the NDMOS devices in both high and low states of the input signal, IN. When IN1 tran-
sitions from Vcell4 to VSS , the gate-source voltage of M1 increases while its drain-source
voltage decreases from Vcell3 to Vcell4 . This causes M1 to turn on, which in turn activates
M3 in a cascading sequence through the stack until the topmost device, M7 , is triggered.
At this point, Vcell1 is evenly divided across the drain-source terminals of all devices in the
stack. Conversely, when IN1 transitions from VSS to Vcell4 , M1 enters a high-impedance

                                             185
state, causing its drain voltage to rise towards Vcell3 . The remaining devices in the stack
respond similarly, maintaining a constant drain-source voltage across each device equiv-
alent to the supply voltage. Thus, the devices operate in the saturation region during the
low state of the input signal and in the cut-off region during the high state. When the
input is low, the drain voltages approach the gate voltages of their respective devices,
making the devices function as diode-connected transistors with an effective resistance
of 1/gm , where gm is the transconductance of the device. The other branch of the level
shifter operates in a similar manner but with an opposite phase.

   The power consumption of the circuit is determined by the dimensions of the devices
in the stack. In applications where high-capacity batteries serve as the power source,
a small DC current in the level shifter is justifiable, as it helps minimize layout area,
enhance speed and symmetry, and extend the voltage operating range. This offers a dis-
tinct advantage over multiple-output cross-coupled level shifters, which typically exhibit
higher delay during the pull-up phase.

   If a specific power constraint must be met, the W /L ratio of the switches can be
adjusted accordingly, assuming the devices operate in the active region:

                               W                 PDC
                                 =       2      ′
                                                                    ,                  (5.28)
                               L   2n ·Vcell · Knd · (Vcell −Vtnd )

where n is the number of battery taps, PDC is the total DC power consumption of the level
          ′ represents the process constant (the product of the oxide capacitance per unit
shifter, Knd
area and electron mobility), Vcell is the nominal cell voltage, and Vtnd is the threshold
voltage of the NDMOS device.

   The output signals of the level shifter, VLS R1 ...VLS R4 and VLS L1 ...VLS L4 , are subse-
quently used to control the operation of the half-bridge sub-modules gate driver circuits.

   To maintain waveform polarity and phase alignment, preserve the pre-set dead-time
duration, and ensure proper circuit operation, the low-side and high-side control paths
are designed to be identical. As a result, floating voltage level shifter circuits (LS) are
symmetrically employed for both control paths. For the low side, the LS blocks primarily
serve to equalize delays. Each LS block utilizes a conventional cross-coupled architec-
ture, as described in [138].

   The high-side switches receive their gate overdrive voltages from the outputs of the


                                               186
upper sub-modules. For instance, the output of HBC1 , denoted as VOUT1 , supplies the
gate overdrive voltage for the high-side switch of HBC2 . For the high-side switch in the
uppermost sub-module, a floating gate overdrive voltage supply must be generated using
techniques such as a bootstrap capacitor or a charge pump circuit [127]. Alternatively, the
entire stage can be implemented using PDMOS–NDMOS switch pairs, which eliminates
the need for a floating supply at the expense of increased area due to the larger size of the
PDMOS device.
The low-side switches, by contrast, are directly driven by gate voltages derived from the
same battery domain they are regulating. This advantage is enabled by the use of low-
voltage NDMOS devices, which, unlike discrete switches, can be effectively turned on
by gate overdrive voltages within the typical Li-ion battery voltage range.
Finally, the circuit outputs VOUT1 through VOUT4 are connected to off-chip capacitors C1
through C4 , enabling charge redistribution for active battery cell balancing.



5.3     Experimental Results
The proposed multistacked half-bridge circuit was fabricated using a 130-nm BCD pro-
cess featuring 5 V high-voltage devices. To improve area efficiency and minimize rout-
ing resistance, power pads have been placed both adjacent to the devices using a circuit-
under-pad (CUP) structures and directly above their active regions using a bond-on-active
(BOA) pad structure. Since BOA is not commonly applied in low-dielectric constant, k,
technologies, this layout also serves to evaluate its reliability under various wire-bonding
techniques, including both wedge and ball bonding.
Both pad configurations were used to interface the chip with a custom-designed printed
circuit board (PCB). In both cases, the assembled systems were fully functional, with no
observed yield or reliability issues attributable to bonding over the active regions of the
power switches or over the CUP structures. The top-level layout and a micrograph of the
wire-bonded chip are shown in Fig. 5.8 and Fig. 5.9, respectively. The total chip area is
3.4 mm2 .

   To validate the chip’s functionality as a battery voltage balancer, the test setup shown
in Fig. 5.10 was established. The wire-bonded chip was mounted on a two-layer PCB
(daughterboard) that includes one 0.1 µF and two 22 µF decoupling capacitors across
the four battery terminals. For rapid characterization, a 50-contact card edge connector

control is provided by an FPGA signal, operating over a frequency range of 1 kHz to
5 MHz. An additional power supply is used to deliver the gate-drive voltage for the top-

approaches discussed in Section II.

parallel ceramic capacitors were connected to the output of each half-bridge in a star con-
figuration. These capacitors exhibit a self-resonant frequency of approximately 200 kHz
and an equivalent series resistance of around 10 mΩ.

   To fully characterize the proposed battery balancer chip prototype, a comprehensive

outputs of the half-bridge sub-modules are shown in Fig. 5.11 at switching frequencies
of 1 kHz and 1 MHz, with input supply voltages ranging from 2.5 V to 4.2 V. The corre-

tions are presented in Fig. 5.12. Furthermore, the measured bus voltage at the common
plate of the balancing capacitors, denoted as Vbus , is illustrated in Fig. 5.13, also under

    These basic experimental results are in agreement with the theoretical model. Since
the balancing capacitors share a common, electrically isolated plate, the net charge across
all capacitors remains zero:

                                   n                    n

As a result, during each switching phase, the bus voltages Vbus1 and Vbus2 can be expressed

These results demonstrate the capability of the proposed chip to operate effectively
across a wide range of input voltages and switching frequencies. This enables its use as a
battery balancer to achieve charge equalization. To further evaluate the chip’s balancing
performance, four test cases are considered. In each case, one input source is swept from
2.4 V to 4.2 V—covering the full voltage range of typical Li-ion battery cells—while the
remaining inputs are held constant at 3.3 V. For instance, Case 1 corresponds to the sce-
nario where the bottom-most cell undergoes the voltage sweep, while the others remain
at 3.3 V. Conversely, Case 4 involves sweeping the top-most cell, with the remaining cells
fixed at 3.3 V.


    The average balancing current sourced or sunk by each supply across a switching
frequency range of 8 kHz to 500 kHz has been measured and shown in Fig. 5.14. As
expected, the average current increases approximately linearly with the voltage imbal-
ance at a given switching frequency. Additionally, the balancing current increases with
the switching frequency. For instance, when the topmost or bottommost cell is at 4.2 V
(corresponding to the maximum voltage imbalance), the average current increases from
approximately 0.5 A at 8 kHz to about 1.25 A at 250 kHz and 500 kHz. Beyond the
125 kHz–250 kHz range, the balancing current either plateaus or slightly varies, depend-
ing on the scenario. These variations are attributed to mismatches in the PCB routing
paths of the balancing capacitors and supply lines, as well as to inconsistencies intro-
duced by measurement cables and current probes.

    At low switching frequencies, below approximately 32 kHz, the system operates in
the slow-switching limit (SSL), where the equivalent impedance is dominated by the bal-

                                            194
ancing capacitors. According to the switching limit impedance analyses in [84], the SSL
resistance is approximately 0.66 Ω for the given parameters. As the frequency increases,
the system transitions to the fast-switching limit (FSL), where the full switching cycle is
utilized for the charging and discharging of the balancing capacitors. In this regime, en-
ergy transfer between higher-voltage and lower-voltage cells is maximized, accelerating
the balancing process. The equivalent resistance in the FSL regime can be estimated by
dividing the voltage imbalance by the measured average balancing current. For example,
in Cases 2 and 3, the observed switched-capacitance resistance is around 0.6 Ω, implying
a total circuit resistance—including switch and routing contributions—of approximately
0.12 Ω, based on the FSL impedance model presented in [84]. Further increases in bal-
ancing current are constrained by the self-resonance of the tank formed by the total stray
inductance and the effective balancing capacitance.

   Balancing current waveforms are shown in Fig. 5.15-Fig. 5.18. These currents were
measured at frequencies ranging from 16 kHz to 250 kHz for the same input voltages
Cases explained earlier in this section.

   In Fig. 5.15 and Fig. 5.16, the topmost and bottom-most supplies deliver currents, IB4
and IB1 , that increase gradually with the switching frequency to become a unidirectional
waveform from 32 kHz upward. The circuit transitions from the slow switching limit
to the fast switching limit where the current is only limited by the equivalent switches,
routings, and parasitic resistive elements in the current paths. It can be seen that that
under this voltage imbalance scenario, the first cell mainly transfers energy to the second
neighbour cell via the mediating capacitors, and to the non-neighbour cells at the high
switching frequencies. This trend continues due to the reduction of path impedances,
until exceeding the resonance frequency, where the stray inductance of the path and com-
ponents will start impeding further increases in the value of the balancing current. In this
design, that starts around a frequency of 250 kHz.

   These current waveforms exhibit behaviour that deviates from the expected results
based on the analytical model or parasitic-free simulations. Ideally, the waveforms would
resemble those of IB1 and IB4 , shown in Fig. 5.4 and Fig. 5.5, where the current drops to
zero for at least half of the switching cycle. The discrepancy observed in the measured
current waveforms (Fig. 5.15 and Fig. 5.16) is primarily attributed to the presence of
decoupling capacitors, totalling approximately 140 µF, and cable inductance of about


                                            195
1 µH. These parasitic elements form an LC network with a resonance frequency around
13 kHz, causing the current waveforms to resemble sinusoidal signals near this frequency.
As a result, the LC circuit behaves like an integrator. Consequently, as the switching
frequency increases, the current becomes smoother and gradually approaches a DC-like
waveform, as evidenced by the measured waveforms at 250 kHz.

   The middle supply currents are higher than those of the terminal supplies at the same
switching frequencies, as shown in Fig. 5.17 and Fig. 5.18. This occurs because the
middle supplies contribute current during both switching states, whereas the topmost and
bottom-most supplies ideally conduct only during one half of the switching cycle. For
these terminal cells, there is no complementary path for current to be sourced or sunk
during the other half of the cycle, resulting in zero current during that interval and thus
lower average balancing currents.
Overall, the balancing current results demonstrate the high-speed operating capability
of the proposed chip-based balancer, enabling significantly faster balancing. This per-
formance is expected to improve further under real system integration due to reduced
parasitics and optimized board layout.


   To characterize the circuit’s efficiency, one voltage supply is set to the maximum cell
voltage of 4.2 V, while the remaining supplies are set to 3.3 V. Efficiency is evaluated
under this voltage differential and defined as the ratio of output power—i.e., the power
transferred to the lower-voltage cells—to the input power drawn from the higher-voltage
cell. Figure 5.19 presents the measured efficiency of the balancing chip as a function of
switching frequency.
At low switching frequencies, where slow-switching impedance dominates the perfor-
mance of the switched-capacitor balancer, a notable efficiency disparity is observed be-
tween the middle and terminal cells. However, this disparity diminishes as the switch-
ing frequency increases, eventually converging at high frequencies where the system ap-
proaches the fast-switching limit. Under the circuit conditions specified in Table 5.2, the
proposed balancer demonstrates significantly improved equalization performance, par-
ticularly at higher switching frequencies in the range of 100–200 kHz. Table 5.3 presents
a comparison between the proposed integrated battery balancer and other related balanc-
ing systems. The proposed design is particularly well-suited for applications involving



                                           196
  Figure 5.19: Efficiency variation with switching frequency across all imbalance conditions.


a large number of battery cells, where implementation costs typically escalate due to
the extensive use of switches, gate drivers, and complex balancing algorithms. The pro-
posed design is compact, modular, and supports high-performance switches, enabling
high switching frequencies with minimal switching losses. Other existing solutions often
rely on discrete implementations that incorporate bulky and costly gate driving compo-
nents based on transformers and opto-couplers as described in Section I.

   An integrated solution proposed in [142] utilized 50-V high-voltage PMOS and
NMOS switches but is limited to a low balancing current of 100 mA. While this approach
may be suitable for series-connected batteries with mild imbalance—caused primarily by
intrinsic cell variations—it is inadequate for addressing more severe imbalance scenar-
ios. Such scenarios are typical in multilevel converters, where selective cell usage for
output voltage synthesis results in significant operational imbalances. These applications
demand high-speed balancing circuits capable of actively transferring energy between
cells to maintain system stability and performance.

5.5     Conclusion
This chapter has proposed an on-chip integration approach for battery balancing cir-
cuits. The proposed battery balancing chip incorporates four stacked half-bridge con-
verter modules, which can be extended on-chip up to the voltage limit supported by the
semiconductor process. The design is fully modular and stackable, requiring only a sin-
gle PWM control signal in an open-loop configuration, eliminating the need for voltage
or current sensors to balance the cells. This approach offers an extremely compact solu-
tion for applications with stringent weight and size constraints, enabling easy integration
with battery cells. The balancing speed is independent of the battery string size, as the
proposed circuit can transfer energy simultaneously among all cells in the string. Op-
timisation can be further achieved by customizing the design and board layout while
minimising parasitic inductance and resistance introduced by long wires and PCB tracks.
This would maximise the low resistance advantage of the on-chip switches and enhance
their capability to operate at higher frequencies, thereby increasing balancing speed and
enabling the use of low-cost small balancing capacitors. Compared to discrete imple-

                                              200
mentations, the proposed design significantly reduces size by integrating a large number
of switches and simplifies driving requirements, eliminating the need for isolating trans-
formers or optical gate-driving solutions. Furthermore, due to the low threshold voltage
of the balancing switches, the design can support a wide range of battery technologies,
operating efficiently from approximately 2.5 V to 5 V per cell. Future work will focus on
optimizing the chip packaging to reduce total path resistance, integrating the system into
a real battery pack, and investigating other balancing capacitor configurations.




                                           201
Chapter 6


Integrated Binary-Tree Multilevel
Converter Operating at the
Battery-Cell Level


6.1     Introduction

Multilevel power converter topologies implemented with low-voltage Si Metal-Oxide-
Semiconductor Field-Effect Transistors (MOSFETs) can improve the efficiency of elec-
tric powertrains while reducing overall cost, compared to conventional two-level con-
verter implementations using Insulated-Gate Bipolar Transistors (IGBTs) or Silicon Car-
bide (SiC) MOSFETs [188]. These multilevel approaches demonstrate not only high ef-
ficiency and cost-effectiveness across different driving cycles, but also additional benefits
such as reduced electromagnetic interference (EMI) and total harmonic distortion (THD),
increased fault tolerance capability, and reduced voltage and current stress in semicon-
ductor devices, making them suitable for a wide range of electric vehicle concepts [188],
[53], [189].

   In battery-powered drive applications, such as electric vehicles, individual battery
cells, e.g., Lithium Iron Phosphate (LiFePO4 ) or lithium-ion (Li-ion) cells, can be di-
rectly interfaced with the power converter, enabling the use of high-performance, low-
voltage switches for efficient conversion. However, this approach requires a large num-
ber of switches and gate drivers to synthesize high-resolution output waveforms from
the individual cell voltages. Consequently, full on-board implementations face signifi-
cant challenges, including assembly complexity, increased switching losses and leakage,
and costly, bulky gate-driving requirements, which limit scalability in cost- and space-
constrained drive systems. Furthermore, the lack of flexibility in tailoring the current
and voltage ratings of converter switches to cell-compatible voltage levels can result in
suboptimal designs that increase cost, switching losses, and converter size.

   To provide a more quantitative perspective on these challenges, Table 6.1 summarizes
major multilevel converter topologies and the required unidirectional and bidirectional
power switches, Nsw , as a function of the number of output voltage levels, n. As the num-
ber of switches, gate drivers, and their voltage supply circuits increase, the implementa-
tion becomes prohibitively complex, particularly when input voltages are finely divided
down to the single-cell level to achieve the benefits of low-voltage power processing. For
example, a P2 converter architecture with self-balancing capability and cell-level voltage
blocking switches requires a number of switches proportional to the square of the number
of input voltage levels.
The NPC, FC, CHB, and MMC topologies do not require an additional polarity genera-
tion circuit as they are inherently capable of generating positive and negative parts or full
AC waveform. However, most of them require passive components such as buffer induc-
tors in MMC, flying capacitors in FC converter, and clamping diodes in NPC converter.
Various hybrid and reduced-component-count topologies have also been proposed. In
many of these inverter designs, a unipolar level-generation stage is followed by a polarity-
generation stage, typically implemented using an H-bridge, to produce a bipolar AC
output, this includes P2, MLDCL, SSPS, SCSS, MLM, RV, and 2SELG topologies.
Switches in the polarity-generation stage must be rated to withstand the full operating
voltage of the converter, yet they operate at the fundamental output frequency when
the level-generation stage is capable of producing the zero-voltage level, such as in P2,
MLDCL, SCSS, RV, and 2SELG topologies. In the effort to reduce the number of power
switches, several trade-offs are introduced, including increased voltage stress on semi-
conductor devices, reduced redundancy in switching states, loss of modularity, and the
need for bidirectional switches [54].

   These topologies operate with either isolated DC sources, as in CHB, MMC,
MLDCL, SSPS, and SCSS, or with series-connected DC sources. In battery cell-level
converter applications, isolated sources allow energy equalization among cells during
converter operation using advanced modulation and control strategies. For example, the



                                            203
Table 6.1: Comparison of hardware requirements in different single-phase multilevel inverter
           topologies

 [33]          Neutral-point Clamped (NPC)                                      4(n − 1)
 [34, 35]      Flying Capacitor (FC)                                            4(n − 1)
 [66, 190,     Cascaded H-Bridge (CHB)                                          4(n − 1)

 [62,    63,   Modular Multilevel Converter (MMC)                               4(n − 1)

 [55]          Generalized Toplogy (P2)                                        n(n − 1) + 4
 [67]          Multilevel DC-link (MLDCL)                                      2(n − 1) + 4
                                                                                (n−3)∗
 [56,    57,   T-Type Converter                                                   2    +4
 58]
 [68]          Switched Series-Parallel Sources (SSPS)                         3(n − 1) + 4
 [69]          Series-Connected Switched Sources (SCSS)                        2(n − 1) + 4
 [59]          Cascaded Bipolar Switched Cells (CBSC)                            (n + 1)∗
 [60]          Multilevel Module (MLM)                                           n∗ + 4
 [191]         Reversing Voltage (RV)                                          2(n − 1) + 4

 This work     Binary-Tree Multilevel Converter (BTMLC)                        2(n − 1) + 4
 ∗ refers to bi-directional switches.




MMC can incorporate battery balancing directly into its control strategy [30]. However,
such topologies require a large number of control signals and extensive voltage and cur-
rent sensing to support the control algorithms. Moreover, isolated gate drivers, typically
based on high-frequency transformers or opto-couplers, are needed to transmit control
signals across different voltage domains. These requirements pose significant challenges
for scaling to higher-voltage drive converters when they are implemented with cell-level
input voltages, as the number of submodules increases substantially, resulting in consid-
erable control overhead and design complexity.

   These limitations highlight the need for a different approach to enable scalable, ef-
ficient, and compact cell-level power processing converters. In this chapter, a novel
binary-tree multilevel converter (BTMLC) design is proposed, fully integrated on a sil-
icon chip and operating at the single-cell level of LiFePO4 /Li-ion batteries. The design
employs seven battery cells connected in series. The proposed architecture allows high-

                                             204
performance switches to operate at high frequencies while blocking only cell-level volt-
ages, whereas switches with higher voltage-blocking capabilities operate at progressively
lower switching frequencies. Series-connected cells are also utilized to supply the gate-
driving circuits via Zener-diode based voltage limiters or regulators, eliminating the need
for floating power supplies such as bootstrap capacitors, charge pumps, or isolated DC–
DC converters, thereby simplifying overall system design and integration compared to
conventional discrete approaches.
This architecture enables a miniaturized converter with stacking capability, making it
suitable for applications requiring higher current or voltage operation. Moreover, mature
and low-cost Complementary Metal-Oxide-Semiconductor (CMOS) technologies with
high-voltage isolation can be employed for its implementation. The design is particu-
larly suitable for weight- and size-constrained applications with stringent requirements
on output waveform quality.
This work represents the first design to integrate a large number of power switches along
with their control and driving circuits on a single silicon chip, demonstrating that lateral
integration of the switches on the same substrate using the proposed voltage synthesis
methodology can enable highly compact and efficient power converter designs.
The rest of this chapter is organized as follows. Section 6.2 introduces the proposed
BTMLC topology, detailing its key features and operation, including power loss mod-
elling and switch sizing. Section 6.3 describes the circuit design and implementation
of the proposed BTMLC. Section 6.4 presents the chip fabrication and experimental re-
sults, which validate the proposed design and demonstrate the feasibility of its on-chip
implementation.




6.2     Proposed BTMLC Architecture
The proposed binary-tree battery cell-level converter architecture is shown in Fig. 6.1.
The converter consists of seven series-connected battery inputs, denoted B1 to B7 , each
with a typical cell voltage of Vcell . The topology is organized into three levels, Level I,
Level II, and Level III, each comprising a group of switches configured as half bridges or
choppers, driven by the same complementary logic signals and designed with identical
voltage-blocking capability. The circuit can be extended to additional levels, with the
number of Levels scaling as log2 (n), where n is the number of battery taps or positive

                                            205
voltage levels generated at the output terminal Vbus (t) as defined by:


                                            n = 2k ,         k ∈ Z≥1                        (6.1)


The rationale for limiting the architecture to n = 8 in the proposed implementation is
determined by the voltage breakdown limits of the chosen semiconductor technology, as
further discussed in Section III.




6.2.1    Basic Operation and Features

A cell voltage is selected to the output by sending a single control signal to each level;
Level I, Level II, and Level III. Therefore, the circuit in Fig. 6.1 is capable of generating
eight voltage levels, namely 0 V, Vcell , 2Vcell ...7Vcell , assuming the cell voltage is denoted
by Vcell , as detailed by the logic states in Table 6.2. The number of switches needed for
the level-generation part is given by:

                                                    log2 n
                                        Nsw = ∑ 2 j = 2n − 2,                               (6.2)
                                                     j=1


For single-phase AC load operation, the polarity generation part, implemented by a full-
bridge module, must be included to convert the pseudo-sine waveform into a full sinu-
soidal AC waveform.
Any two adjacent switches within a switch pole operate in a complementary manner (i.e.
when one is on, the other is off and vice versa). A dead-time period must be introduced
to avoid shorting the battery cells and causing large static currents to flow through the
switches and battery cells.
The blocking voltage, Vblk , for switches in Level j (with j = 1 corresponding to Level I
in Fig. 6.1) is given by:


                       Vblk, j = Vcell · 2 j−1 ,           for j = 1, 2, . . . , log2 (n)   (6.3)


The switching frequency at which the switches in Level j operate is also given by:

                                       n · fbus
                            fsw, j =            ,      for j = 1, 2, . . . , log2 (n)       (6.4)
                                        2 j−1

where fbus is the frequency of the output synthesized waveform, Vbus (t). When a polarity
generator or a reverser H-bridge circuit is used, it operates at the output AC waveform
frequency, fout , and thus (6.4) becomes:

                                   2n · fout
                        fsw, j =             ,   for j = 1, 2, . . . , log2 (n)            (6.5)
                                    2 j−1

                 Level I Logic     Level II Logic      Level III Logic      Vbus
                      Low                Low                 Low           7Vcell

Hence, for the proposed circuit, Level I, II, and III switches must respectively block Vcell ,
2Vcell , and 4Vcell and toggle at 8 fbus , 4 fbus , and 2 fbus . The high-voltage switch capability
can either be realised by a high-voltage tolerant device of the required drain-to-source
voltage, or by stacking multiple units of lower voltage switches.
Based on (6.3) and (6.4), the proposed BTMLC architecture allows switches with higher
voltage-blocking requirements to operate at lower switching frequencies. This allocation
enables the construction of higher-voltage DC–AC converters using multiple units of both
on-chip and off-chip components, with the off-chip part employed to scale the proposed
topology. The off-chip switches have relaxed dynamic requirements since they operate
at lower frequencies compared to the on-chip switches. Consequently, multiple off-chip
units can be paralleled to reduce their on-state resistance without increasing switching
power losses.

6.2.2     Converter Losses Modelling
The primary loss components of the converter include static (conduction) losses and dy-
namic (switching) losses:

    • Conduction power losses
        The conduction power losses of the single-phase converter can be calculated based
        on the on-state resistance of the conducting switches. For instance, the circuit in
        Fig. 6.1 have three switches conducting at any time from the batteries to the output
        bus.
