6.3                                              Circuit Implementations
The complete circuit implementation of the proposed BTMLC is presented in Fig. 6.5.
The design comprises both the power and control sections of the converter. The power
stages in Level I, Level II, and Level III utilized N-channel Laterally Diffused Metal-
Oxide-Semiconductor (LDMOS) switches. The control circuitry is responsible for driv-

                                                                       212
Figure 6.5: Proposed circuit design of the battery-cell-level eight-tap BTMLC, with ’LS’ denot-
            ing level shifter circuits and ’DT Gen.’ denoting dead-time generator blocks.


ing these power switches, and includes the design of voltage level shifters, dead-time
generators, gate drivers and and the circuits for generating their supply voltages.


6.3.1    Dead-Time Generators and Voltage Level-Shifters

The dead-time circuit, located at the input of the level shifter, introduces a deliberate
delay interval defined by the time constant of the circuit, set by Rdt1 and Cdt1 . The input
control signal is split into two complementary signals that drive Mdt1 and Mdt2 . The ris-
ing edges of both signals are delayed by the same time constant, Rdt1Cdt1 , thus creating a
dead-time period that prevents shoot-through during switching transitions.
Voltage level shifters are essential for translating control signals from the logic-level
domain to the appropriate voltage levels required by both the low-side and high-side
switches of the converter. To up-shift the control signals for the low-side switches, a

                                             213
stack of series-connected NDMOS transistors is used. The gates of these stacked ND-
MOS devices are connected to the taps of an eight-tap resistive voltage divider, V1 to V7 ,
which divides the maximum input voltage, VDD8 to equal voltage levels. This voltage
divider is implemented using eight resistors, denoted by R1 –R8 , as illustrated in Fig. 6.5.
The resistance values in the voltage divider are chosen to be sufficiently high to minimize
static power consumption. In the proposed design, these values are chosen such that the
current drawn by the resistive divider remains within 1–2.5 mA across the variation of
the input DC voltages. While it is also possible to tie the gates of the level shifter directly
to the cell voltages, this approach may increase the risk of electrostatic discharge (ESD),
thereby reducing the overall immunity of the design to ESD events.
The stacked NDMOS level shifter circuit operates by distributing the total voltage, VDD8 ,
equally across the NDMOS devices in both high and low states of the input signal, VCNT .
When VLS L1 transitions from high (VDD1 ) to low (VSS1 ), the gate-source voltage of MLS1
increases while its drain-source voltage decreases from V2 to V1 . This causes MLS1 to
turn on, which in turn activates MLS3 in a cascading sequence through the stack until
the topmost device, MLS7 , is turned on. At this point, VDD8 is evenly divided across the
drain-source terminals of all devices in the stack. Conversely, when VCNT transitions
from VSS1 to VDD1 , MLS1 enters a high-impedance state, causing its drain voltage to rise
towards V2 . The remaining devices in the stack respond similarly, maintaining a constant
drain-source voltage across each device equivalent to a single battery voltage. Thus,
the devices operate in the saturation region during the low state of the input signal and
in the cut-off region during the high state. When the input is low, the drain voltages
approach the gate voltages of their respective devices, making the devices function as
diode-connected transistors with an effective resistance inversely proportional to the
transconductance of the device, 1/gm .
The power consumption and speed of the circuit for a given battery voltage are de-
termined by the dimensions of the NDMOS devices in the stack. If specific power
constraints are required, (W /L) ratio of the switches can be adjusted based on the as-
sumption that the devices operate in the active region. During the low control-signal
state, when all stacked devices are turned on and exhibit low impedance, the conduction
current associated with the control signal flows through that side of the circuit. This op-
erating condition enhances speed, improves waveform symmetry, and extends the overall
voltage operating range.

                                             214
If zero static power consumption is targeted, the circuit can be implemented using a
stacked cross-coupled level shifter [179]. However, this approach requires P-channel
LDMOS devices, as it relies on an active push–pull level-shifting mechanism. The weak
pull-up devices increase delay, and the overall layout area becomes larger. Moreover, the
cross-coupled level shifter requires careful device sizing to ensure correct operation un-
der worst-case process corners. Finally, if the converter chip is stacked for higher-voltage
operation, the cross-coupled level shifter cannot be extended, since its cross-coupled ter-
mination prevents stacking at the top-most outputs.
The output signals of the level shifters, VLS1 R1 ...VLS1 R7 , VLS1 L1 ...VLS1 L7 , and
VLS2 R1 ...VLS2 R5 , VLS2 L1 ...VLS2 L5 are subsequently used to control the operation of
the half-bridge sub-modules in both Level I and Level II of the converter. The design
maintains the polarity and phase alignment of these waveforms to preserve the pre-set
dead-time duration and ensure proper operation.
The floating voltage level-shifter (LS) blocks employ a conventional cross-coupled ar-
chitecture, as shown in Fig. 6.6. For correct DC operation, the aspect ratios of the
intermediate PDMOS and bottom NDMOS devices relative to the pull-up PDMOS de-
vices were determined based on worst-case corner simulations and the design guidelines
in [138].
Overall, the proposed level-shifting scheme enables fully differential operation, with the
dead-time period introduced only at the input of the stacked level-shifter circuits and
completely eliminated from the half-bridge stages. This ensures symmetrical control
signals between the low-side and high-side switches. These features represent key im-
provements over conventional control strategies that rely on local dead-time generation
circuits and asymmetric level shifting of the control signals. In such approaches, the
dead-time often must be extended to compensate for the propagation delay introduced
by the high-side switch level shifter, resulting in increased power losses. Finally, the
proposed strategy can enable universal dead-time controllability, as it requires only a
single dead-time circuit with control signals referenced between the first battery tap and
circuit ground.


6.3.2       Level I, II, and III Circuit Implementations

The Level I circuit of the converter comprises four half-bridge power stages, each imple-
mented using 5 V NDMOS devices connected across four battery cells. Each half-bridge

                                            215
Figure 6.6: Non-static VSSH cross-coupled voltage level shifter circuit, configured as a differential
            input–differential output stage for fully symmetric operation.




control stage integrates several key components: CMOS inverters functioning as gate
drivers and buffering stages, cross-coupled level shifters, and two voltage limiter (or
regulator) circuits based on Zener-diode voltage regulation.
The level-shifted complementary control signals serve as inputs to each half-bridge sub-
module control circuit, as shown in Fig. 6.5. The low-side control signals require no
further level shifting, as they are already referenced to the proper battery cell voltage.
However, the high-side control signals require an additional level-shifting stage, imple-
mented using a cross-coupled active level shifter (Fig. 6.6).
To maintain symmetry between the low-side and high-side control signal paths, an iden-
tical level shifter block is placed in the low-side signal path. In this configuration, the
high-voltage node of the level shifter is connected to the low-voltage domain, effectively
serving to equalize delays rather than shift levels. This approach ensures that the dead-
time interval remains constant and that delay matching is preserved across process and
temperature variations.
The Zener-based voltage regulators are used to generate the gate drive voltages for both
the high-side and low-side drivers. Although the low-side gate drive could be directly
sourced from the cell voltage. This design employs regulation to ensure symmetry be-
tween the high-side and low-side drive voltages. This ensures both switches are driven
with the same gate overdrive voltage, resulting in matched on-state resistances. However,
when minimizing layout area is prioritized, the low-side gate drivers for Level I of the

                                                216
converter can be connected directly to their respective battery cells.
Each Zener regulator is implemented using a 6-V Zener diode and a source-follower
transistor MR . Current bias sources for these circuits are realized using resistors Rz and
Rs , which bias the Zener diode and source-follower transistor. Capacitors Cz and Cs are
included for voltage filtering and stability.
These circuits are connected to higher voltage taps relative to the voltage domain of their
corresponding half-bridge sub-module, as detailed in Fig. 6.5. The voltage limiter circuit
operates by defining the gate voltage of the source-follower transistor MR1 through the
Zener diode, resulting in an output voltage of approximately VDRV ≈ VZ − VGS , where
VZ is voltage across the Zener diode and VGS is the gate-source voltage of the source
follower device. The Zener bias current is set by a high-voltage isolated resistor Rz
and is on the order of a few microamperes. To minimize the variation of VGS with load
current, the source-follower transistor is sized with sufficiently large W /L ratio, provid-
ing high transconductance and keeping the output voltage, VDRV , within a few hundred
millivolts. In addition to its simplicity, a key advantage of this voltage limiting circuit
is that it operates as an open-loop circuit, which allows it to respond immediately to
driver current changes. Nevertheless, negative feedback is still present due to the finite
transconductance of the source-follower [49]. To avoid open-circuit conditions in cases
where insufficient voltage is present across the regulator and to stabilize the reference
gate voltage, a buffer capacitor is connected across each Zener diode.
The Level II gate driving circuits share a similar architecture with those of Level I. The
complementary control logic signals are first level-shifted by the multiple-output voltage
level-shifting stage to the lowest battery tap within the selectable range of the Level II
sub-module.
For example, in the second half-bridge submodule, consisting of switches M11 and M12 ,
the two complementary control signals are first level-shifted to the fourth tap, VDD4 . This
produces the signals VLS2 L5 and VLS2 R5 , which toggle between the VDD4 and VDD5 volt-
age rails. These signals are then fed differentially into floating cross-coupled level shifter
circuits. The outputs from these level shifters subsequently drive the CMOS inverter gate
drivers.
The drive voltages for this level are generated using the same method as in Level I.
Specifically, the low-side switch drive voltage is sourced from the Zener regulator circuit
used for the high-side switches in Level I. As a result, the generated supply rails VDRV2

                                                217
and VDRV6 are used to control the low-side switches in Level II, since the high-side
switch in Level I and the low-side switch in Level II share a common source node. For
the high-side switches in Level II, dedicated Zener regulator circuits are used.
the generated supply rails VDRV9 and VDRV10 are produced by voltage limiter circuits
connected to taps VDD6 and VDD8 , corresponding respectively to the bottom and upper
half-bridge sub-modules in Level II of the converter.
The Level III control circuit architecture also follows a structure similar to that of Level I
and Level II. The control signal VCNT,3 is converted into a two-phase signal with a fixed
dead-time period. This two-phase signal is then level-shifted from the logic domain,
between VSS1 and VDD1 , to the Level III low-side voltage domain, defined by VDRV9 and
VSW1,L2 .
The outputs of the first level shifter, VLS3 L1 and VLS3 R1 , are subsequently translated to
the high-side and low-side gate drivers symmetrically. The role of the level shifter in
the low-side signal path is the same as in Level I: to equalize the propagation delays
between the high-side and low-side, thereby preserving the dead-time duration set by the
input-level shifter and the dead-time generation circuit, denoted as the ”DT Gen.” block
diagram in Fig. 6.5.
The generation of the drive voltages for both the low-side and high-side rails follows the
same architecture as in Level II. The low-side drive voltage is directly sourced from the
high-side voltage limiter output of Level II, while the high-side switch in Level III uses
its own dedicated voltage limiter circuit connected to the VDD8 tap.
The proposed converter architecture can be extended to support higher voltage operation.
The current eight-tap design is limited by the junction breakdown voltage of the selected
BCD technology. However, higher voltage capability can be achieved either by adopting
alternative technologies with higher breakdown limits, such as silicon-on-insulator, or by
stacking multiple lower-voltage modules at the board level. For example, a 32-tap system
can be implemented using four stacked modules of the proposed design. In the stacked
configuration, the modules are combined and interconnected through external discrete
or integrated switches, whose blocking voltages are defined by (6.3) and switching fre-
quencies by (6.4) and (6.5), in order to synthesize the complete output voltage waveform.




                                             218
6.4     Fabrication and Experimental Results

The proposed converter was fabricated using a 130-nm BCD technology. High-voltage
switches with suitable drain-source voltage ratings were utilized for the Level I, II, and III
power stages. A cross-sectional view of a typical 5 V NDMOS/PDMOS device is shown
in Fig. 6.7. The device bodies are connected to their respective source terminals and are
isolated from the substrate through a buried N-layer.
To minimize on-state resistance and layout area, all power switches were implemented
using N-type devices. Although the Level III power stage does not require devices rated
beyond 17 V for a Li-ion/LiFePO4 battery voltage range, the fabrication run used for
tape-out only offered a 24 V device that met the necessary specifications. This resulted in
a sub-optimal voltage rating for the switches implementing the output half-bridge stage,
which contributed to a substantial increase in the overall path resistance of the converter.
To enhance area efficiency and reduce parasitic resistances, power pads were placed ad-
jacent to the devices as well as directly over their active regions. Both pad configurations
were employed to interface the chip with a custom-designed printed circuit board (PCB).
The total chip area is 15.2 mm2 , and the top-level layout is illustrated in Fig. 6.8(a).
A micrograph of the fabricated chip, wire-bonded using 33 µm aluminium (Al) wires, is
shown in Fig. 6.8(b). The wire-bonded pads include the output, input battery cell pads,
and the control logic signals. Since multiple circuits were integrated on the same chip,
the output pads were positioned near the centre of the chip and connected via multiple
wire bonds to ensure reliable connection with low resistance.
To validate the chip’s functionality as a multi-tap selector, the test bench shown in
Fig. 6.8(c) was established. The wire-bonded chip is mounted on a two-layer PCB (a
daughterboard) that includes decoupling capacitors across the various power supply rails.
For rapid characterization, a 50-contact card edge connector was employed to interface
that daughterboard PCB. Each pad was routed to four edge connector contacts, and input
logic signals were assigned to two contacts.
A separate test board was used to evaluate the functionality of the converter chip, as
shown in Fig. 6.8(d). The converter is controlled via three logic signals generated by a
digital microcontroller.


   Initially, the chip was operated as a DC voltage tap-selector to estimate the total path

                                             219
Figure 6.7: Process cross-section illustrating N-channel and P-channel devices with a deep N-
            layer for substrate isolation.




Figure 6.8: (a) Top-level converter chip layout (3.530 mm × 4.297 mm). (b) Die micrograph

resistance. A constant load current of 2 A was drawn from each selected voltage tap.
The measured path resistance was approximately 0.472 Ω. The average I 2 R losses are
thus computed to be approximately 1.88 W, which is about 20% higher than the losses
predicted by the model in Section III. This discrepancy is attributed to the additional
resistances introduced by connector wires, daughter and test board routings, and wire
bonds.
To fully characterize the dynamic behaviour of the converter chip, a no-load test was
performed using variable tap voltages and output frequencies, employing a nearest level
control (NLC) strategy. The measured output voltage waveforms are shown in Fig. 6.9(a)-
(b), where all supplies were set to 2.5 V and the NLC reference frequencies were set to
10 Hz and 5 kHz.


   The results demonstrate successful synthesis of the reference NLC waveforms across
a wide frequency range. Figures 6.9(c)-(d) further demonstrates the converter’s capabil-
ity to generate staircase voltage waveforms with step resolutions corresponding to higher
cell-level voltages of 3.7 V.
An overshoot is observed in the output voltage waveform when transitioning from volt-
age levels of 10 V (Fig. 6.9(b)) or 14.8 V (Fig. 6.9(d)) to the next lower level. This

                                             220
Figure 6.9: Measured NLC output voltage waveforms: (a) 2.5 V, 10 Hz; (b) 2.5 V, 5 kHz; (c)
            3.7 V, 10 Hz; (d) 3.7 V, 5 kHz.




transition involves turning off switches M5 , M11 , and M14 , and then turning on switches
M4 , M10 , and M13 . Since the high-side switches in Levels I and II turn on faster than the
low-side switch in Level III, the output voltage is momentarily pulled toward the maxi-
mum voltage for approximately 50 ns. This overshoot can be mitigated by synchronizing
these switching events through driver design.


   To enable current delivery to a resistive load, the voltage taps were decoupled using
ceramic capacitors with multiple capacitance values: 0.1 µF, 0.33 µF, 10 µF, and 22 µF.
Figure 6.10 shows the measured output voltage and current waveforms under different
load conditions, supply voltages, and switching frequencies, as described in the figure
caption. These results confirm that the proposed converter chip operates reliably and
delivers the expected output current across a wide range of load, frequency, and voltage
conditions.
To demonstrate the converter chip’s ability to transition arbitrarily between voltage taps,
the input control signals were driven by a uniformly distributed random sequence. The
tap voltages were set to 3.3 V, and a resistive load of 12.7 Ω was connected. The result-
ing output voltage and current waveforms, recorded across multiple time intervals, are
shown in Figs. 6.11(a) and (b). This test validates the converter’s capability to synthesize
arbitrary voltage waveforms at high transition rates while continuously delivering load
current.

ied, as illustrated in Fig. 6.12. The results confirm a smooth dynamic response despite
the abrupt frequency changes. Similarly, the reference voltage amplitude was randomly

6.5        Conclusion
This chapter has presented a fully integrated binary-tree multilevel converter capable of
generating eight distinct voltage levels using only three control logic signals. The con-

                                                 223
verter was experimentally validated over an output frequency range from DC to 5 kHz. A
prototype implemented in a 130-nm BCD process demonstrated reliable operation at load
currents up to 2 A. Operating at cell-level battery voltages of around 2.5 V–3.7 V, the con-
verter achieves high-resolution output waveforms with significantly reduced output dv/dt,
thereby lowering electromagnetic interference compared with traditional topologies that
employ large voltage steps. By integrating a large number of low-voltage switches on-
chip and eliminating bulky isolation components and gate drivers such as high-frequency
transformers, optical gate drivers, and DC-DC converters, the proposed design enables
a compact converter architecture. This approach can be extended to higher voltage and
current levels either by employing semiconductor processes with higher breakdown volt-
ages or by integrating the design with off-chip switches that operate at low frequencies,
thereby minimising power losses. These features make the proposed converter particu-
larly attractive for applications with stringent size and weight constraints, where compact
integration with battery cells is desirable.




                                               224
Chapter 7


Integrated Capacitively Coupled
Modular Full-Bridge Battery Balancing


7.1      Introduction

The battery management system (BMS) is a vital component in battery-powered drive
systems, such as battery electric vehicles and hybrid electric vehicles [192]. A typical
BMS consists of several key units, these include cell monitoring unit (CMU), control and
management unit, protection unit, and communication unit. Control and management
unit controls cell balancing, charge and discharge, and thermal management based on
inputs from the CMU. To do so, it employs active or passive battery balancing circuits
[181].
The cell balancing is necessary as repeated charging and discharging cause imbalances in
the capacities of series-connected battery cells. This results in variations in cell voltages
and state-of-charges (SOCs) across the battery string [193]. The root causes of these
imbalances include differences in the cells’ electrical and chemical characteristics [44],
[182] as well as due to unequal utilization of the individual battery sources in multilevel
power converters [183].
To ensure the safety and longevity of the battery while maximising energy utilisation, it
is a crucial aspect of the BMS to minimise voltage imbalances [194], [195]. Therefore,
passive (dissipative) and active balancing circuits are used to achieve balancing.
Dissipative balancing circuits use resistors to dissipate excess energy from battery cells
with higher SOCs to balance them with cells that have lower SOCs. These resistors can
either have a fixed value and be permanently connected across the cells or be switched
on and off to improve efficiency [196]. Although this scheme is inefficient and can cause
thermal management issues, cell shunting is still used in many commercial BMSs, such
as the LTC6803 and MAX11068, due to the challenges still existing with other efficient
active balancing solutions.
Unlike dissipative balancing schemes, active balancing methods provide a more efficient
and faster solution by redistributing energy among the cells [46]. However, due to the
high implementation costs and complexity of assembling such circuits, their adoption
in battery management systems is still limited. These challenges result from the high
number of circuit elements, such as switches and gate-drivers, and the assembly with
high parasitic impedances that limit the balancing current capability and the switching
frequency of the switches.
Inductor-based, transformer-based [177], [85], [197], [198], and converter-based [199],
[200],[201], [106], [202] active balancers utilize inductors and/or transformers as the
primary balancing elements, supplemented by switches However, these circuits have
notable drawbacks, including the bulky size of magnetic components, higher system
complexity and cost, core losses, and the requirement for higher switching frequencies
and soft switching to reduce component size.
On the other hand, capacitive-based balancing circuits can provide higher power-density
designs as well as lower cost and complexity of system assembly. They transfer energy
directly between battery cells during both charging and discharging operations. Energy
transfer is mediated by capacitors, which can be configured in different schemes, such
as single-switched capacitor [184], single-tiered switched-capacitor [79], double-tiered
switched-capacitor [80], chain structure switched capacitor [81], star-switched capacitors
[83], delta structure switched capacitor [86], mesh structure switched capacitor [85], and
a full-bridge structure with star-switched capacitors [88].
However, when implemented using discrete components, the number of switches and
associated gate drivers can significantly increase system cost and assembly complexity,
particularly in large-scale multicell applications and may hinder practical implementation
and diminish the advantages in speed and efficiency. For instance, a full-bridge-based
capacitive battery balancer equalizing n battery cells requires 4n switches together with
an equal number of gate drivers, resulting in increased assembly complexity, higher
gate-driving power consumption and leakages, and bulkier designs.
To manage this complexity and keep the battery balancing circuits compact with low

                                           226
routing parasitics, integrating the switches and all associated control circuitry directly
onto a single integrated circuit (IC) can lead to lower leakage losses, faster equalisation
time, and miniaturised circuit size. Moreover, the selection of semiconductor devices
can be optimized with respect to their voltage and current ratings. Specifically, a switch
that only needs to block the cell voltage (less than 5 V) does not require a higher voltage
rating, in contrast to discrete or board-level circuit implementations.
A stackable battery management IC is proposed in [187]. The system integrates the
ADCs and other necessary components to monitor 6 Li-ion battery cell voltages and
currents as well as temperature. It also offers 12 integrated gate driver outputs for im-
plementing off-chip passive or active battery balancing devices. The drawback of this
system is that the external cell balancing components increase the system implementa-
tion costs and integration complexity when it is used to balance a large number of battery
cells.
A 7-cell stacked BMS with on-chip integrated battery balancer switches is proposed in
[142]. 50 V high-voltage PMOS back-to-back switches are used to isolate each battery
cell. Either passive or active balancing schemes can be used depending on the off-chip
configuration. This approach suffers from a low balancing current of 100 mA, and the
need to measure and process battery voltages with dedicated circuits. Moreover, the used
switches are rarely available in modern technologies and have expensive masks, leading
to increased system costs and longer development cycle.
A stackable half-bridge IC module, employing four star-connected capacitors to balance
four Li-ion battery cells, was proposed in Chapter 5. The half-bridge configuration ex-
hibits cell-location dependency in balancing speed, since terminal cells remain unused
during half of the switching cycle. Consequently, the balancing current becomes discon-
tinuous, resulting in a high root-mean-square (RMS) component with a small average
value, which causes unnecessary power dissipation in the cells [88]. This excess heat
may accelerate cell degradation and adversely affects the state of health. To address these
limitations while maintaining compactness, simple assembly, and low cost, this chapter
proposes a fully integrated battery-balancing module based on stackable full-bridge con-
verters, which can interface with various external balancing capacitors. All balancing
switches, gate drivers, voltage level shifters, and dead-time generators are integrated us-
ing a 130-nm Bipolar-CMOS-DMOS (BCD) process. The proposed solution achieves a
highly compact design that can be stacked across an arbitrary number of modules without

                                            227
adding control or gate-driving complexity.




7.2     Integrated Full-Bridge Active Battery Balancer

The circuit architecture proposed for on-chip integration comprises two stacked full-
bridge converters as shown in Fig. 7.1. The switches are controlled by two complemen-
tary open-loop signals. During the first half of the switching cycle (State I), the high-side
switches (M13 and M23 ) and the low-side switches (M14 and M24 ) are turned on, while in
the second half (State II), the low-side switches (M12 and M22 ) and the high-side switches
(M11 and M21 ) are activated. This complementary operation ensures continuous current
flow through all battery cells, unlike single half-bridge topologies, where the top-most
and bottom-most cells experience zero current during half of the switching period, lead-
ing to slower equalization speed and lower balancing efficiency, especially for a large
string of battery cells.



7.2.1    Analysis of Balancing Current

To determine the capacitor currents, capacitor voltages, and battery balancing currents,
the circuit in State I is analyzed based on the loop comprising capacitors C11 and C21 (de-
noted as C11,1 and C21,1 to indicate State I quantities), the high-side switches (M13 and
M23 ), and the top battery B1 with cell voltage V1 . The corresponding current directions
are indicated in Fig. 7.1. A dynamic analysis was presented in [88] for the case where
the battery cells are interconnected through full-bridge circuits (isolated batteries), rather
than directly connected in series as considered here. Accordingly, the present analysis
derives new dynamic equations specific to the series-connected configuration and formu-
lates corresponding initial conditions for the capacitor voltages, which differ inherently
from those in the isolated battery case, as will be demonstrated later. The derived quan-
tities are subsequently employed to evaluate the power losses and to assess the balancing
performance of the circuit under various imbalance conditions, as well as to investigate
the influence of parameters such as resistance, capacitance, and switching frequency on
the balancing currents.

7.2.2    Balancer Losses Modelling

Given the critical role of efficiency in battery balancing systems and its impact on the
overall performance of the BMS, it is essential to analyse and minimize the power losses
associated with the proposed integrated balancer. A major advantage of the on-chip im-
plementation is the ability to precisely size the power devices to match specific operating
conditions. The dominant sources of power loss in the system are conduction losses and
dynamic (switching) losses. Dynamic losses primarily result from the periodic charging
and discharging of the switches’ parasitic capacitances, specifically the gate-source (Cgs ),
gate-drain (Cgd ), and drain-source (Cds ) capacitances, as illustrated in Fig. 7.1. The total
dynamic power loss for n battery cells is given by:

                                                                 
                        Pdyn = 4n f Cgs ∆Vgs2 +Cgd ∆Vgd
                                                     2
                                                        +Cds ∆Vds2 ,                   (7.27)


where f is the switching frequency, and ∆Vgs , ∆Vgd , and ∆Vds represent the voltage dif-
ferences between the terminals during switching states.
Conduction losses are attributed to the RMS current flowing through various resistive
elements in the current path. These include the aforementioned resistances Rsw , RC , and
Rwire . The total conduction loss over a complete switching cycle can be modelled using
a unified expression:

                                      n           n       
                                        2               2
                         Pcond = 2Re ∑ ICk1,1 = 2Re ∑ ICk1,2                           (7.28)
                                     k=1                  k=1

                                              233
Summing all the above components, the total losses of the balancing circuit are:


                                   Plosses = Pdyn + Pcond .                            (7.29)


The total capacitance and on-state resistance for a given switch width, Ws , can be ex-
pressed by:

                                                ron
                                        Ron =       ,                                  (7.30)
                                                Ws
                                        Cgs = cgs ·Ws ,                                (7.31)

                                        Cgd = cgd ·Ws ,                                (7.32)

                                        Cds = cds ·Ws ,                                (7.33)


where cgs , cgd , cds are the per-unit gate-source, gate-drain, and drain-source capacitances
of the switch, respectively, and ron is the per-unit on-state resistance. Defining the fol-
lowing parameter for dynamic losses:


                             ζ = cgs ∆Vgs2 + cgd ∆Vgd
                                                   2
                                                      + cds ∆Vds2 ,                    (7.34)


   The total losses per switch are then expressed as:

                                        ron       2
                            Pswitch =       · 0.5Iswitch + f ·Ws · ζ ,                 (7.35)
                                        Ws

where Iswitch denotes the RMS current through the switch. Constrained by the available
area on the chip, a device width of approximately 200 mm was selected. If an optimal
switch size is required for a specific balancing current and switching frequency, Pswitch
has to be differentiated with respect to Ws and the result is set to zero to yield the optimal
switch width. This optimal switch sizing was not implemented due to limited available
chip area.

7.2.3    Simulation Results
To highlight the characteristics of the proposed full-bridge balancing circuit, a set of sim-
ulation results based on the developed model are presented in Fig. 7.3–Fig. 7.6.
The simulations consider two battery cells with V1 = 4.3 V and V2 = 3.3 V, and balancing
capacitors of 256 µF, yielding an equivalent capacitance of 128 µF between the switching

                                               234
terminals of the top and bottom full-bridge submodules. For f = 1 kHz and Re = 0.5 Ω,
Fig. 7.3(a) shows the capacitor current IC11 , Fig. 7.3(b) shows the balancing currents IB1
and IB2 , and Fig. 7.3(c) shows the equivalent capacitor voltage, ∆VC = VC21 −VC11 . Even
though the capacitor current exhibits full charging and discharging within each switching
cycle, resulting in a zero average value. The balancing currents of the two cells, how-
ever, display unidirectional behaviour: the top cell delivers charge while the bottom cell
receives it through the shuttling capacitor connected to each cell. The capacitor voltage
therefore alternates between the top and bottom cell voltages.
To accelerate the balancing process, the average balancing current must be increased.
This is achieved by raising the switching frequency so that the entire switching cycle is
utilized for charge transfer with continuous current flow, as demonstrated in Fig. 7.4 for
f = 16 kHz. At this frequency, the capacitor voltage swing reduces to approximately
0.1 V, indicating that the balancing current is mainly limited by the total path resistance
Re . The effect of this resistance is further illustrated in Fig. 7.5, which shows higher cur-
rent operation when Re = 0.12 Ω at the same switching frequency. These two operating
regimes correspond to the “slow-switching” and “fast-switching” limits commonly used
in switched-capacitor circuit analysis [87] and in capacitive balancing circuits [85].
Finally, Fig. 7.6 presents the simulated average and RMS currents across switching fre-
quency for the same Re values. It can be observed that the average and RMS balancing
currents converge at sufficiently high frequencies for the given capacitance. This con-
vergence arises because the full-bridge circuit achieves continuous current operation (as
shown in Fig. 7.4), which improves balancing efficiency and speed. In this regime, the
current waveform exhibits an increased DC component for the same RMS value, leading
to a higher ratio of average-to-RMS current, and thus faster and more efficient balancing.
The switching and conduction losses, computed from the derived loss equations, are
shown in Fig. 7.7 for two imbalance scenarios with Re = 0.5 Ω and Re = 0.12 Ω at a
switching frequency of 200 kHz. The results indicate that conduction losses dominate,
suggesting that higher switching frequencies can be employed to reduce the required
balancing capacitor size and overall cost.


7.3     Circuit Implementation
The complete schematic of the proposed balancer is shown in Fig. 7.8. The circuit con-
sists of two stacked full-bridge sub-modules. For implementation, 5 V NDMOS and

PDMOS switches are employed, isolated within an N-buried layer (NBL) to enhance
both performance and reliability.

    The circuit is driven by a single control signal, VCNT , which is level-shifted using a
multiple-output voltage level shifter. This level shifter comprises three stacked NDMOS
devices, M1 , M2 , and M3 , that generate multiple outputs for controlling each full-bridge
sub-module. The total voltage, Vcell,0 , is equally distributed across the stacked devices in
both logic states of VCNT .

    When VCNT transitions from high to low, the gate-source voltage of M1 increases,

     0.5
                                                       2
    0.45

     0.4                                             1.5
    0.35
                                                       1
     0.3

    0.25                                             0.5
     0.2
       100                 101                 102     100               101                 102


    Figure 7.6: Average and RMS balancing currents at (a) Re = 0.5 Ω and (b) Re = 0.12 Ω



while its drain-source voltage decreases from Vcell,1 to Vcell,2 . This turns on M1 , which
subsequently activates M2 and M3 . At this stage, Vcell,0 is evenly divided across the stack.
Conversely, when VCNT transitions from low to high, M1 enters a high-impedance state,
causing its drain voltage to rise toward Vcell,1 . The other stacked devices behave similarly,
maintaining a constant drain-source voltage equal to the cell voltage. Thus, during the
input low state the devices operate in saturation, while in the high state they remain in
cut-off. In the low state, the drain voltages approach their respective gate voltages, so
the devices behave as diode-connected transistors with an effective resistance of 1/gm ,
where gm denotes the device transconductance. The power consumption of the level

                                              237
Figure 7.7: Dynamic and conduction power losses at 200 kHz for two imbalance conditions at
            Re = 0.5 Ω and Re = 0.12 Ω




          Figure 7.8: Full-bridge stacked modules using dual-NMOS power trains.



shifter depends primarily on the dimensions of the stacked devices. In applications using
high-capacity batteries, this consumption is acceptable, as it improves speed, symmetry,
and the voltage operating range.

   The level-shifter outputs, VLS1 , VLS2 , and VLS3 , control the half-bridge sub-modules
composing the full bridges. Additional building blocks include the dead-time (DT)
or non-overlapping signal generators, shown in Fig. 7.8(c) and denoted ”DT1” and
”DT2”, and the floating cross-coupled voltage level shifter (”LS”), shown in Fig. 7.8(b)
[138]. Each dead-time circuit receives a level-shifted signal and consists of cross-coupled
NAND gates with two inverters at their outputs, along with an RC network that defines
the dead-time interval. This interval prevents cross-currents through the switches and

                                           238
battery cells. Proper polarity and phase alignment of the control signals are essential to
preserve the preset dead-time duration. Therefore, DT1 and DT2 generate outputs with
opposite polarities so that the left- and right-side half-bridge circuits are driven comple-
mentarily.

   The floating level shifter (”LS”) employs a conventional cross-coupled topology. The
aspect ratios of its PDMOS devices (MLS3 , MLS4 ) and NDMOS devices (Mnd1 , Mnd2 )
relative to the pull-up devices (Mpd1 , Mpd2 ) are chosen according to the design equations
in [138], ensuring robust DC operation and delay performance under worst-case process
corners.

   The CMOS gate drivers are sized to prevent high dv/dt triggered turn on and keep the
gate-source bounce voltage below the device threshold voltage, so the low-side NDMOS
device is sized large enough to lower the resistance while the high-side PDMOS is sized
to keep the rising times long enough to avoid exciting the stray inductances added by the
wire-bond and package parts. The gate-drive supply voltage is provided by the battery
cells, eliminating the need for external supplies or bootstrap capacitors, except for the
uppermost level in the proposed dual-NDMOS half-bridge architecture. In practice, this
can be realized with P-channel devices or with charge-pump/boosting circuits.

   The high-side switches M21 and M23 are driven by the outputs of the upper sub-
module, VOUT3 and VOUT4 , respectively. For instance, to turn on M23 , its gate is pulled
up by a CMOS driver to a voltage exceeding its threshold, ensuring a low-resistance
conduction state. Since the source of M23 connects to Vcell,2 , the gate drive is supplied by
VOUT4 , which provides one cell voltage above that source potential. Conversely, when the
device is off, VOUT4 is at Vcell,2 , ensuring safe voltage stress across the level shifter and
driver circuits. For the top-level full-bridge high-side switches, two low-current push-
pull stages (Ma1 –Ma4 ) generate the gate-drive supplies, VRail,1 and VRail,2 , as shown in
Fig. 7.8.

   The low-side switches are directly driven from the same battery domain. This ar-
chitecture demonstrates the benefits of custom switch design for battery balancers: the
battery cells themselves power the drive circuits, avoiding costly off-the-shelf gate drivers
while improving cost, performance, and compactness.

   The cross-section of the devices is shown in Fig. 7.9. Power pads are located over
active device regions using a bond-on-active (BOA) pad structure to optimize area uti-


                                             239
                        Figure 7.9: 130-nm BCD process cross-section


lization and reduce resistance. The total chip area is 4.68 mm2 , with the top-level chip
layout illustrated in Fig. 8.9.




                        Figure 7.10: Chip layout: 1250 µm x 3750 µm




7.4     Experimental Results
The stacked H-bridge circuit was fabricated using a 130-nm BCD process with 5-V NLD-
MOS balancing switches. For rapid prototyping, the chip was packaged using a commer-
cial J-leaded ceramic chip carrier (JLCC) with 84 pins, as part of a larger chip assembly
containing additional circuits. A micrograph of the chip is presented in Fig. 7.11. The
power pads extend across the drain and source regions, facilitating further investigation
on flip-chip bonding to minimize parasitic resistance and inductance. However, for fun-
damental operational testing, wire-bonding was performed with two bonds per power
pad. The wire bonds consist of 25-µm gold wires.
To validate the chip’s functionality as a battery balancer, the test-bench shown in Fig.
7.12 was established. The JLCC package is mounted on a two-layer PCB incorporating
two capacitors, each configured with six 22-µF capacitors.
Two Keithley 2281S-20-6 battery simulators are used to simulate a Samsung 18650 Li-
ion 3.7 V battery model and to operate in supply mode for characterisation as well. The
simulated battery capacities are set to 50 mAh, with initial SOCs of 2% and 90% to rep-

der similar conditions of switching frequency and input voltage. The rise and fall times
of the output voltage range from 12 ns to 17 ns.
These results validates the correct operation of the chip and its capability to work as a
two-cell battery balancer. To characterise the operation of the circuit under imbalance
conditions, the input sources have been set to supply mode and a voltage imbalance is set
to observe the balancing current. Each cell voltage has been varied from around 2.4 V
to 4.3 V while the other cell is set to 3.3 V across a wide-range of switching frequen-
cies from 1 kHz to 256 kHz, as shown in Fig. 7.15. The average balancing current of
each source increases nearly linearly with the amount of voltage imbalance. The current

                                               243
Figure 7.16: Measured battery current waveforms at different balancing frequencies under max-
             imum and minimum voltage imbalance conditions for the top input source.




Figure 7.17: Measured balancing capacitor voltages and cell currents at frequencies from 1 kHz
             to 128 kHz, with the top cell at 4.2 V and the bottom cell at 3.3 V.



increases from around 50 mA at 1 kHz to around 0.55 A when the switching frequency
exceeds 16 kHz. Beyond 16 kHz the current becomes limited by the combined resistance
of switches, wire-bonds, package leads, and other board routings and connectors.
This demonstrates that the balancer chip is able to transfer energy from each supply
source to the other depending on the voltage difference between the two supplies, the
frequency of operation, and balancing capacitance, and the balancing path total resis-
tance. The average balancing current has been severely impacted by the resistance of the
wire-bonds, package leads, PCB traces, and testing connectors. A much higher average
current is expected to be achieved if these parameters are optimised in a real application

Figure 7.18: Measured SOC balancing at frequencies from 5 kHz to 100 kHz, recorded over equal

due to the very low-resistance of the switches used, which is around 30 mΩ including the
metallization parasitic resistance.
To validate the shape of the balancing currents and their variation with switching fre-
quency, the current waveforms have also been measured under increasing switching fre-
quencies and at the highest and lowest voltage imbalance for each cell. Figure. 7.16
shows the cell’s currents for maximum and minimum voltage imbalance scenario of bot-
tom cell and at switching frequencies from 1 kHz up to 64 kHz. As expected, the currents
at the low frequencies of 1 kH and 4 kHz demonstrate a discontinuous charging and dis-
charging cycles, which indicates that the balancing does not fully utilize the full switching
cycle due to the dominance of the slow-switching impedance manifested in the balancing
capacitors and thus they dominate the total path impedance. Increasing the switching fre-
quency further to 8 kHz, the current waveform of each cell becomes continuos, highlight-
ing full utilization of the switching cycle and hence better balancing speed as the average
balancing current increases, this moves the balancer operation to the fast-switching limit,
where the balancing capacitors start to less dominate the impedance of the path and the
path impedance becomes mainly dominate by other resistive elements. Going higher in
switching frequency keeps increasing the average balancing currents, up to 64 kHz. This
is for the given case of having around 130 µF balancing capacitors. Higher values of bal-
ancing capacitors enable achieving the same average current, and thus the same balancing
speed, at lower switching frequencies. However, in a large battery string, the required ca-
pacitor voltage ratings increase with the number of cells, reaching (n − 1)Vcell /2 for an

                                                     245
n-cell stack with typical cell voltage Vcell . Large-value capacitors with such high voltage
ratings become bulky and costly. In contrast, utilizing the high dynamic performance of
the 5 V NLDMOS switches, along with their optimized input and output capacitances,
allows the average balancing current to be increased by operating at higher switching
frequencies rather than by enlarging the capacitor size. This approach reduces imple-
mentation cost and improves the overall practicality of the system.
It can be observed that the current waveforms are not fully identical, this can be attributed
to the mismatches in the loops due mainly to the differences in PCB tracks and routings,
connector wire lengths, and wire-bonds.
The voltage waveforms of the balancing capacitors are shown in Fig. 7.17 when the bot-
tom cell is at 4.2 V and top cell at 3.3 V across a range of switching frequencies from
1 kHz up to 128 kHz. At low switching frequencies, the capacitor voltages experience
a wide voltage swing equal to the amount of voltage imbalance between the two cells,
this voltage swing keeps decreasing with the increase of switching frequency, reaching
a nearly constant value equal to the average average voltage of the cells. Therefore, at
the fast-switching limit, the capacitors behave as constant voltage sources and the higher-
voltage cell transfers energy to the balancing capacitors which in turn deliver this energy
to the lower-voltage cells. Effectively, the current uses the mediating capacitors as paths
to transfer the charge from the higher SOC/voltage cell to the lower SOC/voltage cell.
Figure.7.18 demonstrates the SOCs for the two cells converging to the average SOC
value after starting at initial SOCs of 2% and 90%, measured up to the same time inter-
val. The speed keep improving up to 50 kHz, as per the SOC values measured at the end
of the time interval, it improves from 17-percent down to 1.1-percent between 5 kHz and
50 kHz switching speeds, this agrees with the average balancing current measurements
and current waveforms presented earlier in this section.
The balancing efficiency, measured at a voltage difference of 1 V, is shown in Fig. 7.19 as
a function of frequency. The efficiency was calculated as the ratio of the energy delivered
to the recipient battery cell (bottom cell) to the energy initially supplied by the donor cell
(top cell). As expected, the balancing efficiency improves with increasing switching fre-
quency, but begins to decline at higher frequencies due to additional losses. The proposed
balancer achieves approximately 75% efficiency over the 10–100 kHz range.

   Table 7.2 compares the proposed integrated solution with other reported battery bal-
ancing circuits and systems. The proposed design is the first to integrate all balanc-

ing switches, gate drivers, and associated control circuitry using 5 V NDMOS devices.
In contrast, prior designs either rely on external switches and drivers, or employ high-
voltage (50 V) devices requiring both N-channel and P-channel transistors, which limits
the achievable balancing current due to the small device sizes. The proposed design is
modular and can be stacked to balance larger battery strings, independent of cell location
or the initial distribution of cell SOCs, while maintaining a very compact circuit foot-
print. Another key advantage is the simplicity of control, which eliminates the need for
complex algorithms and extensive data processing of voltage and current sensor inputs.



7.5        Conclusion
This chapter has presented an on-chip integration approach for battery balancing cir-
cuits. The proposed balancing chip incorporates two full-bridge modules, which can be

                                                       247
extended on-chip up to the voltage limit supported by the semiconductor process. The de-
sign is fully modular and stackable, requiring only a single control signal in an open-loop
configuration, thereby eliminating the need for complex computations based on voltage
or current sensor feedback. This results in an extremely compact solution suitable for ap-
plications with stringent weight and size constraints, enabling seamless integration with
battery cells. The circuit supports simultaneous energy transfer among all cells in the
string through the AC2AC transfer mode. Further optimization can be achieved by tai-
loring the package size and board layout to minimize parasitic inductance and resistance
from interconnects, thus preserving the low-resistance advantage of the on-chip switches
and enabling higher-frequency operation for increased balancing speed. Compared with
discrete implementations, the proposed design achieves significant size reduction by in-
tegrating a large number of switches and simplifies the driving requirements, eliminat-
ing the need for isolated transformers or optical gate drivers. Moreover, due to the low
threshold voltage of the integrated switches, the design supports a wide range of battery
chemistries, operating efficiently from approximately 2.4 V to 4.3 V per cell.




                                           248
Chapter 8


Scalable BTMLC Design for
Higher-Current and Higher-Voltage
Operation


8.1     Introduction

Multilevel converters (MLCs) offer several advantages over conventional two-level con-
verters, including improved output voltage quality, reduced voltage stress on components,
enhanced electromagnetic compatibility (EMC), lower switching losses, improved fault
tolerance, and scalability for high-voltage applications [203]. Furthermore, MLC im-
plementations based on low-voltage metal–oxide–semiconductor field-effect transistor
(MOSFET) switches have been shown to improve power train efficiency and reduce over-
all cost across different driving cycles, compared with conventional high-voltage designs
employing insulated-gate bipolar transistor (IGBT) or silicon carbide (SiC) high-voltage
switches [188].
However, realizing low-voltage processing with MLCs requires more components than
two-level converters, including semiconductor devices, gate drivers, and protection cir-
cuits, which introduces several challenges. These include higher implementation cost, in-
creased assembly complexity, and larger physical size, ultimately leading to lower power
density [203].
To address issues of components count, various application-specific multilevel converter
topologies have been proposed in [67, 68, 69, 56, 59, 60, 191, 61]. These typically in-
clude a ”level-generation” section producing multiple unipolar voltage levels, followed
Figure 8.1: Gate overdrive voltage generation circuits for integrated power converter designs:
            (a) basic bootstrap power supply circuit, (b) charge-pump power supply circuit, (c)
            self-powered supply, (d) capacitively isolated power supply circuit, and (e) voltage-
            limiter (shunt regulator) circuit


by a ”polarity-generation” section (e.g. an H-bridge) that converts this unipolar signal to
a bipolar AC waveform. The switches in the polarity-generation section must withstand
voltages equal to the converter’s total operating voltage [54]. These topologies only re-
quire unidirectional or bidirectional switches to perform voltage synthesis from either
series-connected or isolated multiple DC sources.
These multilevel converters employ discrete switching devices such as IGBTs, MOS-
FETs, and more recently, SiC transistors, in combination with isolated gate drivers.
For converters synthesizing a limited number of voltage levels, this architecture offers
manageable assembly complexity. However, to enhance converter performance, reduce
switching losses, and enable the use of low-cost, low-voltage, high-performance MOS-
FETs, the number of input voltage levels must be increased so that the converter pro-
cesses voltages at the single-cell level rather than in large voltage steps [188]. This leads
to a significant rise in the number of switching devices and a corresponding increase in
the complexity of their gate drivers and their power supplies. For example, in the P2-
based multilevel converter proposed in [55], the number of required MOSFETs increases
quadratically with the number of input voltage levels.
The resulting physical assembly requires substantial space, which reduces power den-
sity and efficiency due to increased parasitic losses and complex routing. Ultimately, the
heavy reliance on numerous discrete components often results in suboptimal designs in
terms of voltage and current matching, overall cost, and system footprint. Hence, from
the perspectives of power density, cost, and reliability, the efficient integration of the gate
overdrive supply circuits, gate drivers, and power switches become essential.
 Isolated DC/DC converters with built-in transformers have been commonly used to
power floating gate drivers in multilevel converters, but their integration remains chal-
lenging [43]. In contrast, other integrable solutions such as bootstrap (BS) circuits

                                              250
[204], charge pump (CP) circuits [127], [158], and self-powered supply (SPS) circuits
[155], capacitively isolated power supply circuits [43], and voltage-limiter (regulator)
circuits [156], which rely on semiconductor devices and low-voltage capacitors have
been proposed for highly-integrated converter designs. These overdrive voltage supplies
are shown in Fig. 8.1. However, these circuit implementations attempted to increase the
level of integration of multilevel converters face design and implementation issues when
used in multilevel converters with extended number of levels. For example, BS circuits
require paths for the charging current to charge all the BS capacitors, therefore, the cir-
cuit has to satisfy this constraint which adds architectural and operational limitations.
Moreover, cumulative voltage drops across BS diodes limit broader multilevel applica-
bility. For independent gate drive generation of the circuit operation, the CP circuits can
be used to independently provide a floating drive voltage for each floating switch in the
topology. This adds more cost as CP circuits requires more capacitors (flying capacitor
and one floating capacitor). Moreover, these techniques are still bulky as at least one big
capacitor is needed for every gate driver, which increases the cost and reduces the power
density and makes the assembly more challenging.
The SPS circuit illustrated in Fig. 8.1(c) establishes the driver-rail voltage by utilizing
the voltage blocked across the switches. When the main switch associated with the SPS
circuit is turned off, the gate capacitance of MR1 is charged, which activates MR1 . Con-
sequently, the capacitor Cs in the SPS path is charged via the diode Ds . An important ad-
vantage of the SPS configuration is that, unlike the CP and BS circuits in Fig. 8.1(a)–(b),
it does not require an external DC source. However, if the off-state period is insufficient,
the supply capacitor of the driver rail cannot accumulate adequate charge, resulting in a
lower output voltage [42, 155].
The driver-rail supply generator shown in Fig.,8.1(d) employs two coupling capacitors
driven by complementary signals from the primary-side drivers. These capacitors pro-
vide high-voltage isolation between the primary side and the driver-rail supply. The cir-
cuit operates independently of the main converter and its switching patterns, and has been
proposed as a multiple-output driver-rail generator for diode-clamped multilevel convert-
ers [43]. While this technique is a potential candidate for IC integration, it involves
trade-offs between the deliverable driver-rail power, the required capacitance, and/or the
charge-pump frequency. In addition, the implementation cost is relatively high, as it
requires two isolation capacitors, one output capacitor, and a Zener diode for voltage

                                            251
limiting and regulation. Another concern is the effect of pulsed leakage currents through
the coupling capacitors when the potential of the main converter node changes rapidly
(high dv/dt). To limit the resulting current stress on the circuit components, series resis-
tors are required, which further increase circuit size, cost and complexity, particularly for
high-voltage on-chip operation [43].
Voltage limiter circuits, shown in Fig. 8.1(e) demonstrates robust operation in providing
stable drive supply voltages for both low side and high side switches, yet it comes with
many drawbacks. These include the large size of the regulator circuits, as they are based
on high-voltage bulky and costly Zener diodes, high voltage resistors and high voltage
source follower-based active devices. This increases the cost of implementation as such
devices are not always available in the semiconductor process due to their need for more
fabrication masks. The design also suffers from a large area dedicated to these voltage
regulators, exacerbated by the need for high voltage isolation.
From these existing solutions, the challenges facing higher levels of integration of multi-
level converters are mainly related to the count of switches, diodes, passive components,
driver circuits and isolation or level-shifting of numerous numbers of control signals, and
integrating them in a limited design space to keep acceptable performance.
In multicell-battery-powered systems, the individual battery cells can be directly inter-
faced with the converter. This approach allows the use of high-performance, low-voltage
switches for power processing and enables lateral integration of these switches on the
same silicon substrate, paving the way for extremely compact and efficient converter
designs. Moreover, the driving of such low-voltage switches can be realized using the
battery cells themselves as they are compliant with the switches driving requirements,
without the need for external capacitors or floating gate-supply circuits.
To address these driving drawbacks, this chapter presents a fully on-chip, binary-tree
single-phase multilevel converter capable of synthesizing arbitrary voltage patterns. It
derives the drive voltage supplies directly from the battery cells using standard low-cost
and area-efficient active devices. This allows to utilize the chip area for increasing the
power devices and minimize the area for the control-side circuitry, which is a superior
advantage that reflects on efficiency and cost of the converter. The converter operates
directly at the single-cell-level of battery modules and utilizes seven series-connected
battery cells, enabling the generation of eight distinct positive voltage levels. Moreover,
the converter has simple control with only three control logic signals required to syn-

                                            252
thesize eight voltage levels. This architecture is particularly well-suited for size- and
weight-constrained applications and can be extended for higher voltage operation either
on the board level or chip-level within the maximum breakdown voltage limits of the
semiconductor process.
In summary, the main contributions of this chapter are as follows:

   1. A new design and implementation of a binary-tree multilevel converter architec-
        ture, incorporating low-cost and compact driver-rail supply generator circuits as
        well as control circuits (e.g., multiple-output level shifters), all utilizing available
        input sources with CMOS-compatible voltage levels.
   2. The design and implementation of LDMOS switches with low on-state resistance,
        providing up to 6 A maximum current capability.
   3. Demonstration of successful and reliable wire-bonding of bond-on-active-circuit
        (BOAC) pad structures for low-dielectric-constant (k) silicon technology, enabling
        improved area efficiency, reduced metallization resistance, and lower fabrication
        costs.
   4. Validation of the stacking capability of the proposed converter design to synthesize
        50 V half-sine waveforms under variable-voltage and variable-frequency operation.

This chapter is organized as follows: Section 8.2 presents the proposed binary multi-
level converter topology and provides a detailed analysis of its switching and conduction
losses. Section 8.3 discusses the proposed circuit implementations and the drive voltage
generation circuits. Section 8.4 presents the experimental results and related discussions,
while Section 8.5 concludes the paper.



8.2       Binary-Tree Multilevel Converter
The proposed BTMLC architecture is illustrated in Fig. 8.2. It employs a binary-tree
switch network that maps the input cell voltages (B1 to B7 ), each with a nominal value of
Vcell , to the output through nearest-level control (NLC). The converter structure is orga-
nized into three switching stages, namely Level I, Level II, and Level III. In each stage,
all switches operate at the same frequency, and in the off-state, each device sustains an
equal voltage stress. The fundamental unit of the architecture is a half-bridge (chopper)
cell.

    This topology can be generalized to higher resolutions, with the number of switching

                                              253
stages scaling as log2 (n), where n denotes the number of battery taps, or equivalently, the
number of distinct positive voltage levels available at the output terminal Vbus (t):


                                       n = 2k ,       k ∈ Z≥1 .                         (8.1)


In this work, the design is constrained to n = 8, dictated by the voltage breakdown ca-
pability of the selected semiconductor process, as further discussed in Section III. For
single-phase AC operation, a polarity generation stage is required. This can be imple-
mented using a full-bridge module, which converts the pseudo-sinusoidal waveform into
a true bipolar sinusoidal output. Within each bridge leg, the two complementary switches
operate in opposite states (i.e., when one is on, the other is off). A suitable dead-time in-
terval must be introduced to prevent shoot-through currents across the battery cells.




8.2.1    Basic Operation and Features

The output voltage level is determined by applying digital control signals to the three
switching stages, denoted as VCNT1 , VCNT2 , and VCNT3 corresponding to Level I, Level II,
and Level III, respectively. With this configuration, the converter in Fig. 8.2 can generate
eight distinct output levels:


                                   0, Vcell , 2Vcell , . . . , 7Vcell .


   The output voltage Vbus (t) as a function of the digital control signals is expressed as
                                                         
                                                         
                    Vbus (t) = 7 − 4VCNT1 + 2VCNT2 +VCNT3 Vcell .                       (8.2)


   The total number of switches required for level generation is given by:

                                             log2 n
                                   Nsw = ∑ 2 j = 2n − 2.                                (8.3)
                                              j=1


   The blocking voltage Vblk, j for switches at level j is given by


                         Vblk, j = Vcell · 2 j−1 ,      j = 1, . . . , log2 (n).        (8.4)

                                                  254
Figure 8.2: Schematic of the battery-cell-level 8-tap binary-tree converter




                                   255
The switching frequency fsw, j of level j switches is expressed as

                                          n · fbus
                               fsw, j =            ,     j = 1, . . . , log2 (n),        (8.5)
                                           2 j−1

where fbus denotes the synthesized bus voltage frequency, Vbus (t). When a polarity gen-
erator (H-bridge) is employed, it operates at the output AC frequency fout , and thus (8.5)
modifies to
                                          2n · fout
                              fsw, j =              ,     j = 1, . . . , log2 (n).       (8.6)
                                           2 j−1
For the 8-tap implementation, the blocking voltage requirements for Level I, Level II, and
Level III switches are Vcell , 2Vcell , and 4Vcell , respectively, with corresponding switching
frequencies of 8 fbus , 4 fbus , and 2 fbus .

    From (8.4) and (8.5), it follows that switches subjected to higher blocking voltages
operate at lower switching frequencies. This characteristic facilitates the realization of
high-voltage DC–AC converters by combining both on-chip and off-chip devices, where
the off-chip stages are used to extend the voltage scalability of the topology. Since the
off-chip switches function at lower frequencies, their dynamic performance requirements
are relaxed. Therefore, multiple off-chip devices can be paralleled to reduce the on-state
resistance without incurring additional switching power losses.



8.2.2     Converter Loss Modelling

The total losses of the proposed converter consist primarily of conduction losses and
dynamic switching losses.

    Conduction losses arise from the on-state resistance of the conducting switches, met-
allization resistance, and other routing resistances. For the converter in Fig. 8.2, three
switches conduct simultaneously between the battery stack and the output bus. Assuming
the output current contains only the fundamental component and the converter consists
of n battery taps, the conduction loss is given by

                                                          log2 n
                                                  2
                                         Pcond = Irms      ∑ Ron, j ,                    (8.7)
                                                           j=1


where Irms is the rms value of the fundamental load current and Ron, j is the on-resistance
of Level j switches, which scale with the total switch width Ws, j for a given per unit

                                                        256
switch resistance, ron, j , as
                                                         ron, j
                                              Ron, j =          ,                         (8.8)
                                                         Ws, j

    Switching losses are mainly due to the charging and discharging of parasitic capaci-
tances (Cgs , Cgd , Cds ) in each device. The total dynamic power loss is expressed as

                                     log2 n
                                                      n               2
                             Pdyn = ∑ fsw, j ·              ·Cgs, j ∆Vgs, j
                                       j=1          2 j−1
                                         log2 n

                                      + ∑ fsw, j ·               ·Cgd, j ∆Vgd, j          (8.9)
                                          j=1            2 j−1
                                         log2 n
                                                           n               2
                                      + ∑ fsw, j ·               ·Cds, j ∆Vds, j,
                                          j=1            2 j−1

where fsw, j is the switching frequency of Level j devices, and ∆Vgs, j , ∆Vgd, j , ∆Vds, j de-
note the gate-source, gate-drain, and drain-source voltage transitions. The device capac-
itances scale with the total switch width as


                                         Cgs, j = cgs, j ·Ws, j ,                        (8.10)

                                         Cgd, j = cgd, j ·Ws, j ,                        (8.11)

                                         Cds, j = cds, j ·Ws, j ,                        (8.12)


where cgs, j , cgd, j , cds, j are the capacitances per unit width.

    Defining the following parameter to represent the dynamic loss contribution per
switch:
                                         2                2                2
                          β j = cgs, j ∆Vgs, j + cgd, j ∆Vgd, j + cds, j ∆Vds, j ,       (8.13)

with the gate–drain voltage variation given by


                                     ∆Vgd, j = ∆Vgs, j + ∆Vds, j .                       (8.14)


    The total converter loss is the sum of conduction and dynamic switching losses:


                                         Ptot = Pdyn + Pcond .

    The total loss per switch at Level j can be compactly expressed as

                                                                    ron, j 2
