where the first term accounts for dynamic switching losses and the second term for con-
duction losses.

    Figure 8.3(a) plots this relationship for the three levels of the proposed 8-level
BTMLC under operating conditions of 5 A rms current and 10 kHz output frequency.
The switches used are rated at 5 V, 10 V, and 16 V. The breakdown of losses, totalling
0.7 W, is shown in Fig. 8.3(b) under the same operating conditions. The distribution
indicates that switching losses are relatively minor (6.5%), while conduction losses dom-
inate, comprising channel resistance losses (42.6%) and metallization resistance losses
(50.9%). The metallization resistance contribution slightly exceeds that of the channel
on-resistance for each switch.
For a 16-tap configuration (realized by stacking two BTMLC modules as discussed in
Section IV), the proportion of on-chip switching losses increases to 21.7% under the
same operating conditions, while conduction losses remain the dominant component.


8.3            Circuit Implementations
The complete circuit implementation of the proposed BTMLC is presented in Fig. 8.5.
The design incorporates both the power stages and the control stages of the converter. The

                                                 258
Figure 8.4: Cross-sectional view of a 130-nm BCD process showing buried N-layer for substrate
            isolation and butted source–body connections.



power stage is realized using NDMOS switches rated at 5 V, 10 V, and 16 V for Level I,
Level II, and Level III, respectively. The control stage performs signal level shifting,
dead-time generation, and gate driving. Furthermore, the driver supply rails are derived
internally with minimal overhead, eliminating the need for auxiliary dc–dc converters or
bulky regulators. This functionality is achieved by utilizing low-cost technology devices
in conjunction with the readily available series-connected battery cells.
A cross-sectional view of typical NDMOS and PDMOS devices is shown in Fig. 8.4.
The devices are enclosed by buried N-layers, which allow operation at high potentials
relative to the substrate. Consequently, the maximum voltage rating of the converter is
constrained by the junction breakdown voltage between the substrate and the isolation
layer. For reliable operation, the device bodies are tied to their respective sources, while
for the NDMOS device the isolation layer taps are biased to the potential of one battery
cell above the drain and source terminals.


8.3.1     Voltage level-shifters

The voltage level-shifters are required to convert the control signals from the logic do-
main to the voltage domains of each low-side and high-side switches. Two types of
voltage level shifters are used, static-VSSH level shifters that utilize battery cells for level
shifting and floating VSSH cross-coupled level shifters.
To up-shift the control signal to the low-side switches, a stack of series-connected
switches whose gates are tied to the cell-voltage taps has been used. Three identical
circuits are used for the three levels and they are controlled by inputs VCNT1 , VCNT2 , and
VCNT3 .
Taking level I as an example, the circuit operates by distributing the total battery string
voltage, Vcell,8 across the NDMOS devices in both high and low states of the input signal,
VCNT1 . When VCNT1 transitions from high (Vcell,1 ) to low (VSS1 ), the gate-source volt-

                                             259
Figure 8.5: Proposed circuit design of the eight-tap BTMLC, with ’LS1’, ’LS2’, and ’LS3’ de-
            noting cross-coupled level-shifter circuits and ’DT’ denoting dead-time generator
            blocks.




age of S1,L1 increases while its drain-source voltage decreases from Vcell,2 to Vcell,1 . This
causes S1,L1 to turn on, which in turn activates S2,L1 in a cascading sequence through
the stack until the topmost device is activated. At this point, Vcell,8 is divided across the
drain-source terminals of all devices in the stack. Conversely, when VCNT1 transitions
from low to high, S1,L1 enters a high-impedance state, causing its drain voltage to rise
towards Vcell,2 . The remaining devices in the stack respond similarly, maintaining a con-
stant drain-source voltage across each device equivalent to the supply voltage.
When the input is low, the drain voltages approach the gate voltages of their respective
devices, while the gate voltages are biased one-cell voltage above the source voltages. As
a result, the devices behave as diode-connected transistors with an effective resistance of
1/gm , where gm is the device transconductance. Accordingly, the devices operate in the
saturation region during the low state of the input signal, and in the cut-off region during
the high state, since their gate–source voltages are zero in the latter case.

                                             260
    The proposed level-shifter circuit draws static current from the battery string when-
ever the control signal is at VSS1 , as the stacked devices each present a resistance of
1/gm . Hence, the average power consumption is determined by the device dimensions
in the stack for a given battery voltage and control-signal duty cycle. This circuit offers
several advantages: compact size, layout flexibility around the control circuits, and ease
of routing. In addition, it provides high speed with low delay, requires no PDMOS de-
vices, enables straightforward device sizing, and achieves high process yield. Compared
with cross-coupled level shifters having multiple outputs, which rely on weak PDMOS
pull-up devices that degrade speed, demand larger layout area, and require careful sizing
to increase the yield, this level-shifter is more compact and simpler to implement. An-
other key advantage is stackability: when multiple converter modules are connected in
series to extend the voltage range, the control signals can be readily propagated to drive
the upper modules in the stack. This feature will be validated in the next section.

    The output signals of the stacked level-shifter circuits are subsequently used to control
the operation of the half-bridge sub-modules that directly connect with the battery cells,
e.g., Vcell,1 , Vcell,2 , ...Vcell,7 . The floating level shifter circuits (”LS1/LS2/LS3” blocks)
have been used for translating the signals translated by the stacked level shifter to the
high-side switches in Level I and to both high-side and low-side switches in Levels II
and III. This is required since the sources of these switches (in Levels II and III) are not
fixed and can vary based on the state of each control signal. Therefore, these circuits have
been implemented using a cross-coupled level shifter with non-static VSSH capability,
as shown in Fig. 8.6(a). For correct DC operation, the aspect ratios of the PDMOS
and NDMOS devices to the PMOS pull-up devices have been sized according to the
design methodology proposed in [138]. The dead-time generation circuit (”DT” block)
is shown in Fig. 8.6(b). It receives the level-shifted input signal and generates a two-
phase waveform with a dead-time period determined by the introduced delay. This delay
is implemented using RC sections placed between the NAND gates and the buffer stages.


8.3.2    Level I Circuit Implementation

The Level I circuit of the converter comprises four half-bridge power stages, implemented
using 5 V NDMOS devices connected across four battery cells.
Each half-bridge control stage integrates several key functions: CMOS inverters as gate
drivers, a dead-time generator, a cross-coupled level shifter, and a high-side driver-rail

                                              261
Figure 8.6: (a) Cross-coupled, non-static VSSH level-shifter circuit. (b) NAND-based two-phase
            non-overlapping clock (dead-time) generator circuit.



supply circuit.
The control signal VCNT1 is level-shifted through stacked NDMOS switches, and the
resulting signals are applied to the two-phase dead-time generation circuit, ensuring non-
overlapping gate signals for both the low-side and high-side switches.
The high-side control signal is further level-shifted by the cross-coupled circuit, with
VSSH tied to the output switching node of each half-bridge submodule (VSW1,L1 –VSW4,L1 ).
The high-side level shifters are supplied by VDDH , connected to VRail,1 –VRail,4 , which are
derived from the upper battery cells of each submodule.
The driver-rail supply is realized using a compact push–pull circuit whose control logic
mirrors that of the low-side switch. When the high-side device is turned on, its gate is
driven through the CMOS inverter to VRail,1 (in the bottom-most submodule), which is
connected to Vcell,2 through the push-up PDMOS device. Conversely, when the low-side
switch is on and the high-side switch is off, VRail,1 is pulled down to Vcell,1 , as illustrated

                                             262
in Fig. 8.7. As a result, the gate–source voltage of the high-side device is maintained
at one-cell voltage, while the voltage stress across the driver circuit and pull-up level-
shifter devices is also limited to a single cell, ensuring reliable operation in both states.
Given a threshold voltage of 0.9–1.3 V, a cell voltage above 2.5 V is sufficient for efficient
switching.




8.3.3    Level II Circuit Implementation


The second-level switches, M9 –M12 , are implemented using 10 V NDMOS devices.
The control architecture of each half-bridge in this level is similar to that of Level I, with
the addition of a cross-coupled level shifter (“LS2” block) at the input.
The control signal VCNT2 is level-shifted using a stacked level-shifter circuit in conjunc-
tion with cross-coupled level shifters to the appropriate nodes: VSW1,L1 and VSW1,L2 for
the bottom half-bridge, and VSW3,L1 and VSW2,L2 for the top half-bridge.
In addition to the power-stage control circuits, the driver-rail supply generators, with out-
puts VRail1,L2 and VRail2,L2 , are also driven by the level-shifted versions of VCNT2 .
Since M9 shares its source with M2 , and M11 with M6 , the low-side devices M9 and M11
in Level II derive their driver-rail voltages from VRail,1 and VRail,3 , respectively, which
are generated in the Level I circuit. For instance, when M9 is turned on, VSW1,L1 toggles
between Vcell,1 and VSS1 , depending on the states of M1 and M2 , and VRail,1 is used to
drive M9 . A similar condition applies to M11 , which is driven from VRail,3 .
In contrast, the high-side devices M10 and M12 in Level II require dedicated push–pull
stages rated similar to power-stage voltage. These push–pull circuits generate VRail1,L2
for the gate driver of M10 and VRail2,L2 for that of M12 .
Specifically, for M10 , VRail,2 is connected to VRail1,L2 when the device is on, and VRail,1 is
connected when it is off, as illustrated in Fig. 8.7.
An analogous mechanism applies in the upper half-bridge, where VRail2,L2 is switched
between VRail,4 and VRail,3 to power the gate driver of M12 .
These driver-rail generation circuits ensure that only a single-cell voltage is applied
across the high-side gate drivers under all operating conditions, thereby enabling the
reliable use of low-voltage switches.

                                             263
8.3.4       Level III Circuit Implementation




The Level III power stage, consisting of switches M13 and M14 , is implemented using
16 V NDMOS devices. The control circuit architecture follows a structure similar to that
of Level II, incorporating multi-stacked and floating level-shifters, a dead-time generator,
and CMOS inverter gate drivers. The control signal VCNT3 is used to control this stage
and is also level-shifted to provide appropriate signals to the control circuitry responsible
for generating the driver-rail supply voltage (VRail, L3 ) for the high-side switch M14 .
The gate driver of the low-side switch M13 is powered by the push-pull stage output
from Level II, specifically VRail1,L2 , analogous to the operation of the low-side switch in
Level II.
The generation of the high-side gate drive voltage in this stage is more complex and re-
quires dedicated push-pull stages capable of selecting the appropriate cell voltage based
on the state of the high-side switch. These small switches generate the driver-rail supply
voltage VRail, L3 and must withstand four cell voltages, equivalent to the voltage blocking
capability of the Level III power switches. Controlled by signals S1,L3 and S4,L3 , which
follow the logic of the Level III control signal VCNT3 but operate in the Vcell,1 -Vcell,2 and
Vcell,4 -Vcell,5 voltage domains, these switches select between VRail2,L2 and VRail1,L2 for out-
put to VRail,L3 , as demonstrated in Fig. 8.7. When M14 is turned on, VRail,L3 is connected
to VRail2,L2 ; when it is off, VRail1,L2 is selected. This ensures that VRail,L3 is consistently
one cell voltage higher than Vbus (t), thereby enabling proper turn-on of the high-side
switch.
The gate driver for the low-side switch within the Level III high-side switch driver-rail
generator is powered by an output voltage denoted VX . This voltage is derived from an-
other auxiliary push-pull circuit, controlled by the Level II level-shifted signals S2,L2 and
S3,L2 . This circuit toggles VX between VSW2,L1 and VSW3,L1 , thereby maintaining a consis-
tent single-cell voltage across the gate driver of the Level III driver-rail supply generator.
Overall, the proposed circuit techniques offer a low-cost solution for driving the switches
in the BTMLC architecture. They avoid the use of special devices such as high-voltage
resistors, Zener diodes, or other costly components that require large layout area and are
less portable across technology platforms. As a result, the BTMLC design is more easily
adaptable to a wide range of technologies.

                                              264
Figure 8.7: Illustration of the synthesis of the driver-rail supply voltages for switches in Levels I-
            III.


8.3.5     Converter Extension
The proposed converter architecture can be extended by stacking multiple modules to
support higher-voltage operation. The current design is limited to an eight-tap configura-
tion due to the maximum junction breakdown limits imposed by the selected fabrication
technology. However, higher-voltage operation can be achieved either by adopting alter-
native technologies with greater breakdown voltages, such as silicon-on-insulator plat-
forms, or by stacking multiple low-voltage modules at the board level.
For a system with a total number of battery taps as defined by (8.1), a total of (log2 n − 1)
converter submodules must be stacked. In this approach, the stacked stages are integrated
with external switches that operate at blocking voltages defined by (8.4) and switching
frequencies defined by (8.6), thereby synthesizing the full output voltage waveform, as
illustrated in Fig. 8.8.
This system architecture allows the use of on-chip low-voltage switches, which inherently
exhibit lower switching losses, as computed in Section II, and reduced on-state resistance
due to optimal sizing based on the converter’s current and voltage requirements. While
the external discrete transistors must be rated for higher voltages, they switch at lower
frequencies, making their switching losses negligible. As a result, these devices can be
optimized for minimal conduction losses and thus can be easily paralleled to further en-
hance system efficiency and power handling.
This stacking concept is validated in Section IV using two stacked modules and an exter-
nal half-bridge stage to extend the converter voltage limit.

                                                265
Figure 8.8: Converter expansion for higher voltage operation using tap-of-eight IC modules,
            demonstrating the extension to sixteen cell taps.



8.4     Fabrication and Experimental Results
The proposed converter design was fabricated using a 130-nm BCD technology. All
power switches are implemented using N-type devices to minimize on-state resistance
and reduce the layout area.
To optimize area efficiency and lower metallization resistance, power pads are placed
directly over the active regions of the devices using a bond-on-active-circuit (BOAC)
pad structure. The total chip area is 20.52 mm2 , and the top-level layout is shown in
Fig. 8.9(a). The top part of the chip includes the four lower battery taps, while the bot-
tom part contains the remaining four taps, with the output terminal located at the center
of the chip.
The experimental validation of the fabricated BTMLC chip is carried out using three ap-
proaches.

                                           266
Figure 8.9: (a) Top-level converter chip layout (5474 µm × 3750 µm). (b) Die micrograph wire-
            bonded to a JLCC-84 package. (c) Test board with 84-pin JLCC socket and decou-
            pling capacitors. (d) Test-bench setup including logic microcontroller, multi-channel
            power supplies, resistive loads, and oscilloscope with voltage and current probes.


First, to verify the correct and reliable operation of the proposed control circuits, in-
cluding voltage level shifters, gate drivers, and driver-rail supply generators, the chip is
packaged in a commercial J-leaded chip carrier (JLCC), which allows access to multiple
test points for characterization.
Second, a high-current BTMLC chip is implemented on a custom board with manual
wire bonding to enable higher current operation and to estimate resistance and efficiency.
Finally, to validate high-voltage operation, two BTMLC chips are stacked on a board to
synthesize higher-voltage waveforms from sixteen battery taps.


8.4.1    Basic Functional Validation Using a JLCC Package

To facilitate testing and provide access to the converter test points, the prototype was
packaged in a JLCC. A chip micrograph with wire bonds is shown in Fig. 8.9(b). Each
power pad is wire-bonded using three 25 µm gold wires, which is sufficient for basic
functional validation. Several internal pads were included to investigate resistance reduc-
tion through external interconnect techniques such as additional wire bonds or flip-chip
stud bumping.
These options, however, were not explored in this work. Only the peripheral pads were
used for PCB connection and current delivery.
The output was taken from one side of the chip using three wire bonds, since the opposite
side contains other circuits (not shown in the figure). This resulted in a suboptimal current
path and an increased series resistance, but the configuration is sufficient to demonstrate
the fundamental operation of the proposed control circuits.
To validate the chip’s functionality as a multi-tap voltage selector, the test board shown
in Fig. 8.9(c) was employed. The JLCC package was mounted in a dedicated socket on a
two-layer PCB with decoupling capacitors across the multiple supply rails.

To enable current delivery to a resistive load, the voltage taps were decoupled using
surface-mounted ceramic capacitors of varying capacitance values: 0.1 µF, 0.33 µF,
10 µF, and 22 µF. Figures 8.11(a)-(b) present the output voltage and corresponding cur-
rent waveforms at 10 Hz and 5 kHz. Initially, each tap voltage was configured to 3.3 V
and then increased to 3.4 V at 5 kHz.
A noticeable voltage drop is observed, primarily due to one-sided long wire bonds at
the output pads. The current is forced to traverse horizontally across the chip output
pad, introducing high path resistance. Despite this packaging limitation, the converter
is capable of delivering approximately 2.5 A, which is close to the fusing current of the
three parallel 25 µm gold wire bonds.
The path resistance, Rpath , is estimated from the DC measurements summarized in
Table 8.1. In this test, the converter was operated as a DC-DC voltage tap-selector,
delivering approximately 2 A of DC current to a variable resistive load across all output
voltage levels, ranging from 3.3 V to 23.3 V. The estimated path resistance is approxi-
mately 0.4 Ω, which encompasses various contributors within the current path, including
wire-bonding resistances, PCB trace resistances, package lead parasitics, and connector
cable resistances.
It is also observed that the input and output currents are nearly identical, with maximum
difference of approximately 10 mA. This current difference accounts for the consumption
of the stacked level shifter circuits, as discussed in Section III.
Thermal performance was also evaluated. The temperature rise was recorded for two
operating conditions: (1) when a constant 2 A DC current was drawn from the highest
voltage tap, and (2) during operation under time-varying conditions corresponding to the
waveforms in Fig. 8.10. These thermal profiles are presented in Figs. 8.12, respectively.
For the 2 A DC case, the peak temperature reached approximately 66◦ C, concentrated

                                                   271
around the output pad and tap seven, as expected. Under time-varying operation, the tem-
perature reduced to around 46◦ C due to a lower average output current (approximately
half of the DC value). In this case, the heat distribution is more spread across the upper
voltage taps and output pad regions.
To characterize the performance of the proposed control circuits and drivers of the con-
verter, Fig. 8.13 shows the measured gate-source voltage (VGS ) waveforms for different
switching pairs, acquired asynchronously with pseudo-sinusoidal NLC. These results
validate the functionality of the proposed gate drive voltage generation circuits, which
successfully derive appropriate drive voltages directly from the input sources.
The measured dead-time and rise/fall time durations are well correlated with the pre-
set design values. Levels I and II exhibit dead-times of approximately 80–85 ns, while
Level III shows a longer dead-time of around 250 ns. The gate-source voltage in Level I
rises to 90% of its final 3.3 V value within 60 ns and falls in about 30 ns. In Level II, the
rise and fall times are approximately 190 ns and 35 ns, respectively. The slowest transi-
tion is observed in Level III, with rise and fall times of approximately 350 ns and 50 ns.
The transition speeds of the VGS waveforms are primarily influenced by the sizes of the
transistors used in the driver-rail generation circuits. As expected, Level III exhibits the
slowest response, since the control signals must propagate through the first and second
levels of the driver-rail generation hierarchy.
The measured drain-to-source voltage (VDS ) waveforms for switches in Level I, Level II,
and Level III are shown in Fig. 8.14. These waveforms confirm that the devices operate
well within their maximum rated voltage limits: 3.3 V for Level I, 6.6 V for Level II, and
13.2 V for Level III when the input voltages are set to 3.3 V.
Figure 8.15(a)–(n) presents measurements of several converter nodes, taken with respect
to ground, while synthesizing a half-sine waveform using NLC. These measurements
include both gate voltages and internal switching node voltages. The results confirm
the reliable operation of the converter circuit under realistic switching sequences used
to synthesize a half-sine waveform and validate the proposed driver-rail generators and
other control signals processing circuits. For example, Figures 8.15(e) and (f) display
the outputs of the top two half-bridge submodules in Level I, VSW3,L1 and VSW4, L1 . The
first switches between 13.2 V and 16.5 V, while the second switches between 19.9 V
and 23.1 V. These measurements demonstrate that all Level I submodules successfully
achieve the expected output voltage swings with full tap-to-tap transitions.

8.4.2      Higher-Current and Higher-Voltage Operation

To enable higher-current and higher-voltage operation of the BTMLC IC, a custom two-
layer PCB with an Electroless Nickel Immersion Gold (ENIG) surface finish was de-
veloped. The ENIG coating improves the reliability of the wire-bonding process and
enhances fabrication yield. The PCB incorporates 0.1 µF and 22 µF ceramic decoupling
capacitors to suppress high-voltage transients and mitigate inductive ringing, thereby al-
lowing pulsed current delivery through multiple converter terminals. Each power pad
was bonded with 10–12 aluminum wire bonds of 33 µm diameter. Contrary to the JLCC
packaging, the output pads were wire-bonded from both sides using multiple distributed
wires across the chip area to improve current handling capability. Two wire-bonded
BTMLC dies are shown in Fig. 8.16(a)–(b).
 The wire-bonding process confirmed the robustness of the BOAC power pad structures
using a fine-pitch flat-wedge bonding method, further verifying their feasibility and reli-
ability.
To simplify the characterization process, a 50-contact card-edge connector was employed
to interface the daughterboard PCBs containing the wire-bonded BTMLC dies with test-
board, as illustrated in Fig. 8.16(c). The test-board was designed with two edge-connector
sockets, enabling rapid plug-in/plug-out of multiple daughterboards.
To extend the operating voltage, an external half-bridge submodule with a dedicated gate
driver was included on the test-board PCB, combining the outputs of two BTMLC mod-
ules as illustrated in Fig. 8.8(c). This configuration enabled higher-voltage operation
using sixteen battery/supply voltage taps. The complete test-bench for validating the

To characterize the converter with the proposed packaging method, the path resistance
was evaluated by operating a single converter module as a DC–DC tap-selector while

The resistance was obtained from the static voltage drop at each current level for the se-
lected tap.
Based on the same measurements, the converter efficiency across all taps within the 2–
6 A range is presented in Fig. 8.20. The measured efficiency varied from 98.5% to 85%,
demonstrating the effectiveness of the optimized converter layout and the positive impact
of the BOAC pads design. Further efficiency improvement is expected if the daughter-
boards are interfaced directly without the edge-connector sockets, which were primarily
included to facilitate rapid testing.

                                                    276
8.5     Multilevel Converter with Embedded Voltage Bal-
        ancing

Depending on the application, multilevel converters (MLCs) achieve multilevel voltage
synthesis either by directly configuring their DC input sources according to the intended
voltage step, or by dividing a high DC input voltage (e.g., 400–800 V) into smaller volt-
age levels, as discussed in [205, 32].

   In both cases, synthesizing a multilevel voltage waveform from these DC sources re-
quires their repeated and periodic utilisation, determined by a modulating voltage wave-
form whose frequency and amplitude depend on the application requirements. The MLC
can operate as an inverter (DC–AC) to drive an electric motor, as a charger (AC–DC),
or even as a DC–DC converter. In all operating modes, the input DC sources (e.g., bat-
teries or capacitors) are repeatedly engaged. Over time, this repetitive usage can cause
the sources to become unbalanced. Furthermore, when batteries are employed as input
sources (such as in electric vehicle drivetrains), their inherent non-uniform characteristics
can also contribute to imbalance. Such imbalance not only leads to suboptimal utilization
of the overall system capacity but can also shorten the battery lifespan.

   Depending on the MLC architecture, the input sources may be isolated and con-
figured in series and/or parallel through the operation of MLC building blocks. This
configuration provides bypassing or insertion capabilities, as seen in modular multilevel
converter (MMC) topologies [206, 30]. Such flexibility enables control strategies that
embed voltage balancing functions directly within the converter operation. For exam-
ple, the dual-star MMC employs a sorting algorithm in combination with arm, leg, and
circulating current controllers to maintain input source balance during normal converter
operation—whether functioning as a motor driver or a charger [30].

   However, MLCs that employ series-connected DC input sources often rely on exter-
nal balancing circuits to equalize the voltages across the sources. As reviewed in Chap-
ter 2, these circuits employ either passive or active balancing methods. Passive balancing
methods are simple and cost-effective but suffer from poor efficiency. In contrast, active
balancing methods transfer energy between sources using nearly lossless components
such as capacitors, inductors, and transformers. Depending on the circuit design and
control strategy, energy transfer can occur in various modes: cell-to-cell, cell-to-pack,

                                            277
pack-to-cell, or any-cell-to-any-cell.

   A multilevel voltage converter with self-voltage-balancing capability was proposed
in [55], as has been illustrated in Fig. 2.8(c). This topology is based on a chopper-
type building block and employs capacitors to maintain voltage balance among the input
sources during converter operation. For N input DC sources (e.g., N capacitors), the
topology requires an additional N(N − 1)/2 capacitors to achieve self-voltage balancing.

   When the topology is extended to accommodate a larger number of input
sources—aiming for finer voltage steps with reduced distortion, enhanced EMI com-
pliance, lower switching frequency, and the use of lower-voltage devices—the imple-
mentation cost and circuit complexity increase significantly. Furthermore, the balancing
speed is expected to decrease due to the higher effective resistance along the balancing
paths, which depend on numerous switching devices within the topology. For example,
in Fig. ?? the switches S f 1 , Sh1 , Sb1 must be turned on so that the capacitors C1 , C2 ,
and C4 are connected in parallel with the bottom-most input capacitor/battery having
voltage Vdc1 to balance their charges. Furthermore, the sequential charge redistribution
among multiple capacitors results in significant charge-sharing losses, thereby reducing
the overall efficiency of the converter.

   In this section, the binary-tree multilevel converter topology is integrated with the
battery balancing topology introduced in Chapter5, resulting in a multilevel converter
architecture with inherent voltage balancing capability and reduced hardware resources.

   The proposed topology employs the first level of the converter, which consists of
N half-bridge choppers, to achieve voltage balancing through N additional balancing
capacitors connected in a star configuration. At the hardware level, the proposed circuit
topology reduces the number of active switches by half compared to implementations
that use a separate balancing stage, as discussed in previous chapters. At the control
level, the same PWM control signal used for Level I of the binary MLC is also utilized
to drive the balancing switches, thereby eliminating the need for a dedicated control
mechanism for voltage balancing and embedding the balancing function autonomously
in the converter operation.




                                            278
8.5.1    Proposed Converter Topology



The proposed circuit topology is illustrated in Fig. 8.22 for a four-cell battery system.
Besides, Figure 8.24 shows the extension of the proposed circuit to an eight-cell battery
configuration.
The circuit in Fig. 8.22 is based on a binary-tree architecture, formed by switches M1 –
M14 which are configured as chopper-cell modules. The balancing stage consists of the
balancing capacitors C11 –C41 , connected in a star configuration, together with the Level I
switch array of the converter.

   The balancing network can be configured in several ways, as will be discussed in the
next Chapter in Fig. 9.1, depending on the desired balancing speed, capacitor ratings,
energy transfer mode, and overall system size. For example, a single- or double-tiered
balancing circuit may be employed, allowing a trade-off between capacitor ratings and
balancing speed. For systems with a large number of battery cells, the star-connected
capacitors enable any-cell-to-any-cell energy transfer, thereby improving the balancing
speed compared to other network topologies [85, 83]. Furthermore, additional capacitors
can be incorporated at the Level II switching stage if higher balancing speed is required,
albeit at the expense of increased implementation cost and circuit complexity.
The full-bridge circuits presented in Chapters 7 and 9 may also be employed instead of
just the half-bridge topology. In this configuration, the second half of the circuit com-
plements the operation of the proposed design, enabling higher balancing currents and
therefore faster balancing speed. Additionally, the top-most and bottom-most battery
cells (or capacitors) participate in both halves of the switching cycle, yielding more sym-
metric operation and increasing the ratio of average to RMS currents. This characteristic
reduces conduction losses and thermal stress.
Nevertheless, for clarity and to maintain design simplicity, the proposed designs focus
primarily on the half-bridge configuration. When implemented using high-speed semi-
conductor devices operating at high switching frequencies, the half-bridge topology can
achieve comparable performance, as demonstrated in Chapter 5, where the balancing cur-
rents become fully continuous at high switching frequencies, resulting in faster balancing
with reduced Joule heating of the cells (or capacitors) at much lower hardware costs and
assembly complexity.

                                            279
    Figure 8.22: Circuit diagram of a single-phase 5-level self voltage balancing BTMLC



8.5.2    Proposed IC-Level Designs

Two IC-level implementations are presented to demonstrate the circuit-level modifica-
tions required for adapting the binary-tree converter architectures discussed in Chapter 6
and the current chapter to include self-voltage balancing capabilities.
The first IC design is illustrated in Fig. 8.24. It employs non-differential (single-ended)
level shifting and adopts a control and power structure similar to the BTMLC IC in-
troduced in Fig. 8.5. The primary modification is the replacement of the push-pull
(PDMOS/NDMOS) stages originally used to propagate the appropriate driver supply
rails to the high-side gate drivers in Level I with dual-NDMOS half-bridge submodules.
In this revised architecture, these half-bridge units additionally function as active bal-
ancing modules alongside the fundamental half-bridge stages responsible for both load
power transfer and cell balancing. This symmetry is advantageous for two reasons: (i)

                                           280
Figure 8.23: Circuit diagram of a single-phase 5-level self voltage balancing BTMLC




                                       281
     Figure 8.24: Circuit diagram of a single-phase 8-level self voltage balancing BTMLC




it enables a unified, N-channel-only power device technology, resulting in a cleaner and
more scalable implementation; and (ii) the identical circuit architecture across both the
power-conversion bridges and the balancing/driver-rail-generation bridges ensures inher-
ently synchronized switching behaviour and consistent gate-drive supply generation.
Another key modification concerns the number and type of chip terminals. Since the bal-
ancing capacitors must now interface with each Level-I half-bridge output, the number
of required pads scales with the number of battery cells. This increases packaging com-
plexity and may necessitate advanced packaging technologies. A flip-chip approach, as
discussed in Chapter 3, is particularly well suited to mitigate these challenges by signif-
icantly shortening the balancing current paths and potentially enabling the integration of
balancing capacitors at the package level or on the substrate backside. Such integration
can reduce parasitic resistance and inductance, thereby enhancing balancing speed and
overall system efficiency.
The remaining circuit blocks follow a similar methodology for generating the floating
driver rails for each switch, consistent with the implementation strategy shown in Fig. 8.5.

                                            282
     Figure 8.25: Circuit diagram of a single-phase 5-level self voltage balancing BTMLC


   Another proposed IC architecture is shown in Fig. 8.25. It builds upon the design
introduced in Chapter 6. In this approach, dead-time is generated at the inputs of the level
shifters, after which the control signals—already dead-time-encoded—are level shifted
and applied to each half-bridge stage in a fully symmetric manner. The high-side driver
supply rails in Level I are derived using an approach analogous to the first design shown
in Fig. 8.24, wherein the internal switching nodes of the half-bridge submodules are
utilized to synchronously generate the gate-driver supply rails. Meanwhile, the low-
side switches interface directly with the corresponding battery taps without incurring
additional circuitry or area overhead. The same methodology is extended to Levels II
and III, enabling all gate-driver supply rails to be generated internally.

   Alternatively, shunt-regulation using Zener diodes, as discussed in Chapter 6, may
be employed. However, this solution incurs a substantially larger area penalty and re-
quires costly high-voltage components, including high-voltage bulky resistors, Zener
diodes, and buffer capacitors. Consequently, the proposed architecture represents a more
compact, cost-effective, and scalable solution compared to bulky Zener-based shunt-
regulation techniques.




                                            283
8.6     Conclusion
This chapter has presented a fully integrated binary-tree multilevel converter capable of
generating eight distinct voltage levels using only three control logic signals. The con-
verter’s power switches are driven and supplied by a low-cost architecture that leverages
standard high-voltage CMOS-compatible components, avoiding bulky and expensive so-
lutions such as Zener diodes or large capacitors. Experimental validation was conducted
across a wide frequency range—from DC to 5 kHz—demonstrating robust performance
without intrinsic frequency limitations. The prototype, implemented in a commercial
package, was tested at load currents up to 2.5 A. However, current handling was limited
by the packaging constraints, specifically the use of a single-sided output pad wire-bonds,
which introduced significant resistance. Ongoing design improvements target perfor-
mance enhancement through optimized packaging strategies, including increased wire
bonding and flip-chip integration using stud-bumping on custom PCBs, and utilizing the
internal pads to enhance the current capability and reduce resistance. The converter pro-
duces high-resolution output waveforms with minimal filtering requirements and reduced
output dv/dt, significantly lowering electromagnetic interference compared to conven-
tional converters with large voltage steps. By integrating a large number of switches and
eliminating bulky isolation components such as transformers or optical drivers, the design
offers a compact, efficient solution ideal for size- and weight-constrained applications.




                                           284
Chapter 9


Complementary Integration of Stacked
Half-Bridge Circuits for Battery
Balancing


9.1     Introduction
Electric vehicle (EV) powertrains are predominantly based on two-level voltage-source
inverters, which are fed on the DC side by a battery pack composed of multiple elec-
trochemical cells [207]. These cells are connected in series to achieve the high voltage
levels required by the inverter to drive the traction motor. However, due to inevitable
differences in the electrochemical characteristics and leakage currents of individual cells,
this series configuration leads to unequal state-of-charge (SOC) levels over time, even
though all cells carry the same current [44]. Such imbalance can result in non-uniform
voltage distribution, leading to underutilization of the battery capacity and a reduction in
pack lifespan [44].

   To mitigate these issues, battery balancing circuits are embedded within the Battery
Management System (BMS) to maintain cell balance by either dissipating the excess
energy as heat or redistributing energy from higher SOC cells to lower SOC ones, during
both charging and discharging [46]. Various BMS topologies have been proposed in the
literature, often categorized based on the type of energy transfer element used, such as
capacitors, inductors, transformers, or dedicated converters [45].

   Active balancing circuits redistribute energy among the cells in a battery string,
increasing the efficiency and the balancing speed compared to passive counterparts.
Capacitor-based battery balancing techniques, which primarily rely on capacitors and
switches, are promising due to their low volume and cost, and simple control [46]. Each
balancing topology involves trade-offs in energy equalization speed, efficiency, voltage
stress on components, circuit and control complexity, and energy transfer mode, which
may include cell-to-pack, pack-to-cell, adjacent cell-to-cell, direct cell-to-cell, or any
cell-to-any-cell (AC2AC) [45].

   Capacitor-based battery balancing techniques include several architectures, such
as single-tiered switched-capacitor (STSC) [79], double-tiered switched-capacitor
(DTSC) [80], single switched-capacitor (SSC) [184], chain-structured switched-
capacitor (Chain-SC) [81], series–parallel switched-capacitor (SP-SC) [82], star
switched-capacitor (Star-SC) [83], delta-structured and mesh-structured topolo-
gies [86, 85], and the complementary star switched-capacitor architecture (CSS-
CAB) [88].

   These active balancing circuits typically require a large number of components, which
complicates integration and increases cost in practical systems. This complexity stems
from the substantial number of switches, gate drivers, and passive elements, as summa-
rized in Table. 9.1. Balancing performance depends on assembling these components
within tight board space to minimize routing lengths between active devices, balancing
capacitors, and battery cells, thereby reducing parasitic impedances. Excessive parasitic
resistance degrades balancing speed by limiting the balancing current, while parasitic
inductance imposes an upper limit on the achievable switching frequency.

   The implementation cost and assembly complexity of full-bridge capacitor-based bat-
tery balancers increase significantly with the number of cells, since n cells require 4n
switches and gate drivers. Achieving high performance is further challenged by the
dependence of the balancing current on the resistance of the path among capacitors,
switches, and cells, which makes low-parasitic assembly difficult.

   For example, the CS2CAB topology employs two complementary Star-SC configu-
rations to achieve faster balancing, continuous balancing current for improved efficiency,
and cell-location-independent balancing, surpassing other SC-based designs. However,
these benefits are achieved at the cost of increased hardware: CS2CAB requires 4n
switches and gate drivers, along with isolated supplies for each gate drive circuit, and
2n capacitors with progressively higher voltage ratings. For low-frequency discrete im-


                                           286
plementations, these capacitors can be bulky and expensive. Furthermore, the balancing
current and maximum operating frequency are significantly impacted by parasitic resis-
tance and inductance from circuit routing between battery taps, switches, and capacitors.
As a result, assembling such circuits for large battery strings imposes limitations on fre-
quency and current due to parasitics.

   In addition to the aforementioned challenges, the balancing circuits also necessitate
costly gate drivers, which increase circuit assembly complexity and adversely affect the
overall reliability of the BMS. In most implementations, the gate-driving strategy em-
ploys multi-winding transformers to provide isolated gate–source drive signals for each
MOSFET [185, 104, 177]. An alternative is opto-coupler-based isolation [186], which
also requires bootstrap (BS) gate driver ICs for each sub-module. This approach involves
dedicated voltage generation, often implemented via a non-isolated boost converter, to
supply sufficient gate drive voltage (typically 10–15 V), which exceeds the native voltage
of common lithium-ion cells.

   Building on the on-chip stacked half-bridge battery balancer presented in [208], this
chapter addresses the aforementioned design and performance challenges by introduc-
ing two on-chip, high-current, high-speed battery balancing circuit modules that oper-
ate complementarily and interface with star-configured off-chip capacitors. Fabricated
in 130-nm Bipolar-CMOS-DMOS (BCD) technology, the modules balance four series-
connected lithium-ion cells, enable AC2AC energy transfer regardless of cell position
in the string, and improve the DC-to-RMS balancing current ratio, while maintaining
low assembly complexity and high reliability through on-chip integration. Moreover, the
architecture is modular and stackable, supporting scalable deployment in larger battery
systems. Compared to conventional discrete solutions such as [83, 88], the on-chip design
significantly reduces system size by integrating multiple switches with their associated
control circuitry, while minimizing parasitics and leakage losses inherent in large discrete
layouts. Higher operating frequencies allow the use of smaller balancing capacitors, low-
ering implementation cost. Compact placement of the integrated modules near the battery
cells reduces wiring complexity and associated power losses, thereby improving overall
efficiency and power density. By matching device specifications to battery-cell voltage
levels, silicon area is optimized and fabrication cost is reduced, making the approach
particularly suitable for large-scale battery management systems.

    The rest of this chapter is structured as follows: Section 9.2 introduces the proposed
integrated balancer, including circuit analysis, loss modelling, and implementation de-
tails. Section 9.3 presents the experimental validation, covering chip assembly, measure-
ment results, and comparative analysis. Section 9.4 concludes the paper.


9.2      Stacked Full-Bridge Converters and Potential Bal-
         ancing Networks
The proposed circuit architecture comprises two stacked half-bridge converter modules
operating in a complementary manner, each driven by an independent control signal. A

                                                 288
generalized active battery balancing circuit for n battery cells, together with five potential
capacitive network configurations applicable to both sides of the circuit output terminals,
is shown in Fig. 9.1. These configurations include double-tier network [Fig. 9.1(b)], as
well as more complex star [Figs. 9.1(c)–(d)], mesh [Fig. 9.1(e)], and delta [Fig. 9.1(f)]
networks. Each topology presents trade-offs in terms of balancing speed, implementation
cost, assembly complexity, and capacitors’ voltage ratings.

   To highlight the advantages of the full-bridge architecture, this work adopts star-
configured balancing capacitors, as shown in Fig. 9.2. Compared to mesh and delta struc-
tures, the star topology offers lower implementation complexity while delivering supe-
rior balancing performance relative to conventional single-tier and double-tier switched-
capacitor schemes. This improvement is attributed to the availability of direct charge
transfer paths between all cell pairs, enabling AC2AC energy exchange irrespective of
their physical location. Additionally, it supports a higher ratio of average to RMS bal-
ancing current [88, 83, 84].

   The four-cell full-bridge capacitor-balancer circuit in Fig. 9.2 uses two PWM signals
to control the complementary switching operation. During the first half of the switching
cycle (State I), the high-side switches (M11 –M71 ) and the low-side switches (M22 –M82 )
are turned on. In the second half (State II), the low-side switches (M21 –M81 ) and the
high-side switches (M12 –M72 ) are activated. This complementary operation ensures con-
tinuous current flow through all battery cells, unlike single half-bridge topologies, where
the top-most and bottom-most cells experience zero current during half of the switching
period, leading to slower equalization speed and lower balancing efficiency.

         and Currents

The equivalent circuit models corresponding to both operating states of the k1 balancing
loop are presented in Fig. 9.3. Similar models apply to the k2 balancing loop, where
k ∈ [1, n−1]. Applying Kirchhoff’s Voltage Law (KVL) to the equivalent circuit in State I,
as shown in Fig. 9.3(a) for k ∈ [1, n − 1], yields:

   For the complementary side of the circuit, the equations governing State II and State I
are identical to the computed quantities in State I and State II of the primary side, respec-
tively. Based on this symmetry and the analysis of capacitor currents in both operating
states and both circuit sides, the battery currents during State I can be derived using
Kirchhoff’s Current Law and are expressed as:


                              IBk,1 = −ICk1,1 ,      for k ∈ [1, n]                   (9.14)

Similarly, State II battery currents are expressed as:


                               IBk,2 = ICk1,2 ,     for k ∈ [1, n]                    (9.15)


State I average battery currents:


                              IBk,1 = −ICk1,1 ,      for k ∈ [1, n]                   (9.16)


State II average battery currents:


                               IBk,2 = ICk1,2 ,     for k ∈ [1, n]                    (9.17)


Hence, the kth average battery balancing current is given by:


                                IBk = 0.5(−ICk1,1 + ICk1,2 )                          (9.18)


This model captures the circuit operation under slow and fast switching conditions. The
time-domain waveforms of the capacitor and battery currents over a complete switch-
ing cycle, simulated based on the analytical model, are shown in Fig. 9.4 illustrates


                                              292
the simulation results obtained at a switching frequency f = 10 kHz, a path resistance
Re = 0.12 Ω, and a total balancing capacitance Ce = 200 µF. The initial cell voltages
from top to bottom are 4.3 V, 3.7 V, 3.1 V, and 2.5 V. The average balancing current is
zero for each balancing capacitor, and the sum of the currents leaving the isolated com-
mon plate is zero at all times. The top two cells exhibit positive current flow, indicating
energy transfer from these higher-voltage cells to the lower-voltage cells at the bottom
of the stack. The balancing currents operate near the boundary of continuous conduction
mode, thereby utilizing the entire switching period for energy transfer and achieving a
high balancing speed. The current direction in each cell is determined by its deviation
from the average string voltage (here, 3.4 V). Consequently, the balancing mechanism is
inherently voltage-driven, responding to terminal voltage differences across the battery
string without requiring additional voltage sensors.

   To further highlight the advantage of the proposed complementary full-bridge oper-
ation, Fig. 9.5 shows the capacitor and cell current waveforms under identical operating
conditions but with only one side of the circuit active (i.e., a single half-bridge configu-
ration). In this case, the terminal cells experience zero current for half of the switching
cycle, resulting in asymmetrical current flow. This asymmetry causes the balancing rate
to depend on the cell’s position in the stack, thereby reducing overall balancing perfor-
mance. For example, although cell B2 has a voltage above the average string voltage, its
current IB2 is not continuously positive, resulting in a lower DC-to-RMS current ratio.
This indicates less efficient balancing compared with the complementary case, where IB2
continuously sources current to cells with voltages below the average. A similar obser-
vation applies to cell B3.

   To enhance the balancing speed, the average balancing current must be increased.
This can be achieved either by increasing the switching frequency or the balancing ca-
pacitance. The dependence of the average balancing current on these two key design
parameters is illustrated in Fig. 9.6, under the same circuit conditions for cells B1 and
B2. The bottom cells (B3 and B4) will have equal but opposite currents.

   In conventional discrete on-board designs, increasing the switching frequency is often
constrained by dynamic switching losses, which can become significant and degrade effi-
ciency. To compensate, designers typically resort to larger capacitance values. However,
this approach leads to bulkier and more expensive capacitors, especially in longer battery

Figure 9.4: Analytically derived waveforms of balancing capacitor and battery currents for a 4-

strings where high-voltage capacitors rated at (n−1)Vcell /2 are required, and introduces
challenges such as complex assembly, reduced power density, and increased system cost.

   In contrast, the on-chip implementation proposed in this work enables optimized
semiconductor switch design in terms of sizing, rating, and layout, as explained in the
next sections. This customization allows high-frequency operation with minimal con-
duction and switching losses, thereby improving balancing speed without sacrificing ef-
ficiency, while also enabling the use of smaller capacitors.

   Figure 9.7 shows both the average and RMS balancing current under the same op-
erating conditions for cells B1 and B2. The bottom cells (B3 and B4) will have equal
but opposite currents. While the RMS current determines power losses and contributes
to self-heating of both the cells and circuit components, the average current governs the
rate of energy transfer and thus the balancing speed. Therefore, a high ratio of average
to RMS current is desirable, indicating efficient and fast balancing. As demonstrated in
Fig. 9.4, the full-bridge topology ensures continuous current paths throughout the switch-
ing cycle, inherently improving this ratio compared to half-bridge architectures. Further-
more, this performance can be enhanced by increasing either the switching frequency or
the balancing capacitance.

9.2.3    Circuit Implementations
The complete schematic of the proposed balancer, including the off-chip balancing ca-
pacitors, is shown in Fig. 9.9. The architecture comprises two independently integrated
multistacked half-bridge modules, each capable of balancing four battery cells.

   Each module includes four vertically stacked half-bridge converter sub-modules, la-
belled HBC11 /HBC12 through HBC41 /HBC42 . The detailed circuit implementation of the
module is shown in Fig. 9.10, where 5 V NDMOS and PDMOS switches are employed,
each isolated using an N-buried layer to improve performance and guarantee device re-
liability. Thanks to this isolation approach, the design can be scaled to integrate up to

                                              298
Figure 9.10: Detailed schematic of the right-side block in Fig. 9.9, illustrating the input dead-
             time generation circuit, multi-output level shifter, symmetric driver circuits, and the
             internally generated driver-rail voltages [? ].




eight sub-modules on a single chip, supporting the balancing of up to eight battery cells.
Moreover, the modular and scalable structure allows for straightforward extension at the
board level, making the system adaptable to larger battery strings.

    The circuit is driven by two input control signals, each with a duty cycle of 0.5.
These signals are internally divided into two non-overlapping phases by an on-chip dead-
time generator and are then fed into a multi-output voltage level shifter that produces
differential gate control signals.

    The dead-time generator consists of transistors Mdt1 and Mdt2 , resistors Rdt1 , Rdt2 ,
capacitors Cdt1 , Cdt2 , and CMOS inverters. The dead-time duration is determined by the
RC time constant Rdt1Cdt1 , which delays the rising edge of the control signals to avoid
shoot-through. Alternative dead-time generation schemes, such as NAND- or NOR-
based logic, can also be employed.

                                               299
   The output signals from the dead-time circuit are passed to a multi-output voltage
level shifter composed of stacked NDMOS devices (M1 to M7 , and M2 to M8 ).

   The level shifter distributes the total input voltage Vcell1 evenly across the stacked
NDMOS devices in both the high and low states of the input signal IN. The resulting
outputs, VLS R1 through VLS R4 and VLS L1 through VLS L4 , drive the gate signals of the
half-bridge sub-modules.

   To preserve signal integrity, ensure correct phase alignment, and maintain the pre-
set dead-time across all switching events, both the high-side and low-side control paths
are designed symmetrically. As such, floating voltage level shifters are employed for
both paths. On the low side, the level shifters mainly serve to equalize delays and are
implemented using conventional cross-coupled architectures, as described in [138].

   The high-side switches receive their gate overdrive voltages from the outputs of the
upper sub-modules. For example, the output voltage of HBC11 , denoted VOUT1 , supplies
the gate drive for the high-side switch of HBC21 . For the uppermost sub-module, a
floating gate overdrive voltage must be generated using either a bootstrap capacitor or a
charge-pump circuit [127]. Alternatively, high-side switches can be implemented using
PDMOS–NDMOS pairs to eliminate the need for a floating supply, though at the cost of
increased area due to the larger PDMOS devices.

   In contrast, the low-side switches are driven directly by gate signals referenced to
their respective battery domains. This is feasible due to the use of low-voltage NDMOS
devices, which can be reliably turned on by gate overdrive voltages within the standard
Li-ion cell voltage range. Finally, the outputs VOUT11 through VOUT41 are connected
to off-chip balancing capacitors C11 through C41 , which facilitate charge redistribution
between cells for active balancing.


9.3     Experimental Results
The proposed multistacked half-bridge circuits were fabricated using a 130 nm BCD pro-
cess that includes 5 V high-voltage devices. To enhance area efficiency and reduce rout-
ing resistance, power pads were implemented using two techniques: circuit-under-pad
(CUP), where pads are placed adjacent to the devices, and bond-on-active (BOA), where
pads are positioned directly above the active regions.
Since BOA is not commonly used in low-dielectric constant technologies, this layout

also serves to assess its reliability under different wire-bonding methods, including both
wedge and ball bonding. Both pad configurations were successfully interfaced with a

either the active device regions or the CUP structures.
A chip micrograph and a photograph of a wire-bonded chip are shown in Fig. 9.11(a) and
Fig. 9.11(b), respectively. The total chip area is 3.4 mm2 .




Figure 9.12: Output voltage waveforms of half-bridge sub-modules at input voltages of 2.5 V,
             3.3 V, 3.7 V, and 4.2 V, and at switching frequencies of 1 kHz and 1 MHz under
             balanced conditions.


    To validate the chip’s functionality as a battery voltage balancer, the experimental
setup shown in Fig. 9.11(c) was used. Each chip was mounted on a two-layer PCB
(daughterboard) equipped with one 0.1 µF and two 22 µF decoupling capacitors con-
nected across the four battery terminals. For rapid testing and evaluation, 50-contact

                                                     301
Figure 9.13: Balancing capacitors voltage waveforms at input voltages of 2.5 V, 3.3 V, 3.7 V, and
             4.2 V, and at switching frequencies of 1 kHz and 1 MHz under balanced conditions.


card-edge connectors were employed to interface the daughterboards containing the wire-
bonded chips. Each power pad was routed to four connector contacts, while input logic
signals were routed to two.

    Two separate test motherboards were used to evaluate the balancer’s operation. Each
motherboard included four 22 µF decoupling capacitors per battery/supply input. Circuit
control was provided by two FPGA-generated signals, with a tunable frequency range
from 1 kHz to 1 MHz.

star configuration. These capacitors exhibit a self-resonant frequency of approximately
200 kHz and an equivalent series resistance (ESR) of about 10 mΩ. To fully characterize
the proposed battery balancer prototype, a comprehensive set of tests is performed under
both balanced and imbalanced operating conditions.

    Figure 9.12 shows the measured outputs of the half-bridge sub-modules at switching

   These results confirm correct circuit operation under balanced supply conditions

   Because the balancing capacitors in each module share a common, electrically iso-

These results confirm that the proposed chip operates reliably over a wide range of in-
put voltages and switching frequencies, enabling its application as a battery balancer
for charge equalization. To further assess its balancing performance, four test cases
were examined. In each case, the voltage of one input source was swept from 3.3 V
to 4.3 V—covering the full operating range of typical Li-ion cells—while the remaining
cells were held constant at 3.3 V. For example, in Case 1, the bottom-most cell undergoes
the voltage sweep while the others remain fixed at 3.3 V, whereas Case 4 corresponds to
sweeping the top-most cell under the same conditions.

   The average balancing current sourced or sunk by each supply over a switching fre-
quency range of 4 kHz to 64 kHz is shown in Fig. 9.15. As expected, at a given switching
frequency, the average current increases approximately linearly with the voltage imbal-
ance. The balancing current also rises with switching frequency. For example, when


                                                305
the topmost or bottommost cell is at 4.3 V (corresponding to the maximum voltage im-
balance), the average current increases from roughly 0.5 A at 4 kHz to about 2.25 A at
32 kHz and 64 kHz. Higher balancing currents are measured for the middle cells, starting
from around 1 A and reaching approximately 2.5 A at the maximum tested voltage differ-
ence. This behaviour is due to the middle cells having two neighbouring balancing loops,
which allows charge to be exchanged with both adjacent cells, effectively doubling the
available current path compared to the end cells that interact with only one neighbour.
Beyond the 32 kHz–64 kHz range, the balancing current either plateaus or exhibits slight
variations for the given balancing capacitance of 200 µF, depending on the specific case.
Variations between cases are primarily attributed to mismatches in the PCB routing paths
of the balancing capacitors and supply lines, as well as to inconsistencies introduced by
measurement cables and current probes.

   Balancing current waveforms are presented in Fig. 9.16 and Fig. 9.17 for switching
frequencies ranging from 4 kHz to 64 kHz. In Fig. 9.16, the bottom-most supply, B4 , is
set to 4.2 V while the remaining supplies are fixed at 3.3 V. As a result, B4 delivers a cur-
rent IB4 that increases with switching frequency, becoming predominantly unidirectional
from approximately 8 kHz upward. Similarly, when B3 is set to 4.3 V, the current IB3
transitions from a high-ripple waveform to one with a larger average value above 8 kHz,
approaching an optimal current distribution among all supplies at 32 kHz. This indicates
that the proposed balancer achieves its highest balancing speed near this frequency.

   At lower switching frequencies (below ∼8 kHz), the system operates in the slow-
switching limit (SSL), where the equivalent impedance is dominated by the balancing
capacitors. As the frequency increases, the operation shifts to the fast-switching limit
(FSL), in which the entire switching cycle is utilized for charging and discharging the
balancing capacitors. In this regime, energy transfer between higher-voltage and lower-
voltage cells is maximized, accelerating the balancing process. The balancing current in
the FSL is primarily limited by the on-resistance of the switches, PCB routing, and other
parasitic impedances in the current path. At even higher frequencies, further increases in
current are constrained by the self-resonance of the LC network formed by the total stray
inductance and the effective balancing capacitance.

   The measured current waveforms exhibit some expected deviations from the analyti-
cal predictions and parasitic-free simulation results. Ideally, they should follow a similar


                                            306
Figure 9.18: Measured average and RMS currents for all cells for the full-bridge capacitor-
             balancer


trend of the charging and discharging behaviour of IB1 -IB4 shown in Fig. 9.4. The ob-
served discrepancies in Fig. 9.16 and Fig. 9.17 are primarily due to the effects of approx-
imately 140 µF of decoupling capacitance and about 1 µH of cable inductance. These
parasitic components form an LC network with a resonance frequency of roughly 13 kHz,
resulting in waveform distortion toward a sinusoidal shape near resonance. Functionally,
the LC network behaves as an integrator, such that with increasing switching frequency,
the measured currents become progressively smoother and converge toward a quasi-DC
profile, as observed for frequencies exceeding 32 kHz.

   The average and RMS balancing currents were measured over the switching-
frequency range of 4 kHz to 128 kHz, as shown in Fig. 9.18. The results indicate a high
ratio of average to RMS balancing current, particularly for frequencies above 20 kHz,
where the ratio approaches unity for all battery cells. This characteristic enables faster
balancing with reduced self-heating and lower conduction losses, facilitated by the con-
tinuous current delivery inherent to the complementary operation of the stacked half-
bridge sub-modules. In contrast, Fig. 9.19 presents the average and RMS currents for
a stacked half-bridge circuit (representing one half of the proposed topology). Over-
all, the average balancing currents are approximately half as large for all cells, and the
terminal cells (B1 and B4) achieve a similar DC-to-RMS current ratio only at signifi-
cantly higher frequencies—approximately four times those required in the complemen-
tary stacked half-bridge case.

   Figure 9.20 presents the measured efficiency of the balancing chip as a function of

To characterize the circuit’s efficiency, one voltage supply is set to the maximum cell
voltage of 4.2 V, while the remaining supplies are set to 3.3 V. Efficiency is evaluated
under this voltage differential and defined as the ratio of output power, i.e., the power
transferred to the lower-voltage cells, to the input power drawn from the higher-voltage
cell.

    Finally, to validate the circuit’s capability of balancing various SOC difference sce-
narios, Keithley 2281S-20-6 devices were used to emulate Li-ion cells with capacities of
25 mAh, thereby accelerating SOC measurement recording. The results confirm the cir-
cuit’s ability to achieve equalized SOCs, with all cases equalizing within a narrow time
window, demonstrating the balancer’s high symmetry and cell-location-independent per-
formance. Minor deviations are attributed to resistance mismatches in PCB traces, wire
bonds, and other test-bench elements.



9.4       Comparison with Other Battery Balancers
Table 9.3 presents a comparison between the proposed integrated battery balancer and
other related balancing systems. The proposed design is particularly well-suited for ap-
plications involving a large number of battery cells, where implementation costs typi-
cally escalate due to the extensive use of switches, gate drivers, and complex balancing
algorithms. The proposed design is compact, modular, and supports high-performance
switches, enabling high switching frequencies with minimal switching losses. Other ex-
isting solutions often rely on discrete implementations that incorporate bulky and costly
gate driving components based on transformers and opto-couplers as described in Sec-
tion I.

    An integrated solution proposed in [142] utilized 50-V high-voltage PMOS and
NMOS switches but is limited to a low balancing current of 100 mA. While this approach
may be suitable for series-connected batteries with mild imbalance, caused primarily by
intrinsic cell variations, it is inadequate for addressing more severe imbalance scenarios.
Such scenarios are typical in multilevel converters, where selective cell usage for output
voltage synthesis results in significant operational imbalances. These applications de-
mand high-speed balancing circuits capable of actively transferring energy between cells
to maintain system stability and performance.
